Th ? publ j c ®y e rejects as uninteresting more
, as \ a 0 w hat appears in the newspapers of
eday. It i S only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
ol general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and nas become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
y the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s ■ conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
I j s ., ]lnes - Tbe result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
Prom the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. 1 hey are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
L .i e appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
u-rT Panted with new type of improved legi-
i . ° n r the ? ne , sfc P a P er > an d adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
trahan journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception,
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery tfhich now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people aver whom it is exercised. The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to .the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most’
popular city publication and .the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward 'ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Octopus Fate’s Defeat.
(FOR THE BULLETIN).
The drainage-shaft, owned by one Cassius, a Pole,
known as well in palmy Maoriland coastal history
as Ooolgardie Bayley is throughout Australia just
now, had ceased work. The diver was engaged in
taking the mighty columns of pumps out of the
mine. He had secured them all to a depth of
200ft., and he had had his diving head-gear
screwed on with the cast-iron resolve that this was
to be his last day at the dangerous work.
It was his last day at that employment. Slowly
the capstan spun round. Steadily the requisite
air was pumped down. When the diver got to his
depth, he rested his foot upon the summit of one of
the columns and felt about. All was dark. Slowly
he reached his arms around, but he could feel no
sides anywhere. He swung across to a second
stand with a like result. The shaft had, perhaps,
caved and twisted during the night.
Suddenly there was a rushing, swishing noise
in his ears. The accursed trap was “running”
somewhere far above his head. He snatched his
rope, and jerked the signal to pull up. The man
at the top recognised danger in the nervous
twitching, and yelled to those at the capstan.
Twenty brawny arms bent to the shafts, and the
rope tightened.
But it was fast. It wouldn’t heave. Then the
signal was repeated. Again they hove with all
their might. At that moment the air-pipe burst.
The manager gave a mighty shout, and slowly
the men got lifting way. Then there was a wrench
and the strain was considerably lessened. The
manager descended the ladder to the tail-race
level—60ft. below the surface —and waited.
The embrace of a vacuum is fearful. The diver
realised by the million sharp steel threads that cut
into his bones that the air was cut off. He tugged
madly at the knocker, and for answer he remained
stationary. In numbers, or in detail, death is a
lonely sort of shuffle.
The manager saw first a section of timber and
lathing, twisted and dove-tailed, that had been
torn from the shaft and in falling became entangled
on the rope. It formed a cone. Under that cone
appeared the diver. The manager saw through
the glass a face, black as the ace of spades. With
a blow of his fist, he smashed the plate.
An hour after, and the diver was conscious. He
must have been hauled through a perfect cataract
of timber and stones. His copper head-gear was
terribly dented. His hands were bleeding, and
he bears a soar, made by a piece of glass, under
his eye. But his wife prefers his life and his
deformity to his death, for the home is a happy
one.
The business of a diver is uncanny.
Polype.
Dubbo (N.S. W.) has 32 pubs.
Stead is almost certain to come here this
year.
Two years ago a Maoriland man received a
legacy of .£10,000. He promptly gave up work,
and in the course of 19 months of consistent drink-
ing and riot he disposed of all his fortune, and
then he returned quite cheerfully to the hard,
unremunerative occupation of gum-digging. He
never expressed any regret for his vanished pile—
in fact he seemed rather glad that it was gone.
A few weeks ago, however, another relative died
and left him £8000 more, and then for the first
time the gum-digger gave way to emotion and
cursed comprehensively, “ It’s just like my
luck,” he said, indignantly ; “ it’ll take me fifteen
months to drink that lot. It’s enough to make
a fellow wish he’d never been born,”
Oolemane & Sons’ Eucal. Loz. are for the voice
During February Chief-Justice Coleridge
found the name of the Rev. Coney among the
J.’sP. named for a certain English Grand Jury,
but,directed that he should not be “it
was better that gentlemen in holy orders should
not take part in criminal investigations.” A
wholesome generalisation—for parsons, when an
alleged offender has to be tried, are usually the
most prejudiced and cruel of judges. Aproj oi,
the wife of Green, condemned to death, and
whose affection for her husband caused such a pain-
ful scene in Court when he was sentenced, in-
terviewed nearly all the great people in Melbourne,
asking them to assist her to save him. She
was fairly received, except by the unco guid.
The only insult she received was from a
suburban clergyman. When she called, the
man of God told the unfortunate woman that he
hoped her husband would be hanged, and was
sorry that his wife would not be hanged with him.
The Gospel of Love—pooh !
All the rage in Paris. Send 13 penny
stamps for latest Parisian preventative. National
Drug Co., Russell-st., Melbourne.
The Lucifer-like fate of barmaids was the
theme of a paper read before N.S.W. Women’s
Christian Temperance Union Convention, held in
Sydney last week. The essayist was a lady bear-
ing the euphonious name of Hogg—Miss Hogg.
The barmaid, as Hogg-limned, is a diaphanous
maiden who becomes swiftly opaque with evil,
corrodes with vice, and dies in a lazarette. She is
a bud that blossoms gloriously but briefly in the
forcing pothouse of vice, then grows rank and
poisonous, is abandoned as a weed and latterly
infests only dunghills. Innocence, captivated by
gold, becomes the, glory of gold-strewn sensuous-
ness ; beauty, ravished of its dower, faces the bilk
of fortune: from private saloon, where she held
imperial sway, the decayed barmaid descends like
dissolving flakes of tarnished glory from hotel to
inn, from inn to pub., from pub. to beer-ken, from
beer-ken to brothel, from brothel to gutter-ways,
from gutter-ways to the Chinese opium-den,
which even the Social Gimletesses recognise
as the deepest deep of all. It is curious that the
barmaid doesn’t strike the average Australian
bibber in these highly fanciful lights and shades.
She is usually a creature extremely well able to
take care of herself, and wherever you find a
respectable hotel you find a respectable bar-
maid. It may be that the soreness felt by Social
Gimleteers is due to the fact that their noble lords
are the most numerous victims to the charms of
the few harpies there are amongst the many bar-
maids. If barmaids were as hurtful in their voca-
tion as Social Gimleteers are in theirs —there would
need be for a universal prayer, of “ The Lord save
creation!” But the average barmaid is neither
better nor worse than the average woman—whether
she be waitress on man or waitress on Providence.
And there remains this telling fact —barmaids
very often marry well. Ex-barmaids, good and
charming women, are to be found amongst the
wives of Australian topsawyers in every province
— judges, barristers, politicians and ennobbled
chanters of the National Anathema.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.

“ Advance Australia.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
We may shout “Advance Australia” till our
throats are dry as tan,
We may talk about our freedom and the “ Brother-
hood of Man,”
But the same old awe for title does our nation still
evince,
She will truckle to a “ Noble,” 'she will grovel to
a Prince,
And although we boast of equal rights, the highest
with the low,
We’re the same old race of “toadies” that we
were ten years ago.
We have leaders who will tell us that our land is
blest by Heaven ;
That a glorious independence to each Cornstalk
heart is given ;
That his heritage is Freedom, and Equality his
right,
That man is but his fellow, be he prince, or lord,
or knight;
But the men who preached the doctrine are the
men who bent the knee,
They have been successful “ toadies ” and are now
K.’sC.M.G.
When our six-foot-three Republican went ’Ome,
some time ago,
Oh ! his heart was all his country’s, as he’d have
his country know ;
And his foot was on oppression : aristocracy was
wrong,
’Twas the People that he worked for, and he
scorned the titled throng ;
But he saw a glittering bauble, and he licked his
sov’reign’s hand,
And got knighted for his loyalty—to Greatness,
not his Land !
And we’ll never be a nation till we stand alone
and free,
Our noblest title Manhood, and our prize
Equality—
And till Worth exults o’er sounding name, our
chronicles will show,
We’re the same old race of “ toadies ” that we
were ten years ago.
T. H. WILSON.
One of the troublesome spots of New
Guinea is Rossel Island, where the cannibal is
still in full blast. A few years ago a shipload of
Chinamen were wrecked there, and more than 200
got ashore safely. That was the grandest experi-
ence that ever befel the Rossel Islanders. The
Chinamen were shut up in pens—a dozen or two
in each pen—and everybody who wanted meat
picked out the fattest Chow he could find, and led
him away by the pigtail to the pot. The supply
lasted several weeks, and that was the last really
satisfying orgy that the islanders had. Things
are woefully changed now. The other day a dozen
strangers were eaten there, and Governor
M‘Gregor came along and proceeded to hang the
revellers straight away.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
A wandering Maorilander, just returned
from the U.S., writes to the Southlander about
his experiences in the prohibition town of Port-
land, in the good, old prohibition State of Maine:
Portland is a dreary, dirty place, the dullest and most
drunken town in the least progressive State in the Union.
You are no sooner landed from the boat than you are
shadowed by drink-touts, who haunt the wharves, public
places, even the barbers’-shops, billiard and oyster
saloons, advertising the places where you can get poisoned
by a villainous compound they call Bourbon whisky. Our
Bob had letters of introduction to a teetotal family in
this prohibition town, and was received very kindly.
Daddy took him into his study for a yarn, and
produced the black bottle, with a wink—just an
appetiser, you know. After dinner Tom, the eldest son,
took him to his boatshed, and from the locker produced
the brandy and curacoa to settle tbe dinner. Mamma,
when coffee-time came, whispered “it’s served d la
Francaise." And, to crown all, Bob, who was pining for
a glass of beer, and hated spirits like poison, after a bout
of tennis with the girls, was fixed up by them with
another teetotal drink that he swears to his dying day
was nothing more or less than brandy and soda. If you
want to study hypocrisy and prohibition go to the tee-
total state of Maine. Bill Nye had a big spree
given him before leaving home. It came off at
the farm about 12 miles from town. The boys put the
horses in and loaded up brandy labelled vinegar ; whisky
was orange bitters, and beer was ginger pop. The girls
and boys danced and had a high old time of it, and the
old men boozed till further orders, elders, deacons,
churchwardens. They never left the farm while there
was a shot in the locker. Old Deacon Cheyne told Bill
the day he left that the prohibition state of Maine was an
object lesson for all creation.

SATURDAY, APRIL 21, 1894.
The Purity of Justice.
\ short time ago a question was asked in
New South Wales Assembly as to an assign-
ment said to have been made by one of the
Supreme Court Judges for the benefit of his
creditors, and Premier Dibbs replied that
the Judge indicated had assured him that he
“had no liabilities whatsoever in New South
Wales.” Then, a similar question having
been put concerning another Judge, Mr.
Dibbs returned a supercilious and hazy reply
to the effect that, in his (Dibbs's) opinion,
no Judge “ would fill the high and respon-
sible office which he holds in a manner
derogatory to the position.” The question
was evidently intended to imply that an im-
pecunious Judge, whose creditors may come
before him any day as parties in a suit, is a
menace to the purity of justice. And the
answer was evidently intended to convey
that justice, in an English-speaking com-
munity, and under the British flag, is
naturally pure, and that it is unnecessary to
lake common, degrading precautions to pre-
vent it going askew. The House in general
took no notice of either question or answer.
It was evidently quite willing to take the
purity for granted, and in this respect its
attitude is that of the community in general.
# * #
It is, however, well worth while to analyse
this fetish of judicial purity. To begin
with—the lowest, cheapest, meanest form of
justice is that which is administered by the
common or garden J.P. This individual
doesn’t necessarily know any law. He is
often a mere item in a very small community
where almost everybody is related to every-
body else by birth or marriage or business
connections. He is part of its general
relationship ; he leans up against the same
public-house bar with it ; he buys from it,
sells to it, drinks, dines, gambles, and loafs
along with it, borrows money from it, tells
obscene stories to it ; and lives with it
generally. When anyone outside his own
circle appeals for justice against anyone
inside the circle he naturally doesn’t get that
article if the J.P. can help it, and when any-
one inside the circle wants the law’s assist-
ance to perpetrate an injustice upon anyone
outside he naturally does get it, if it can be
in imaged by his Worship. The J.P. who
dispensed even-handed justice would often
have to fine or gaol a good customer and
thereby lose his business and that of ail his
relatives, friends, and dependents. He
would have to give decisions against creditors
or the friends or dependents of creditors, who
might sell him up the next day in retalia-
tion, or to severely admonish other people
from whom he hoped to get financial assist-
ance. And he is not paid one solitary copper
for doing these things. He is expected to
wreck his own business arrangements gratis
—for the mere abstract love of justice.
And naturally, being only human, he does
nothing of the sort. In the country districts
of Australia nearly every affluent white male
is a J.P., and nearly every other white male
is certain to be one if he only becomes
affluent. Therefore, if justice is pure, the
inevitable inference is that almost every
male citizen is an absolutely spotless
character and fit to be a martyr at the
shortest notice, and if such were the case
there would be no need of J’s.P., magistrates,
judges, policemen, hangmen, or law in any
form. As the inference is obviously absurd,
the natural assumption is that justice-is just
about as unclean as anything else in a com-
munity which is no better than the average.
* » «
The situation of the stipendiary “ beak ”
is too often not widely different from that
of the J.P. He is sent out to some country
township while still comparatively young,
and is left to vegetate there for 20 or 30 or
40 years. He marries and becomes some-
body’s son-in-law, and by his marriage he
gets a lot of brothers and sisters-in-law, and
acquires an interest in their husbands and
wives and the relatives of their husbands
and wives. He becomes a component part
a narrow clique, and, in the long run, becomes
somebody’s father-in-law. He dines and
drinks with suitors, and plays billiards with
them, and has orgies with them and the local
doctor and the police - inspector in the
principal public-house at forbidden hours.
Also, he borrows moneyfrom them. For some
unexplained reason the elderly country
magistrate is often by nature a miserably-
embarrassed being, and the leading local
attorney is one of his principal pals and
creditors. Therefore, whenever it can bo
managed without an altogether too glaring
perversion of justice, the said attorney’s
client wins. Also, the statutes are often
strained in favour of the magistrate s con-
nections, and the law winks at the midnight
orgies in the magistrate’s favourite public-
house. The miserable stipendiary dare not
send one of the local magnates to gaol for
anything short of murder, else he will be an
outcast fro n local society. And if he dis-
penses even-handed justice he becomes
an elderly, red-nosed pariah, and is
left to drink by himself, and pres-
sure is brought to bear upon the local
member to make him bring pressure
on the Government to have the obnoxious
magistrate removed to some smaller and
m re remote and still drearier town. The
average magistrate has no special incentives
to judicial honesty. He seldom has any
prospect of becoming anything else than a
magistrate. His salary goes on in any case.
If he is promoted it is generally through
political influence, and the best way to
obtain political influence is to keep on good
terms with local society, which is probably
all more or less related to the local member,
and to decide in its favour on every possible
occasion.
# !» *
As for the Judges of the higher courts, it
is the favourite assumption that their inte-
grity is spotless and their fairness absolutely
beyond question. The Bulletin regards
the vast majority of the Australian Supreme
Court Judges, past and present, as men of
spotless integrity of purpose, but the as-
sumption that they are, and must neces-
sarily be, altogether proof against the
influences which consciously and uncon-
sciously ' sway humanity is merely part
of the good old the tv that “ British ’
justice always is pure. The higher Judge,
in fact, is merely an average human being—
made a little better by letral training and
political experience, and partially restrained
by considerations of self-interest, and to
some extent put beyond temptation by the
possession of an office of high trust
and constant association with men of
the high character which, we are proud
to say, ordinarily distinguishes the
Supreme Court Bench. But there is no
reason to suppose that, when distracted
by financial troubles, he is one whit
more inflexible than any other hard-
pressed man who means well in the
main. One Australian inferior-court Judge
has left behind him a malodorous re-
cord as a man who borrowed money from
usurers who were constant suitors in his
court, and who was otherwise celebrated
chiefly because the fact that those usurers
had lent him money was frequently visible
in his judicial decisions. The Bill recently
passed in Queensland, forbidding any barris-
ter to plead before a Judge who is his father
or near relative, is—and was evidently in-
tended to be—a grim reflection upon the
bench. And the perpetual appearance of
certain Australian barristers in the court
where their relatives preside, coupled
with the fact that they very seldom appear
anywhere else, is evidently intended—by the
attorneys who employ them and who prob-
ably judge others by their , own standard—
to be another reflection upon judicial weak-
ness. Also, the same may be said of the
proposed new law in Maoriland, whereby the
Judges are to take all the circuits in rotation,
so that no one of them will do the same
circuit oftener than once in two years or so
—the openly-avowed purpose being to keep
him free from the local influencers which
gather round a. man who moves perpetually
iu one narrow circle.
* # *
To make Justice as pure as possible,
many changes are wanted. The unpaid J.P.
should be entirely abolished because he is
ignorant, untrained, and too often utterly
and incurably corrupt. The paid magistrates
should be moved from one station to another
every two or three years, and should never
serve two terms in the one town, in order to
prevent them becoming altogether the slaves
of personal and family interests. No Judge
or magistrate should be allowed to become
financially embarrassed, for no insolvent
Judge is I oyond temptation. He should
be absolutely disqualified from holding
shares or being a partner in any undertaking,
and any shaVe, transfer, or deed of partner-
ship with his name upon it should bo abso-
lutely null and void. His bill, note, bond,
or security of any kind should be made
waste-paper by Act of Parliament, and his
debts should be irrecoverable. He should
take oath that he does not, and will not,
belong to any secret society whatsoever. He
should be highly paid, and liberally pen-
sioned on retirement, and his investments
should be practically restricted to Govern-
ment bonds, as almost the only form of
investment which cannot lead him into
temptation. He should be shut out, in
short, by every possible means from every
possible kind of influence, financial, social,
political, or otherwise. But to bring about
this result it is necessary, in the first
place, to realise that justice is not
necessarily pure, else no steps will be taken
to purify it. Premier Dibbs’ cheerful, bull
headed assumption that everything must be
correct as a matter of course represents the
State’s customary attitude iu the matter, and
it is mainly because of this attitude that ugly
things pass almost unnoticed.
The Contingent Vote.
The “secondary” or “contingent” vote is a
vote cast by an elector in addition to his first
or “primary” vote. The number of contingent
votes may be arbitrarily limited, or limited
only by the number of candidates for whom an
elector is entitled to vote. The vote is called
“contingent,” because it is only employed con-
tingently on the first vote becoming inoperative.
At the coming New South Wales election, for
example, the majority of votes cast will pro-
bably be inoperative ; that is, a majority of
electors who vote will be unrepresented in
Parliament, and the representatives of the
minority will rule the province.
# * #
Under a democratic constitution, it is unjust
that either a minority or majority of electors
should be unrepresented in the Government.
Contingent voting may be used to secure either
(1) the rule of the majority, or (2) the propor-
tional representation of both majority and
minority. In a simple form of the first case it
could be easily applied in New South Wales.
Under the existing law, in any constituency
where there are more than two candidates, the
chances are that the successful candidate will
represent a minority of the electors who vote.
Were an absolute majority of votes essential to
election, contingent voting seems the best
means of ascertaining the choice of the con-
stituency.
» # •
An alternative system is the second ballot,
which is a second poll taken between a limited
number of candidates who are highest at the
first poll. This system was contemplated by
an amending electoral bill introduced to the
Queensland Parliament in ’9l, but was aban-
doned for the contingent voting Act passed in
’92. The objections to the second ballot are
that (1) a minority of electors are still dis-
franchised ; (2) it is possible that the final
choice does not represent electors’ wishes ; (3)
there is great additional expense, delay, and
dissatisfaction involved in the second poll,
o # *
Contingent voting is based on a system of
preferences expressed by an elector on his
ballot-paper. The simplest way of expressing
preference is to write the figures 1,2, 3, and so
on, opposite candidates’ names, thus ;
4. Brown.
2. Jonhs.
1. Smith.
3. Robinson,
and so on, to any number of candidates. This
voter will be understood to reason : “I wish
Smith to be elected, and so I give him my first
vote. If, however, Smith has not a sufficient
number of supporters to elect him, I would
sooner have Jones than Brown or Robinson.
If 1 can’t have Jones, I prefer Robinson to
Brown.”
» # •
There are several ways of counting con-
tingent votes. Perhaps the most satisfactory
is to give a qualitative value to each, consider-
ing two second votes, or three third votes, or
four fourth votes, equal to one first vote.
Thus, in the above case, supposing 1000 electors
voted, the poll might show :
(The inequality of totals would result from the
probable failure of electors to record some or all
of their contingent votes.)
* * •
Here, under the present plan, Brown is
elected as the representative of a minority, the
majority of voters remaining unrepresented.
Contingent voting on the plan recommended
would count second voles at half the value of
first votes, making—
-Ist. Vote. 2n 1 Vote. Total.
Brown 400 100 500
Joxss 300 300 600
Smith 200 50 250
Robinson 100 25 125
Jones would then be elected by an absolute
majority of votes. (If any candidate failed to
get an absolute majority of votes on the second
count, third votes would be included at pro-
portional value, then fourth votes, and so on.)
« « *
Contingent voting on a system similar to, but
not the same as this, took place at the Queens-
land election last year. The Queensland method
is, however, vitiated by the existence of double
electorates; by the arbitrary exclusion of the
contingent vote given to certain candidates ; by
considering first and contingent votes of equal
value ; by sometimes preferring the contingent
votes of one candidate to the first votes of
another candidate, and vice versa— causing com-
plication and injustice, and defeating the
expressed purpose of the Act.
* * *
Contingent voting on a wider basis, providing
for the proportional representation of all classes
of electors, is hardly compatible with the
existence of local constituencies. If prac-
ticable, the best scheme is too novel to be
popular, and too complicated to be successful.
It could, however, be applied with ease and
advantage to the election of the New South
Wales Legislative Council on a new footing.
The most reasonable plan, that of Thomas
Hare, was actually recommended by Went-
worth during a discussion on the Constitution
of the Council in 1802, and a Bill embodying it
had passed its second reading when the
Government resigned, and its successors estab-
lished the House on another plan.
# # «
Under the proposed system any number of
candidates would offer themselves for a definite
number of seats in the Legislative Council, and
the whole body of electors would vote for the
whole number of candidates at the same time,
using contingent voles as illustrated above.
The total number of votes polled would be
divided by the number of vacant seats, and the
quotient would form a “quota” necessary
to secure the election of any candidate. Those
who obtained this quota on the first vote would
be elected at once, and their surplus of votes
would go as contingent votes to increase the
votes of candidates beneath them on the list,
and so on until all the seats were filled. In the
event of the surplus votes of all elected candi-
dates being distributed, and vacancies still
remaining to be tilled by candidates who all fall
short of the quota, the secondary votes would
be counted on a qualitative valuation, and
so on.
# # *
This seems complicated ; but, once under-
stood, is very simple. Suppose there were 50
seats to fill, with 200 candidates, and 100,000
voters. Then the quota would be 100,000
divided by 50, equal to 2000. Thirty candidates
might get 2000 and over on the first vote.
These would be elected ; the ballot-papers of
those in excess of 2000 who had voted for them
would be examined, and the second votes
thereon distributed as first votes to the remain-
ing candidates. Ten more candidates might
then obtain their quota, and have their surplus
votes distributed. Then a count of all the
second votes of hitherto defeated candidates
might become necessary, followed by qualita-
tive valuation, distribution, and selection of the
remaining ten candidates from the highest on
the list of those who then obtained the quota.
* i *
This scheme provides the most perfect repre-
sentation of all classes of electors that has so
far been devised, by enabling scattered electors
throughout the country to break the trammels
of limited constituencies and unite their votes
for the return of chosen candidates. It would
probably issue in the election of the best men
in the country, now deterred from seeking seats
in the popular Assembly by the preponderance
of local interests ; and the result, at all events,
could not fail to be productive of better things
than the present system of glorification of
wealth, political friendship, and political in-
fluence.
Darkest Womanhood, and the Way Out.
Dear Bulletin, —The other night, on my way
to Princes’ Bridge Station, I passed the
building with the saffron blinds—the Melb.
W.C.T.U. In front, on the side-walk, a group
of women were haggling. Ugh, such a group 1
Each was grasping a roll of yellowish paper —
probably arguments against “ the drink.” Not
one of them had any pretensions to good looks.
And look at the W.C.T.U. portraits which
have been published. What a pitiful crew !
I know that engravings are usually a severe
trial to anybody’s looks, but if the artist
had done these heroines anything like justice
what a libel they would bo upon rosy, full-
lipped womanhood ! 1 have seen them in the
flesh, or bones. Miss Aokermann is about the
only one of the crowd who looks as if she had
any sap in her, and her conspicuous physical
superiority has created such jealousy among the
hags that she had to stand up, the other
day, and publicly deny a rumour that she
is going away to be married. I was sorry,
for a good husband is precisely the thing which
would make Miss Ackermann complete.
* • #
But nobody ever dreams of rumouring limb
Miss Blank or Miss So-and-so is going to be
married. Good Lord, no ! Men instinctively
fight shy of them, and the reason is that sexual
attraction in their case becomes repulsion.
Nominally they are females ; but actually and
physiologically they are aborted males. You
cannot be in their company without noticing
the signs—the strong muscular system, the
prominent bones, the harsh voice, the dim
beards and unsuccessful moustaches, the thin
hair, and the general predominance of katabolic
tendencies. The males who congregate with
them are generally similar examples of sex-
perversion—nervous, excitable creatures with
small frames, no muscle, delicate skin,
and an irresistible itching for long hair


an d tea. They are scorned by vital
women just as their counterparts are
shunned by vigorous men. Luckily Nature
has made a freak to fit a freak, and provides
that the negatives of humanity shall mutually
attract as well as the positives. And the reason
why the he-female and the she-male pride
themselves on monopolising virtue is because
nothing else is left to them.
* # »
Hear my aphorism : It is well to have virtue
—when one has neither beauty nor wit. The
more you study this the more you will see in it.
Do you think for a moment that there is any-
thing but sex-jealousy at the bottom of the
agitation against barmaids ? Do you think it is
really sympathy for the poor unfortunates in the
bars which impels sister after sister to rise and
shriek for their forcible removal ? Nothing of
the kind ; the impulse is an instinct, the most
natural of all instincts, the instinct of self-
preservation. Under existing social conditions,
when there are not enough eligible men to go
round, and some women must draw blanks, all
are engaged in a fierce competition for the
prizes. Each displays the wares she has in the
hope of attracting a customer. The good-
looking ones take two hours to djress ;
the clever ones get a University degree ;
but for those, alas ! who are not good-looking
and not clever there is nothing left but virtue
and the W.C.T. U. They don’t want the
W.C.T.U., they would shirk it if they possibly
could, but circumstances force them to embrace
it. “ Evil, be thou our good !” they sorrow-
fully cry.
# # *
It isn’t much good. Men are naturally so
constituted that virtue doesn’t attract them in
anything like the numbers which by rights
should rise to such a morally superior bait.
While warm blood pulsates in their arteries
(and even when it cools) they go for good looks.
Of course the natural man is right. Good looks
are generally a sign of the vitality necessary to
the perpetuation of a healthy race; virtue, by
itself, generally isn’t. Consequently, pitting
tiro barmaids against the W.C.T. U., the
average barmaid attracts fifty admirers to
every one who drifts up to sniff doubt-
fully at a W.C.T.U. I have figured the
thing out on the basis of women who want
to marry and men who don’t, and the barmaid
gets exactly 49 83416 more men than she has
any business to get. This is monstrous, and
the W.C.T.U. does well to be angry (see
Jonah, iv., 9). »
# # *
t Then, if you notice, all the leading
Women’s Franchise lights have either had
unlucky love - affairs, or made unhappy
marriages. I could name you two in Bris-
bane, three in Sydney, two in Melbourne,
and one in Adelaide, in the very front
rank of female liberators, who have all had
misfortunes with their men. You will never
find a woman who is really happy and busy at
home making a fool of herself on a platform.
The great majority of women need happy homes
much more than men do. They don’t want
votes, or total-abstinence tickets, or social
purity badges. What they do want is husbands
—good, upstanding husbands. They are yearn-
ing for husbands, pining for husbands, dying
for husbands —and children. They long to
nestle their little heads close into somebody’s
waistcoat. Give them husbands. How ? Easily.
Let the Y. W.C. A.’s,and W.C.T.U. ’s, and all the
other faddy associations, form themselves into
one grand Association for the Promotion of
# #
Oh ! and what of morality ? Mora-
lity be hanged! Excuse me, but the case
demands strong language. Is it morality to
make natural instincts criminal, to set law
above reason, and cramp humanity to the stan-
dards of Mrs. Grundy ? Walk down any street,
and see the scores of young women, shrivelled,
yellow, diseased through leading unnatural lives.
Ask any doctor to tell you how many die in
early womanhood from “ decline,” “consump-
tion,” “ lung complaint,” or a dozen other
ailments whose names euphemistically hide
their real cause. And monogamy is “moral,”
forsooth ! What is monogamy, after all, but
veiled polygamy, with hetairai for second
wives ? How many men are rigidly mono-
gamistic? Ten per cent? The unions, the
W.C.T.U., and their kindred run hither and
thither seeking palliatives for prostitution. Let
them take polygamy, and prevent it. Poly-
gamy is natural, polygamy is scriptural, poly-
gamy is holy; and I am, yours, Brigham
Brown, M.D., F.R.C.S.
Work versus Shirk.
The gist of New South Wales Premier Dibbs’
Bathurst speech was its endorsement of the
policy of work as opposed to the policy of
shirk. As yet, Australia has hardly com-
menced to tap the springs of wealth which on
every hand surround her. Where minerals lie
on or near the surface of the ground, we have
picked them up. Where cattle will breed we
let them breed. Where wool will grow on a
sheep’s back we let it grow. But where actual
labour of brain and muscle is involved we have
hitherto done pitifully little. Seven million
acres under crop make a poor figure beside
200 million pounds of debt. The fatal
facility with which we have been able
to borrow money has been the national
undoing. It has starved the country of
labourers to accumulate drones in the cities ; it
has turned our Governments from directors of
industry into employers of industry, demoralis-
ing individual character and destroying indi-
vidual energy and self-reliance ; it places daily
before us the contrast of untilled fields and idle
hands, of mouths empty while earth and air
and sea teem with food, of the greatest natural
resources in the world side by side with poverty
and destitution.
# # s
Australia has only too faithfully imitated
the carter in the fable, who fell on his
knees and prayed Jupiter to help him when
his cart stuck in a rut. We have prayed to the
English capitalist for all he is worth. O, that
Jupiter would insist on our own shoulder going
to the wheel ! Observer after observer has
come to these shores to be astonished at the
apathy exhibited in the face of such golden
opportunities. Miss Shaw told us that the
characteristic attitude of young Australia was
to lean against a post ; and it is true.
Professor Shelton told the Queenslanders
that if there was in America one-hundredth
part of the rich coast-land which lies idle be-
tween Cooktown and Buudaberg, the Americans
would never rest from advertising and hustling
until every inch was under cultivation ; and it
is true. Armour, the Chicago meat-exporter,
asked by a New South Wales squatter what he
would do with bullocks walking about worth
50s. here and £25 in England, said he guessed
he’d do something, for such a state of things
was a slur on our progressiveness ; and it is
true.
* # *
Australia has long enough sat still and waited
for something to turn up : it is time she went
ahead and began to turn up something. Aus-
tralian Governments have been long enough in
the habit of feeding the people with a spoon ;
it is time they were turned adrift to
feed themselves. Premier Dibbs outlined
a policy of decentralisation as the policy
for New South Wales to follow. The
sooner the better. The Queensland Divi-
sional Boards Act has atoned by its suc-
cess for many of MTlwraitii’s blunders.
Anything which makes patronage and plunder
cease to be regarded as political perquisites ;
which sets the people on their own feet, and gives
them to understand that God and the Govern-
ment help only those who help themselves;
anything which strives to level class distinc-
tions, to put poor and rich on a footing of equal
opportunity, to give every man a fair chance,
and none more than a fair chance ; will be a
blessing in this world, and the next, and the
next after that. The eight-hour day is good, if
a man has to work for an employer ; but the
fewer employers Australia has the better, and
when a man works for himself he doesn’t count
his hours. There is plenty of grit in the com-
munity ; and when it is driven to the wall it
will put back its ears and fight. It is getting
pretty close to the wall now. The more need
for Dibbs and others to preach down the old
gospel, the gospel of shirk, and preach up the
new gospel, the gospel of work. /
Hopetoun and his Private Income.
Governor Hopetoun has contrived to drag
into a country banquet speech the statement
that his annual Victorian expenditure exceeds
the £lO,OOO and extra-allowance moneys pro-
vided by an obsequious Government. Lord
Hopetoun, with delicate Scotch humour,
describes himself as a “remittance man,” com-
pelled to send “ home ” for cash from “ his own
income” in order to “ keep up the position of
Governor thoroughly.” He somewhat doubts
the propriety of continuing this drain upon the
resources of Great Britain. Ho has to study
the feelings of his tenants, who, “though for
some time they may not object to money col-
lected from them coming out here, at the end
of that time may perhaps ‘ kick ’ at the money
going out of the country.” Brassy self-lauda-
tion is the rule at official gobbles, and the bush-
town banquet has ever been a favourable
opportunity for the diffusion of inflated non-
sense per medium of newspaper reports, but
probably Lord Hopetoun’s financial revelations
in the Mallee district deserve the palm for
absurdity.
# * *
In the first place, this non-productive noble-
man presumes to consider that Victoria has left
the discovery of a “ thorough ” maintenance
ofthe gubernatorial position to Hopetoun,
whereas he is actually, and even from the
“loyal” point of view, merely an imported
figurehead supplied with a certain fixed quan-
tity of gilt for gilding purposes. He is expected
to live comfortably en famille, and devote the
balance of his subsidy to the least objectionable,
or most worthy, matters that come within his
range. The quantity of dry champagne, the
number of expensive horses, the items of super-
fluous luxury that he may choose to order from
Europe are nothing to the people who provide
his Victorian allowance. The position of
Governor has a money limitation, and a very
liberal limitation it is. Nine-tenths of the
population would select a Governor (until such
time as they can abolish the office) who did his
little bit of official duty thoroughly, and saved
money from his official salary like any other
prudent occupant of a good billet, in preference
to the young lordling who draws upon a truly
scandalous source of unearned increment at
“Home.”
# # #
Admitting that the snobocracy prefers a
Hopetoun, it is certain that a Rothschild
would be even more acceptable. The Roths-
child figurehead might vote himself an extra
£200,000 a year from his privy purse in order
to keep up the position of Governor
“ thoroughly,” and yet what that plutocrat did
outside of his salary limit would be no more
irrelevant to the point than is the thing which
Hopetoun does. As regards these remit-
tances extracted from the Scotch earl’s “own
income ” —-what do they represent ? The fruits
of other people’s industry to which he has no
tittle of moral claim. The outcome of shame-
ful land laws by means of which a tribe of in ■
fluential drones maintain their social and poli-
tical ascendancy over mankind at large. Lord
Hopetoun’s first and foremost occupation in
life is the spending of riches that ought not to
be his. So much undeserved money is placed at
his disposal in Scotland every year, and what
he doesn’t scatter he heaps up to the family
glory. For some time past the growth of his
pile has been accelerated by the good people of
Victoria who give him what is tantamount to
£20,000, or more, a year for consenting to live
in their midst.
» # •
But because the lord has chosen to be not satis-
fied with living up to £20,000 a year, and has,
by some imperceptible means managed to ex-
ceed that sum, he has the puerile “ cheek” to
hint that his Scotch tenants —poor devils! —may
feel aggrieved at the spectacle of their callow
chieftain spending a few of his Scotch bawbees
in foreign parts. Observe the thin attempt to
represent peers as men responsible to their
tenants for all dealings with the rent-roll !
Whoever heard of a British farmer kicking on
account of the noble landlord spending half the
year on the Continent, and—Oh—is there any
case on record of a noble landlord caring
whether his vassal did so kick, or not? The
Morning Post is herewith commended to the
notice cf any up-country innocent on whom
Lord Hopetoun’s speech may have produced a
beautiful impression. In that flunkey journal
the reader may find abundant proof of bucolic
docility in the lists of landed proprietors who
are eternally leaving the country—let alone the
county —to spend money wrung from its soil.
Also, he may generally assume that the duke,
who departs on an unusually long tour, has
instructed his steward to miss no chance of
raising rents whilst the thoughtful landlord is
far away.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Political Deficits.
Inasmuch as the Victorian Government has
failed to adapt its expenditure to its income
during the current financial year, Melbourne
Age affects to believe that “the country is
still drifting to leeward at the rate of £ISOO a
day.” The country is warned of its imminent
peril and advised to cease drifting, as though
each individual inhabitant were privately
getting further and further into debt through
the agency of half-a-dozen feasting speech-
makers. This confusion of “ Crown ” and
country in financial matters is a popular press
fallacy, or trick—it is hard to say whether a
daily newspaper misrepresents a case with
malice aforethought, or from ignorance—and
thereby the public are taught to accept a partial
responsibility for the sins of their reckless ser-
vants. J. B. Patterson is the manager of a
unique kind of business, which may be styled
the State Taxation and Expenditure Co., un-
limited. He has full power, in his simple
business capacity, to prevent the faintest possi-
bility of a loss on his year’s transactions. He
could ensure a surplus in unprosperous times as
easily as he could run the co. into debt when
its income was exceptionally large. Very little
wisdom is needed to govern the world. The
smallest of nous would suffice to produce a
satisfactory State balance-sheet if the business
were conducted with that sole end in view.
Mean political considerations have governed
Patterson’s management of taxation and
expenditure, as they will always govern all
Governments founded on parliamentary cor-
ruption and “ party” principles. The moneyed
classes protested against a practical reduction
of the Civil Service, upon which their relatives
subsist, neither would they hear of a tax on
land or incomes sufficient to pay the salaries of
the swarm of State drones. Ideas of retrench-
ment in a number of dark corners were aban-
doned lest they might affect the prospects of
Patterson, and, as a last resource, the Govern-
ment added a special Customs duty in the blind
hope that it might work a miracle. The
miracle did not eventuate, and Patterson is
drifting to leeward at the rate of £ISOO a day.
Meanwhile, the country, meaning its working
population, practises economy. People are
living within their legitimate means, or
cautiously eating up their own capital, for the
reason that credit is strictly limited. There is
more real industry and far less extravagance in
Victoria to-day than at any time since Mel-
bourne began to fatten on foreign loans, and
although the country may not be steaming
ahead, it most certainly is not drifting the
wrong way. As for Patterson, he can be
safely left to work out his own destruc-
tion. If he can’t avoid a deficit under the
present easy conditions, when the wail
of the discharged barnacles would be drowned
by shouts of hungry derision from the masses—
then Patterson must go the way of the poli-
tical fraud who has been found out. Munro,
Shiels, and Patterson have each in turn
undertaken to tax Fat Man, or dispense with a
horde of his -proteges and other abuses, in order
to make Expenditure tally with Receipts.
Each of them has drifted further into debt
without in the least degree altering the state of
the country. The people have paid, as of yore,
a certain amount of money for the maintenance
of necessary and unnecessary departments, and
for the perpetration of scandalous jobs by
uncalled-for legislators, and the amount paid
has fallen short of the sum absorbed in
“ governing.” The balance is still owing, and
must be paid very shortly. This could be most
easily accomplished by electing a clean, new
Parliament, in which a new, clean Government
might exist without drifting. There is nothing
so conducive to official reform as a large deficit,
which the country is determined to wipe out.

Characters and Characters:
The libel-case of Hayes v. The Australasian
has enabled a few more people to understand
the rank injustice of barristerial procedure, and
the unfairness of law-reporting by “great”
journals. Hayes, the plaintiff, is a jockey—a
“ flash ” young jockey, with a taste for bursts
of debauchery, and none of the strength of
mind, or coolness of character, which preserves
the small minority of jockeys from evil com-
panionship. Inasmuch as he had dared to con-
tend that he was not the dishonest rider por-
trayed by a weekly newspaper, Hayes was
made the victim of a violent verbal assault from
the defendant’s counsel. In a fiery opening
speech the aforesaid counsel described Hayes
as a light-weight monster of bagnio
wickedness, a committer of horrible crimes
upon unfortunate women, and the jury’s
attention was specially drawn to the impru-
dence of such a creature in bringing an
action for defamation of character against a
virtuous newspaper proprietary. No evidence
of these horrible crimes was adduced, however.
Women who were to go into the box and reveal
mysterious deeds, did not fulfil the awful pro-
mises of the opening speech. In point of fact,
no female entered the witness-box at all,
although a barmaid’s name was mentioned (and
reported) in a singularly aimless manner.
Jockey Hayes revealed, without hesitation, the
fact that he had gone to “bad houses ” in the
company of men older than himself, but the
evidence of his “ vice ” went no further. All
that could be made of this improper behaviour
was made by a truculent cross-examining purist
in a horse-hair wig, and the newspapers backed
him up with a full report. Previous to the
full exposure of the ignorant jockey-boy’s mis-
doings, a witness of higher social standing had
given testimony in support of the Australasian
case. Hayes’s solicitors saw cause for seeking
to discredit the evidence of this important
allegator, to which end they briefed a story
of his earlier life a story which, if
true, told considerably against his sense
of honour, and which was therefore as much
entitled to a full hearing and full publi-
cation as was any narrative of the Jockey’s
viciousness. Yet no sooner had this particular
old scandal been broached by the jockey’s bar-
rister than the Australasian barrister begged
the Judge to forbid publication. He raved and
wept, did this bold traducer of Hayes, this
famous “ terror to decent witnesses,” merely
because a gentleman on the Austrai-asian side
was experiencing the same kind of treatment
that was administered to the jockey. Further-
more, it is remarkable that the jockey’s bar-
rister soon desisted from questioning the person
of higher standing, and almost apologised for
having commenced to make himself unplea-
sant. He said that his questions were “ in
his brief,” wherefore he had seen no harm
in putting them, but the opposing wig-
wearer snorted, “In your brie/!’’ with all the
indignation of a man who wouldn’t hurt a
gentleman’s feelings though a thousand briefs
had instructed him to do so. The incident then
dropped. After being dropped, it was prac-
tically wiped out of the case where, on the
other hand, the immorality of Hayes was writ
so large. The Judge, forsooth, could not order
the suppression of evidence unfavourable to the
Australasian’s chief witness, but the “great”
newspapers suppressed it on their own account.
The Bulletin withdraws the remark that this
incident was “ wiped out.” It was covered up
by general consent of barristers and Press out
of respect to the majesty of the Law, which is
no respecter of persons, or for some other highly
respectable reason.
The progress of the pistol is so rapid that in
about a year, at present prospects, every Sydney
man will have to carry a revolver in his hip-
pocket, and learn how to draw first bead in a
street encounter. “ The strongest constable in
the force,” one McQuaker, was on duty in
Cook Park at 11 o’clock la,st Saturday night
when he saw a “suspicious” nigger. Why he
was suspicious, or even why he was a nigger, is
not clear ; but McQuaker alleges that he asked
for information and received a butt in the
stomach from the nigger’s head, which effectu-
ally doubled up “ the strongest constable in the
force.” Then the nigger ran away, and the
constable recovered sufficiently to reach for his
revolver and fire thrice after him. He didn’t hit
the nigger, but he very nearly hit a woman, and
he might easily have hit any one of the large
number of persons who pass through Cook Park
on a Saturday night. It seems almost a pity
he didn’t “blood” his new revolver, which, in
the ordinary course of human nature,. every
constable must itch to do. As for the nigger,
and the woman—well, there are plenty of
women and niggers. Led by Dibbs (who, it is
publicly stated, also carries a revolver and
shows it off before admiring reporters) the
police might organise a little battue in the
parks on Saturday nights, being careful always
to ask a person if he was “ suspicious ” before
shooting him, and if not why not. As the
average park proinenader would probably
become indignant and refuse to answer, he
could be dropped off-hand. When the sus-
picious public take to carrying revolvers them-
selves, the fun will become fast and furious,
and the province will be happy and free and a
pleasant place to live in.
Constable Andrews mixed himself up in a
dispute between Mr. Garvan, M.P. (N.S. W.),
and his tenants, in such a fashion that the In-
spector-General of Police thought he should be
reprimanded. That was foolish. Premier
Dibbs mixed himself up in a dispute between
Mr. Garvan, M.P., and a policeman, and lent
himself to further his supporter’s vengeance by
adding to the punishment awarded by the
Inspector, and disrating and removing a
man for the first mistake made in 14 years,
during which he had borne an excellent charac-
ter. That was unjust. New South Wales
Assembly, overwhelmed with work, wasted a
whole evening in discussing this, because the
Opposition wished to make political capital out
of the matter, f That was both foolish and
unjust.
The Marquis of Ailesbury, turf blackguard
and “ Juggins,” has emulated the more amiable
Marquis of Hastings by dying of liquor and
late hours, in his stale young manhood. He
leaves a widow (Dolly Tester) of giddy repu-
tation, and a sister who is the wife of bookmaker
Sutton, to mourn his loss, whilst the Hebrew
money-lenders who hold his life - assurance
policies are probably drinking champagne in
honour of his early decease. The splendid
Ailesbury estate has already passed into the
hands of exalted brewers, or was lost to the
noble Marquis in a maze of mortgage, and as
he no longer owned a single shilling there was
nothing in particular left for him save death
and burial in the ancestral vault. This high-
born personage was a natural larrikin, accord-
ing to all accounts, an enthusiastic consort of
blacklegs, bullies, and inferior boozers. The
Jockey Club warned him off the turf, and even
middle-class tuft-hunters, possessing ideas as to
social decency, would have refused to open
their doors to the title borne by this rowdy
character. But the House of Peers, it is
interesting to note, was always open to the
Marquis of Ailesbury. The glorious British
Constitution recognised him as a pillar of the
State, and his power to obstruct the march of
progress with his hereditary vote was unim-
paired up to the day when dissipation carried
him off. Therefore, Rule Britannia, for Britons
never will be slaves !

know why the Board of Works borrowed this
£500,000. There is still on hand, it is said, a

A meeting of the Vic. Women’s Suffrage
League was held in Melbourne the other day
in the presence of about 40 “ ladies ” of assorted
ages and shapes. No men were there. The
majority of those present belonged to the
squatto-financial crowd, the remainder being
social-gimletists, suburban mayoresses, temper-
ance-faddists, Blavatskites, and a handful of
Austral-Salonites to give things tone ; but one
and all appeared to be well under the control of
a certain Tory pen-lady who “ ran ” the meet-
ing and maintained excellent discipline. A
well-nourished member made the hit of the day
by remarking that “ Our grooms and coachmen
and workmen to whom we give employment are
liable to make laws to govern us. The lower
orders can dominate those who own Property ”
—which was received with a snort of indigna-
tion. The remark, however, need hardly have
been made in the presence of the wife and
daughters of an ex-coachman who sat lower
down the room. The election of office-bearers
was proceeded with, Agar Wynne, the egg-
headed Postmaster-General, being chosen presi-
dent, on the ground that he was “very nice.”
The well-known opposition of a prominent
politician was overcome by a lady who under-
took to secure his adhesion before “ to-morrow
morning.” What means she intended to em-
ploy were not made public. The glorious
principle of the woman property-vote is to be
furthered by “drawing-room meetings” every
week, and Parliament is to be urged and
threatened to pass the bill immediately upon
opening and give the Fat Woman a show to join
the Young Victorian Patriotic League by next
election time.
[?]
846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
April 18, 1894.
My Dear Moorabinda—•
There is at least one “ town ” fashion which
hasn’t caught on in Australia yet, and which I
hasten to place before the local Anglo-maniacs in
time for the social season. It is that the up-to-
date British woman in a crush behaves more like
a circular saw than a human being, and without
the suspicion of an apology whether she rips up
somebody’s veil with the tip of her brolly, or
carries an eye off on the savage point of her orna-
mental hat-pin.
In the up-to-date English reception-room, too,
plain and fancy hustling is the most fashionable
behaviour for a well-dressed mob, and the woman
who apologises for carrying away half-a-yard of
tulle on her sharp shoe-buckle is immediately sus-
pected of being no lady. In fact, the woman who
begs your pardon is lost in “ town ” just now ; so,
when Potts Point prods me with its naked elbow
without apology, and treads on my heels without
looking shocked at itself, I will know the reason
o’t.
The very latest phase of British ignorance, as
applied to this impossible continent, cropped out
in a Sydney drawing-room the other day, when a
recent arrival from the shady side of Piccadilly
was asked by the hostess if he would come to a
dance? “ Weally delighted, Ai’m shuah,” said
the object, “ but Ai have no clothes. Dontcher-
see, Ai didn’t think evening dress would be worn
in Austrailyah, so Ai didn’t bothah bringing it.”
People who make a hobby of seeing the big
mail-boats off to “ town ” get a little amusement
out of it. For example, it is always interesting to
see how the only single captain now left in the
service (and even he is only the left-handed sort
of bachelor a widower makes) always sets steam
from a wharf absolutely crowded with ladies—
frisky little society women who are the last to
leave the ship, and at whom thebe-buttoned auto-
crat gazes with rapt opera-glasses as they linger
down the gangway. The rush of women to the
ship of this unattached male is simply sensa-
tional, and it strikes mo that the shipping
owners who want to boom their line ought to
advertise that none but bachelors command their
tubs, and a chaplain is always carried ! Though,
as a rule, mind you, the sea-dog moves in a
mysterious way his mashings to perform, and ik
takes a clever woman many voyages before she
knows for certain whether there i» a Mrs. Captain
or not!
Which reminds me that one single ruler,
on a certain line, was haunted by a devoted
voyageuse who pegged to and fro in his ship,
gazing up in his face from her sealed seat at
the cap’ns table, and always appearing under his
arm on moonlight nights in the shadow of the
smoke-stack, voyage after voyage. At last, when
he had landed her for about the forty-ninth time,
and observed her, on the day of sailing, coming
peacefully aboard for her fiftieth trip, his patience
gave out. “I say,” said he, desperately, “don’t,
you think it would be a square thing to give
another ship a chance ?”
But as I said (before I interrupted myself) yon
see some funny sights when the mail-boat is cast-
ing off. At a recent departure the crowd round
the freight shed was kept lively by the spectacle
of a departing youth hanging by the tips of his
toes to the bulwarks and holding out his yearning
arms to his sweetheart on the wharf, whom he
implored to “Kiss me, darling,” although there
were three or four feet of dubious wharf-water
between the quay and the ship.
And the darling’s superhuman efforts to balance
herself at right angles over the watery hiatus with
her lips puckered up ready for the salute shows
what a blank the whole grinning world is to a
woman when her best boy wants to hug her; ib
also proved that, after all, women aren’t much
more modest than men. However, the youth was
dragged away without the kiss he asked for,
though probably if he has many moonshiny nights
en route he will avenge his unkind fate somehow
if there is a pretty girl aboard.
The only point of interest that I discovered in
the recent Sydney newspaper controversy on
“Why don’t girls marry?” was when “A happy
father” burst into print to accuse other and pos-
sibly unhappy papas of not encouraging eligible
sons-in-law about the premises. “For his parb
he always made it a rule to encourage young men
whom, he knew to be gentlemen ’’—with the result
that all the dear girls got married, thanks to dear
pa’s sixpenny cigars !
But life will be a twig simply smothered in
matrimonial bird-lime if Pa augments the trans-
parent blandishments of Ma in making an un-
decided youth too comfortable when he only
meditates “ hanging up his hat.” Hitherto the
scowling parent who fidgets about the gas after
ten p.m. and converses in scornful grunts, has been
the olive in love’s saccharine young dream, and
has lent a fictitious uncertainty to the sure and
certain hope of winning the willing damsel—bub
if Pa takes to beaming from ear to ear from 7 p.m.
to midnight on Angelina’s victim, Gussie in
search of a wife will have a truly awful time.
Pa’s smiles would frighten Adonis off. Man
respects man’s opinion, and when Gussie sees his
Angelina’s progenitor on tenter-hooks to surrender
his darling che-yild to a stranger, he will eye the
che-yild with as much suspicion as a bargain-
hunter eyes the shopman who is concealing the
spotted part with a graceful fold of the “goods,”
His Excellency of You-Know-Where —he offers
people two fingers of his gloved hand at receptions
continues to run foul of his loving “subjects,”
and the last V.R. blunder is that the Government
steam-launch is large enough to carry a Crown
Minister or two beside the Governor and party,
but not the ladies of Crown Ministers. Conse-
quently, when his Ex. and her ladyship and a
comprehensive suite journeyed to the Darktown
show the other day, a politician, whose foot is
always on his native heath and his nameM’Gregor
in that particular district, was barred out of the
patronising party and travelled with his wife per
ordinary steamer for the first time in the whole
course of his parliamentary career. But where,
may I ask, is the woman who would give up the
fearful joy of sousing along in the same tub as the
Guv’nah’s party and travel in the cheap saloon of
a smoky coaster in order to stick to her husband ?
I am afraid that, under the circumstances, the
average Darby would have to make his pilgrimage

Apart from the invisible gratification of Mrs-
Politician at being thus snubbed out of the hunt,
it won’t make their Excellencies wildly popular
in feminine political circles if the penalty a vir-
tuous M.P. pays for fetching his legal consoler
with him to the side-shows is that he is shorn of
