simply because the Missus is “along.” And the
lady in question, too, considers herself some pota-
toes in the town hereinbefore alluded to. Her
bouquet on similar occasions has always been bub
two rosebuds smaller than Lady Blank’s. In the
best interests of the nation, if the Government
steamer really is too small for married Ministers,
do let us get one a more easy fit.
A recent ’Ome sensation is a woman who pulls
splinters out of herself at the rate of a hundred
per day, and as no pain attends the mysterious
exudation it must be rather pleasant to be able to
provide tinder for the kitchen-grate out of your
elbow-joints. Which reminds me of the Australee
man who got married, nob an age since, to a
charming girl, who was unfortunately handi-
capped with a wooden leg. Of course, the bride-
groom was “honourably” enlightened on the
subject of the timber toes, and in the magnanimity
of green inexperience declared that such a thing
was a distinct advantage to have in the family.
And so the marriage took place. But sometime
afterwards a trusted friend asked him how the
timber had turned out. “Ib is not the leg I
object to,” he said, sadly, “it’s the splinters !”
On dit that the grandfather (I notice a steady
increase of grandfathers in Australian society, by
the way) of the man who is going to marry one of
the richest of Southerners made his little heap in
preserving pigs, or—to pub ib more formally,
bacon. His uncle alleviates the porky aspect of
things by being of considerable literary distinc-
tion, and another prominent relative gets farther
away from Grunterdom by making his immortal
pile in a gold-mina Bub the true secret of the
family is hog, nevertheless.
Here is a Chinese anecdote fit to keep company
with that hideous Bulletin story “Shduffin.” Ib
occurred on a N.S.W. station, fed by a celestial
cook whose wonderful gift was the smoothness
with which he could spread his bread with butter.
No matter whether the season was one for oily
butter or for butter frozen into marbles, the rolls
of bread always came up as beautifully even as if
a dog had licked them. But there, it wasn’t the
dog that did it. Somebody coming quietly into
the kitchen one day discovered Ah Sin carefully
licking the spread loaf.
It is level-headed of Mrs. Brough to dress
“ The Second Mrs. Tanqueray ” to the last curl on
her sinful poll. Whatever is thought as to the
extent to which she revealed the inner workings
of the complex character of the second Mrs. T.,
all the women in front were quite satisfied that
lull justice was done as regards the outer husk.
In the first act a powder-pink silk with Beatrice
sleeves and lashings of fine lace was eclipsed in
more senses than one by the wrap in beaver cloth,
brown fur and brown silk embroidery, made from
a design by Mrs. Potter’s idol —Worth (of Paris).
In the second act, a very good-looking gown of
white stamped velvet on a satin ground with
shirred under-sleeves and artistic intervals of
yellow velvet, the bodice fastening up the back
with rosettes of white and yellow (rather sug-
gesting spinal notches, I thought) was just the
sort of expensive tackle a promoted demi-mondaine
would be likely to sport at breakfast; and it was
a relief when Grace Noble appeared in neat tailor-
cut grey. Miss Noble steals all through the
tragedy, by the way, in the Carmelitish tint,
like a grey tabby eat—which is a good enough
pattern for the character she takes in this parti-
cular play.
A second dress worn by Mrs. Brough in the
second act is a dark seal-brown out-door toilet
with the deep shoulder-flaps designed in black
jet and a vest of pale primrose silk—picture hat
of brown velvet trimmed with jet and feathers,
which will hardly live to the end of the run if
Mrs. Brough continues to scrunch it in her wrath
as she did at the premiere. But the way she
maltreats those feathers gives realism to the
situation. No woman falls foul of her best hat
unless she really means every word of it.
The swell gown of the play was worn in the last
two acts. It was very pale brocade—the colour
of town milk—brocaded in a design of pale-pink
flowers and made with a sacque back and full
train line in the front with quilted blue satin.
The underdress of the brocade was made plain,
but with enormous sleeves trimmed in a novel
way with silver-tagged laces tied in bows, and
frills of creamy lace. Diamond jewellery com-
pleted an imposing costume, which had, by-the-
way, another finishing touch—an inky-black
rosette on the bosom of it suggesting that she was
wearing her heart on her corsage for daws to peck
at. In the same acts Emma Temple, as the
peroxided Lady Orreyed (pronounced ’Orrid), is
very suitably dressed in petunia-mauve satin
theatrically contrasted with gold-spangled yellow
gauze and “ salted ” with wild-cat diamonds.
A pleasant thing in the deportment of Mrs. T.
No. 2 is that she doesn’t smoke a cigarette—which
looks as if the harmless paperpuff is losing ground
in the worst stage circles. But, instead of a
cigarette, we have strong language. It isn’t so
long ago that somebody wrote a play with a girl
in it who said “Damn !” and there was a news-
paper-war about it, but in “ Mrs. Tanqueray ” the
language is—er—forcible. “Deuce take it all!”
says the heroine, using the weakest oath in the
play.
By the way, B. & B.’s jeune premier, Mr.
Renouf (they don’t need to bill him as an importa-
tion : quarter-acre checks and tan boots speak
louder than words), has an unattractive habit of
sticking his hand in his pocket that makes it im-
possible to take him seriously. Even if it is the
go at ’Ome for a man to plunge his horny fist
among his loose silver in the exigencies of conver-
sation with a lady, it wasn’t very artistic to make
Captain Ardale keep one hand coolly in his
pocket for almost all the time he argued fran-
tically with Mrs. T. An actor who melodrama-
tically grabs the star by the wrist with one hand
and keeps the other pouched, looks about as tragic
as Romeo would shinning up the ladder in carpet
slippers.
The part of the husband, Aubrey Tanqueray,
taken by Mr. Titheradge, is a most ungrateful
one. Mr. Tanqueray is the sort of hero who says
“Never mind, my dear,” in an unmoved voice,
and then kicks up a gorgeous row about it. He is
always morally justified in his wild expostulations
—which is probably the reason why the audience
generally sides with Paula—but the change of the
pig’s ear to a silk purse is the mere A. 8.0. of
legerdemain to what Tanqueray expected of the
frisky Mrs. T. after twelve months or so of holy
matrimony.
There was a full and plenteous dress-circle to
sample Mrs. T,, but all I remember was a vision
of Essie Jenyns in a stage-box accompanied by a
lady in bright scarlet, and “Jack,” of course, who
ought to begin studying hair-restorers. The
divine Essie wore cream colour draperies and
diamonds, but the interest of the house was
absorbed by her fan. Slowly Essie let it drop
stick by stick on the ledge of the box—every stick
split right up as if the fan was broken to bits—
and then with a sudden wave it was all put to-
gether again and as sound as a cast-iron punkah.
What is the true inwardness of a fan that splits
its sides at the option of the purchaser I am at a
loss to conjecture, but it must be the very latest
snap.
The ground-plot of “Mrs. Tanqueray,” I may
say for the benefit of those who can’t be there, is
not complicated. A lonely widower marries a
demi-mondaine, in the cheerful hope that Mrs.
Grundy isn’t as black as she’s painted, and
believing that his daughter has taken religious
vows and won’t obtrude her innocence into his
experimental menage. But the frigid daughter
pops out of her convent —a lover turns up for her
who is one of the gilded youths the second Mrs.
T. has been “friendly” with, and when matters
get into this Gordian knot, Mrs. Tanqueray, with
the ghosts of dead-and-gone champagne suppers
whispering in her ear, disposes of it by suiciding
—behind the scenes.
I must confess to being sorry that the fascinating
Mrs. Gordon-Baillie has again got into trouble in
England. I am sorry because the handsome
adventuress, “ who a few years ago was quite as
good to look at as Lily Langtry,” says a man who
knew both, had the makings of a really good
sort of duchess had circumstances not perverted
her. “It is easy to be good on five thousand a
year.” A typical Highland lassie, full-blooded,
warm-hearted, was Mrs. Gordon B.
She was a washerwoman’s daughter, and yet by
her tact and cleverness in holding her tongue at
critical moments, her stately figure, quietly modish
dressing, and grand manner, she impressed you
as a lady despite her big hands—the only mongrel
point about her. Some women, of course, are so
adaptable that one voyage first-class in a P. & O.
is enough to give them the veneer of a marchioness,
and “girls quickly learn the trick of dress”—
but others, again, might go to the moon first-elass
and eat their supper with a teaspoon when they
got back. I know a Sydney woman who has been
the guest of no end of swells, but who tilts her
plate to this day when she eats porridge.
Like the elevated Australian personage who
alleged that she couldn’t sign a petition because
her gloves were tight, Mrs. G. B. never wielded a
pen in public, and it is possible that the scribal
contortions of Brough’s policeman in “Dandy
Dick ” are similar to what this fascinating woman
went through in writing a note. But then her
tact drew a veil over the scene, and if she wrote
with square elbows and her tongue out, the world
at least never knew it for certain. Then there
were the carefully-looked-after children, any of
whom were pretty enough to be the original of
Millais’ “Cherry Ripe,” as Mrs. G.B. said one
baby was ; and altogether—reviewing the whole
ladylike masquerade—it seems a pity even now
that an unpaid Melbourne milkman’s bill should
have spiked the wheel of one of the most charming
women Australia has ever done or been “done”
by.
A picture of the past: Mrs. Gordon-Baillie, in
the Governor’s box, affectionately stroking the
silky heads of the children of “the very first” in the
fair land of Australia. It’s no use now for people
to say they could “see through her.”
The marriage of Miss Chirnside and Everard
Brown is, I am told, to take place at the con-
clusion of the hunting season, and will be followed
by a trip “ ’Ome ” in time for the English one.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
The Wild-Cat Column.
That wonderfully dead-and-gone institution,
the Freehold Investment and Banking Co. of Melbourne,
wants to reconstruct on the most truly wonderful basis
yet heard of. Firstly, it is proposed to make a new co.
with a capital of £250,000, in 250,000 shares of £1 each.
Every shareholder who pays up his shares in the old,
busted Freehold Investment Co. to the extent of £6 per
share will get £1 shares in the new co. in exchange for
them, the other £5 per share being written off as utterly
lost and dead ! As it happens, however, the old shares
are now only paid up to £3 10s. and the most desperate
efforts of the liquidators (who have made a call of £2l
10s.) have only resulted in extracting an average of a few
coppers per share from the most utterly dead-broke set
of contributors in Melbourne. The great mass of the
contributors, in fact, either have no money or have it
securely planted, and they have successfully taken up the
attitude that the liquidators may go to Sheol. Seeing,
therefore, that they can get through—as they have
already demonstrated—without paying anything, the
suggestion that they should pay up £2 10s. per share, for
the pleasure of getting a £1 share in their own hopeless
co.— a co. which no one would have at a gift—is lovely.
When the new concern is formed, and the share-
holders have paid up 50s. a share, and 30s. has been
written off as lost, and in exchange for the other 20s. they
have got all their old debts and embarrassments back
again with the prospect of more calls in the future, (he
creditors are to be considered. The liabilities (£1,362,000
of them) are to be paid by “ \ ” debentures bearing 5 per
cent, interest. When the interest on the “ A ” debentures
can’t be paid in cash it is to be paid in “ B ” debentures,
and when the co. can’t pay the interest on either its “ A’’
or “ B ” debentures, it will probably set tle up by issuing
more debentures, even unto Z. This transaction means
that the new co. makes itself liable for £68,000 a year in
interest alone, and the shareholders are to pay 50s. per
share for the pleasure of having a 20s. interest in the right
to pay that interest. The “A ” debentures and the “B ”
debentures, and any other debentures, are to accumulate
until the co. can buy them up out of profits, at current
market rates, whatever these may be. When such a con-
cern is likely to have any profits no one Knows.
Ib all reads like a hideous joke or a nightmare.
In reality, the Freehold directors might as well try to re-
construct the original Moses of the Exodus, by issuing
debentures to Isaac Isaacs, the Hebrew bookmaker.
When Fink, the Jd. composition man, is able and
willing to pay 2s. 6d. in the £ as a bonus for
the privilege of paying up the 19s. in the £
which he still owes, then the Freehold Investment
scheme will be a success, but not till then. The
concern has nothing to reconstruct save its deficiency.
Among its principal shareholders are three of the biggest
boomers, and the prospect of these persons paying £2los.
per share on their stupendous mass of scrip is a jovful
circumstance. The present situation is that all the old
capital, reserve funds and alleged “undivided profits”
are completely lost, that three-fifths of the capital of the
new co. are lost before it is paid up, and that whether the
other two-fifths is lost or not is a matter for conjecture.
Which is not a gratifying basis to start any new co. upon,
or the sort of thing which leads to a frenzied rush of
subscribers.
* • * •
The Federal Bank balance-sheet for the latter
half .of 1891 was bolstered up with £95,000 of “ emer-
gency ” payments, which wiped out the big overdrafts for
a day or two and inflated the appearance of “cash at
bankers.” The ledger being ruled off, cheques were
drawn for the same amounts that had been paid in, the
overdrafts stood as before, and the sudden addition to
cash at bankers melted away like the shadow of a dream.
That is to say, a director who had exactly nothing to his
credit, or a debit already, drew a huge cheque which was
in effect duly honoured. There was no formal application
for a renewal of the old overdraft. It just vanished and
then popped up again, like a revolving light. This system
of balance-time finance was perfectly legitimate, accord-
ing to ex-manager Priestley, because “ the same thing was
being done in connection with other Melbourne banks.’’
And is being done now, The Bulletin presumes.
* * * »
Liquidator Priestley, in denying his personal
responsibility for many sins committed by the Federal
management, said quite enough to justify the contention
that no high official of that ill-used bank could be a
proper instrument for winding it up.

The reconstruction of the New Zealand Loan
and Mercantile Agency Company is sanctioned by the
Court, and a new*" board of directors appointed, including
Martin (of Turnbull, Martin & Co., shipowners), John
Beaumont, formerly of Christchurch (M.L.), and W. W.
Oswald, manager of the National Bank of Australasia.
If the directors face the situation squarely, there is no
reason why the co. should not prosper yet. There is any
amount of uncalled capital, and if the shareholders are
content to take the losses upon themselves, after the
valorous manner of the 8.N.Z., some years ago, instead
of trying to pass them over to the public, things will gee
straight away. Whether the old directors can be brought
to bock is not very clear.

Mr. M‘Mahon, landowner, of Clyde Elver,
N.S.W., on whose estate a little gold was lately found,
met 400 miners, and read the registered regulations under
which he would allow mining-amongst others the pay-
ment by the men of £1 a month each, and a quarter of
the gold found by them. The miners yelled and groaned.
No wonder. Thev knew that the gold belonged not to
Mr. M’Mahon, but to the State. “Subsequently a com-
promise was arrived at.” But the thing was a duffer.

Broken Hill estimated rates have fallen from
£12,095 last year to £9391. The mines, which paid £4221
last year, are now rated at £3496 ; in each case they con-
tributed over a third of the town revenues. Ratable
property has fallen from £256,329 to £188,354.
* » * •
Block 10 lode, struck in Campbell’s shaft 80ft,
below the 1015 ft. level, is no improvement on the 815 ft.
and 915 ft. levels, where it was driven through in cross-
cuts. It, is the usual forbidding sulphide, high in zinc,
low in silver. The theory of improvement of sulphides
at depth receives a distinct shock.

♦ * * •
In March, ’93, two men, Pain and Bartley,
found a reef while fencing at Yalgogrin, 30 miles west of
Wyalong; it showed paying stone and caused a bit of a
rush. The reef, being narrow, Pain and Bartley sunk 20ft.
to catch it thicker, but found it clean gone. Yalgogrin
men who are on Wyalong reckon the nature of the soil
exactly the same, and for the better field give the palm to
Yalgogrin.

When Wyalong rush first set in men employed
on adjacent railway stations took the gold-fever badly,
threw up their billets and started for the field. At Junee,
a busy junction, the station-master and one man done
remained at their posts. Even some of the men after-
wards sent from Sydney to fill the vacancies “ cleared.”
However, that’s all over now and many would like to get
their old billets back again.

Editor Bulletin. Sir, —I have to thank you
for your reference to the Bodalla reef, and to confirm
your statement as to, the enormous quantities of ore in
sight. At the same ’time, I desire that you will correct
the statement that attempts are being made to float. No
such attempts have been, or are being, made. The few
men associated with the Mumuga Creek leases on the
giant reef (and they include Mr. Johnson, M.P., of Ade-
laide, and Mr. E. Pritchard, of N.S. Wales) are hearing
the whole cost of development themselves, and have no
intention of asking the public to join them until they
have thoroughly tested the reef, and are fully satisfied of
its payable character ; and probably not then, unless
abnormally extensive reduction-plant is necessary. With
a view of exhaustively testing the reef, a small plant,
capable of crushing 100 tons of stone per w'eek, is now in
course of erection, and a trial lot of at least 1000 tons of
quartz will be put through before deciding upon further
steps. —Yours truly, Chas. L. Garland.

The Tas. Govt. Geologist has reported on a
large deposit of iron ore discovered at Blyth River (Tas.).
According to the analysis made, “ the ore is one of the
finest and purest in the w’orld, ranking with the famous
Spanish, Algerian, and Cohan ores, which are now
exported in very large quantities.”

♦ * * •
Victoria once had an organising inspector of
schools, named Joseph Geary, a brilliantly-educated man
of brilliant brains. He afterwards became a journalist,
and once wrote: “ If ever a man offers you a horse cheap,
ask him ‘ Why ?’ ” The Bulletin ventures on a piece of
advice, founded on Geary’s apothegm. Never buy into a
specimen-mine. If ever you see in a prospectus: “The
owners dollied from the reef 400 oz. in two days.” simply
a S k—“ Why didn’t they go on dollying ?” Broadly speak-
ing, no enduring mine is a specimen-mine. A good body
of stone in which you can only just see the gold—that is
the mine which makes a company rich. The specimen-
mine is the mine that rarely enriches anyone but the
individual. When a promoter shows you glittering hunks
of gold, button up your pocket. Follow this advice, and,
if you live and invest for 20 years, you will bless the
writer.
• » * *
Cutting the reef at the 200 ft. level of Bayley’e
mine has not solved the water question. Not a drop of
water is coming from the face, proving pretty conclusively
that the reef was met with in sinking the shaft below this
level, and the water drained. The battery is ready to start,
but must wait till the dam fills. Two showers, totalling a
little over half an inch, put very little into the tank—in
fact, only a few inches.
* » * ♦
It is alleged that the recent row between the
manager and the managing-director of Bayley’s mine at
least partially arose out of the system of setting a “ swell ’’
to watch each miner—and that it wasn’t a miner who was
charged with taking too much care of the rich stuff.
* * * *
The reef at the 200 ft. level of Bayley’s is hard
white quartz without any sign of ironstone or the mul-
locky leader about which so much has been said. The
gold is coarse and free, and the stone carries a lot of iron
pyrites, being much mineralised in places. The reef is as
big and strong as ever, and the only question to be settled
i s _how much gold does it carry ? One thing is certain,
the face now exposed is not as rich as those in the upper
levels, but further work must lie done before the value of
the shoot can be determined. The most satisfactory
feature of the new level is that the ironstone has cut out
but has not affected the gold, which still goes down.
* * ♦ *
Bayley’s Consols, immediately south of the
Reward claim, has got nothing as yet, and it is being
hinted that a slide has thrown the reef into the No. 1
South ground. This would make Bayley’s and Gorry’s,
on the eastern and western reefs, one, and would suggest
that a similar slide is accountable for the extraordinary
turn in the reef between the main shaft and the Kelly’s
block of the big mine.
* * * *
The official estimate of the value of Bayley’s
reef at the 200 ft. level is 50z., but this is probably rather
above the mark. The absence of water at this level sug-
gests that the lode was thoroughly opened and tested in
the shaft 20ft. lower down, where quartz was met with
weeks back.
* * • *
If the slide theory is accepted it will add vastly
to the importance of the blocks on the western side of the
Consols towards the Ritanita.
* * • *
The crowd interested in the Premier line of
reef, at the 25-Mile, Coolgardie, have had a fright owing
to the reef in two properties giving place to mullock.
The country is soft schist, and after following the forma-
tion for a few feet the quartz came in again, and now
carries rather better gold than at the surface.
* * * ♦
Bayley’s South No. 1 is driving south at the
100 ft. level, where the reef is from 6 to 14 feet wide,
howing splendid gold. The stone is exactly similar to
he 100 ft. stone in Bayley’s, and in places carries almost
equally rich patches.

gardie, some of which, taken up by D. Lindsay on his
behalf, resemble small sheep-stations, and will coat a

“Jack” Pearce, late manager of the Golden
Gully, N.S. W., has gone to Coolgardie as manager for the
Uniled Coolgardie syndicate. He was trained in the
quartz mines of Bendigo, managed a very wee alluvial
mine at Ararat, also a tin mine in India. There are few
better all-round managers in Australia. His last mine,
Golden Gully, was hardly worthy his good work.

The Murchison batteries mostly silent for want
of water. A few weeks ago, newspapers proclaimed that
the field had an abundance. Want of timber is another
great drawback, and the want of a railway the greatest
want of all.

A correspondent, en route to the Murchison,
writes from Geraldton : “ I see signs that the field is all
right. Men returning here mostly come for a spree. They
make first for a bank and then for a pub. Most of the
teams are engaged in carrying liquor to the field, while
machinery (of which there is an enormous quantity accu-
mulating here) is allowed to wait. Yes, I think the field
is good enough for me.”

» * * *
A cruel but common thing in the Coolgardie
districts is to quench one’s thirst and refuse to give any
water to the horses. Speaking to the writer, a returned
prospector said, “ Either ourselves or the horses had to
go without. So we tied them up and went into the bush
to drink. Yes, I have seen a horse shed tears as we
walked away with the water-bags.”

The prospectus of the Coolgardie Crushing Co.
is advertised in this issue. The co. is formed to supply
the battery-power so badly wanted at Coolgardie, and
under good management should give a handsome return.
The chief difficulty is water, but it is said an ample supply
is obtainable at 200 feet; if this is so profits should com-
mence straight away.
♦ ♦ * ♦
A fair average flock of sheep cuts about 71bs. of
wool per head, which yields a gross return of 3s. 6d. per
sheep, if the wool only realises sixpence per lb. under the
present low prices. From this has to be deducted shear-
ing expenses,'carriage to market, and selling commission,
so that in the case of a station at a long distance the nett
proceeds are reduced to about 2s. 6d. per sheep. This
means a clear profit of £875 from the clip of 7000 sheep,
and any practical man should average that income from
any of the three grazing farms, which are now being
offered by the Waggonora Building Society to its share-
holders, as they each consist of 20,000 acres of best salt-
bush country within easy access of the town of Cunna-
mulla, Q., and have 20,000 good young sheep divided over
the three properties. It is hardly surprising then that
anyone who can afford to invest £1 in a share should be
anxious to seize this opportunity of getting a nice little
sheep-station of 20,009 acres and 7000 sheep. The whole
thing in going order, without any interest or debt to pay,
and all for £l. There are only to be 20,000 shareholders,
and failing to win any of the three grazing farms, a share-
holder has five other chances of getting some of the 171
remaining prizes, worth from £9OO cash downwards.
Notwithstanding bad times and scarcity of money, appli-
cations are continually coming in, and the list of share-
holders closes 25th May, unless previously filled.

A “Hard Case."
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Ay, it’s me, boys—alive—though I’m bloomin’
well cooked ;
’Twon’t be long till I pull up my pegs.
Fetch a doctor? What use?—just to tell me I’m
booked !
Damn it! mind how you handle my legs !
Shouldn’t swear ? Perhaps not! Though I’m
blest if I see—
Since I’ve cussed for these sixty years past
How ’twould make things a morsel the better for
me
Just to turn mealy-mouthed at the last!
I’m the wrong sort of joker to snivel, or pray
To be passed up aloft “ on the nod.”
I was never no good on the hypocrite’s lay,
And you can’t put no games up on God !
If He’s right on the job, as the parson-chaps tell,
He is bound to know all about me—
How that, plank me in heaven or dump me in
hell,
I’d be just the same Billy Magee !
How was it? Oh ! just as you said it would be—
“ That my bones in this gully I’d smash.”
’Twas a slip and a stumble-a grasp at a tree —
And old Billy was knocked into hash !
Was I tanked? To the eyes!—and don’t you
fellows say
That I funked about that when I croaked,
Nor make out, if I’d lived, I’d have altered ray
way,
For I’d like to die bloomin’ well soaked !
But I’ve come to the end of ray tether; and so
Don’t you write up no lies over me !
But just say on the stone that the party below
Was poor, drunken, old Billy Magee!
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.

would quit the company and retire to Moss Yale.
There is a cry from the sugar-districts of
N.S. Wales for the abolition of the excise duty on
locally-distilled rum. It is said that the sugar
industry is languishing. In truth, sugar without
rum never has paid, and never will pay, and the
duty, together with the expense of maintaining
the excise-officer necessary tor its collection, puts
any but very large distilleries out of the question.
Thus the Colonial Sugar Co. has acquired a prac-
tical monopoly, which, like all monopolies, has
become oppressive. The danger of throwing open
the business of distilling is a chimera. A strict
Adulteration Act would prevent inferior spirit
going into consumption, and at any rate open dis-
tillation would at once shut down the host of
illicit stills now in operation in defiance of the
law. Once abolish or largely reduce the excise-
duty, and N.S. Wales’enormous surplus of maize
would be turned into corn whisky—a far more
wholesome tipple than a good deal of the imported
gjotch and Irish.
Dear Bulletin, —“ Why don’t white men
pay more attention to vegetable growing in Aus-
tralia?” A swagsman lately told me that he and
two mates started it in N.S.W. and found that
they could grow vegetables quite as well as any
Chinese; but they also found that when they
offered them for sale the wife of the Australian
working-man, as a rule, absolutely declined to
buy, preferring—although there was advantage
neither in price nor quality—to deal with the
Mongolian. My authority assured me he could
have dune well at the business but for this un-
accountable prejudice and, as a case in point,
instanced the fact that in Melbourne the Chinese
were gradually but effectively acquiring a mono-
poly of this form of trade. His only explana-
tion is that the fascinating Chow, in going round
to each'different house, puts on every article a
price above that which will pay him to part with
it. He knows that the average woman, who is
always a bargain-hunter or a sweater, will feel so
proud of herself when she can beat John down a
penny or so that she will always think she is
saving money by dealing with him.” — Paul
Blunt.
GOVERNMENT BY PALAVER.
Eagles and reptiles alone may mount to the crest
of the cliff that is cleaving the sky,
And the places of power are the prize and the prey
of such as can creep or can fly ;
But these whom “ Our Rulers ” we name cannot.
Now, tell me, how rose they so high?
Fearless in fight and a man of might, wary of eye,
and in counsel cool,
Must the Leader stand in a troubled land who
rises to right and to rule,
But to reign in the House of Palaver ye need but
the Purse of the Fatted Fool.
I hear the roar of a rock-bound shore where the
waves in thunder sweep;
I hear on the edge of a beetling cliff that bends
o’er the billowy deep,
In the midnight dread, a fearful tread where a
nation walks in sleep.
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
Of Mrae. Melba, thus a Parisian correspon-
dent in one of the papers :
Melba has a fault quite common with artists—that of
singing to someone she knows in the hall, and she warms
up only on the condition that she has friends listening to
her. Strange faces freeze her, and then she becomes
indifferent. Melba mads a great mistake to go to America
in a crowd. She ought to have gone alone, in better
times, with a good company, as Patti and Nilsson did, and
then she would have been appreciated at her true worth.
It is not often that one meets with such an agreeable
voice as Melba’s, and with such a superb method, and
comparisons are often odious. They certainly were in the
judgments between her lyrio talent and that of Oalvd.
Naturally, perfect acting is attractive to the general
public, and it takes an educated ear to understand per-
fect singing ; and to my mind that is why one had more
success than the other in America. It was not possible to
see a more plain, insignificant type of an Englishwoman
than Melba when she first came to Paris to take lessons of
Marches!. Her plain woollen dress, too short in the skirt,
looked like the last year’s cut of an obscure provincial
town; the combination of colours showed that she had
not the least notion of what taste was in dress, and her
hat looked as if it had been made for anyone else’s head
but hers. To-day she is not only the best-dressed woman
in Paris, but she wears her clothes as if she were born
with one of Doucet’s last creations. Mine. Marchesi told
me that, of the three stars now occupying the music-
loving public in America, Melba is the greatest musical
artist.

According to N.S.W. Premier Dibbs, it
takes two policemen to watch (or protect) his
rival, Barkes, all the year round. Parkes didn’t
deny the soft impeachment—not he. It all adds
to his importance. Nothing would please him
better than to have a bodyguard constantly attend-
ing him, in the style of a feudal lord, with heralds
and trumpeters warning people to “ Make way,
make way, for the illustrious Prince Parkes ! ”
Brqkenhillionaire Horn, a M.P. of S.A.,
was most anxious to get Bray’s place as Agent-
General in the interest, probably, of the inevitable
knighthood. He was ready to do the work
without salary, which would have been a grate-
ful change after Bray, who drew the salary but
did no work. Playford, however, had a strong
grip and would not let go the place.

ing Australian audiences with an account of her
remark-able a-chieveraents, once told a Towns-
ville audience that she was the only woman who
had ascended Mount St. Elias in Alaska ; where-
upon an enthusiastic male admirer of the lady’s
6ft. lin. and otherwise impressive attractions,
thumped the floor loudly with his stick and
exclaimed : “ Good, old woman, / believe you ! ”
Waiapu diocese (Maoriland) which Bishop
Stuart deserted in the interests of Persian mis.
sionary labour, will remain widowed of an over-
seer till the diocesan fund is swelled to =£lo,ooo.
The fund, on the interest of which Bishop Stuart
had to survive with episcopal dignity, was only
.£5OOO, and missionarying in Persia will be a less
tax on his ingenuity than supporting apron and
gaiters was in Waiapu.
Re departmental censorship of Civil ser-
vants’ names. Years ago a Brisbane lady-teacher
yclept Mary, paid a visit to Paree. Writing to the
Education Office, on her return from France, she
signed herself “Marie.” Whereupon the Queens-
land Education Office, which has the highest
reputation for urbanity, wrote back to Miss Marie
requesting her to notify the Department in case
she wished to Frenchify her name. '
Among Queensland Civil servants is one
Church to four Bishops, two Deacons, three
Deans, five Sextons, two Popes, one Pagan, one
Savage, one Crane, one Crow, one Drake, one
Swallow, ten Marlins, one Wren, one Dove, one
Nightingale, two Herons, one Good, no bad ; one
Rich, no poor; one Sword, with Shield, and
Armour; three Kings, without Queens; twenty-
seven Browns, four Blacks, ten Whites, one Blue,
three Grays, six Greens, two Judges to one Court,
one Cain and one Abel, six Adams, but no Eve, to
one Eden ; one Curry with two of Rice.
Railway-contractor Robb, whom bank-
smashes have financially crippled, during the
progress of the great Cairns Railway Arbitration
case (Robb v. Queensland Govt, for £225.000,
extras on contract) gave a luncheon to all parties
concerned, and in replying to a toast, expressed
his wearim sj of toil, and stated that, should he
get a verdict for a fair amount, he would realise
on all his properties, buy a farm on his native
Scotch mountain and spend the rest of his days in
peace. “I suppose you’ll call the place ‘Dun-
robin?’” mischievously suggested one of the
opposing counsel. The veteran laughed gaily
with the rest.
Dowe, of bullet-proof-armour fame, only
applies to human covering the principle which old
Mr. Arnold, of Marrickville, Sydney, after a life of
experimenting, applied to the covering of ships of
war. Governors Hercules Robinson and Loftus,
with a variety of Premiers, visited Arnold and
examined his work, leaving testimonials as to the
value of his invention. Arnold’s hope was that
he could get it taken up by the Britisli Admiralty,
hut influence seemed to fail him in that quarter.
The present writer fired a revolver at the leathery
composition, at a distance of 10 feet, and picked
up the bullet, which rebounded, without doing
more than leaving a mere “dent” in the com-
position.
“ Thank God, so far as I am aware, during
200 years the name of Hopetoun has been borne
by my family it has never been disgraced, and I
do not think this township need be ashamed of its
god fattier. ” —(Hopetoun at Hopetoun, Vic.) Also,
as far as The Bulletin is aware, the small collec-
tion of crude buildings need not assume frill on
the strength of its toady-made title. The pro-
bability that no Hopetoun has committed murder,
or been tenanted by cruel fortune to “ shake ” a
pair of boots, is nothing to feel proud of, any-
way, whereas the certainty that gudpa failed to
pass a military examination some years ago is
evidence that the new township might as well
have adopted the name of Jones. There are plenty
of clever Joneses about.
Young Toby Barton (son of G. B. Barton
and nephew of the Toby Barton), who died nob
long ago, to the great regret of all who knew him,
was an outspoken wit who would have made his
mark in the world. To him once the grim host at
a caravanserai where the meals were uninviting :
“Off your feed, Mister Barton?” “No,” replied
Young Toby, “off yours.” One day, at a meeting of
a much-perfumed and hideously fashionablecricket-
club of which the youth was a member, a certain
individual, gushing over a civil letter just received
from Lord Somebody thanking the club for its
hospitality, said : “Our club is the nobbiest club
in 'Australia, and its nobbiness, I claim, is entirely
owing to the committee.” “Yes,” chorused
young Toby, with a face of aggravating solemnity,
“our club is the snobbiest club in Australia, and
its snqbbiness, we are all quite willing to admit,
is entirely owing to the committee.”
The mental equilibrium of John Bray, the
Agent - General for South Australia, always
seemed, from an outside point of view, to be safe-
guarded by his pompous dullness. The writer
conjures up a vision of Bray as he appeared when
filling the chair at a Federation “Conference”
held by A.N.A. delegates in a little room atMelb.
Town Hall. The solemnity of the man amidst
those wildly inane proceedings was magnificent in
its way. There he sat, the personification of im-
portance, whilst Ninian Melville roared, “ The
eye of Europe is watching our conference, Sir
John, and I will never consent,” &c., &c., and
Peacock called upon the Natives to be true to
themselves at that great crisis of the world’s
history. Bray looked incapable of losing the little
bit of reason that was so deeply imbedded in his
self-esteem. His brain lias succumbed, perhaps,
to the strain of keeping Bray’s greatness up to
concert pitch in a city where his dignity was often
under-estimated. London contains too much
population for personages of the Bray type. He
might walk the streets for weeks without hearing
anyone exclaim “ There’s Sir John ! ” and his mind
broke down at last. Fortunately, the average
•knight has his title always ringing in his ears, and
fancies he can detect admiration in the glance of
every creature who crosses his path. That keeps
him out of the asylum.
HARRY LAING, champion boxer of Maoriland—killed
last week by falling from a train in motion.
Tom Playford goes to London to succeed
Bray as S.A. Agent-General. Nothing could be
more dissimilar than these two men physically,
mentally, or morally, but both have a sharp eye
to the main chance. Playford is 6ft. 6in. high
and broad in proportion, be is lumbersome in bis
modes of thinking, and an ascetic in his habits of:
living. Bray is sft. nothing and thin, he is as
cute as a child of Israel, and he has pampered his.
senses in more ways than one. Playford first,
entered the Assembly as M.P. for Onkaparingai
in April ’6B, but in Nov. ’7l be was shot out by
this Teutonic constituency because he indiscreetly
remarked on one public occasion tliat “the
stinkwort, like a lot of other bad things, came
from Germany.” However, he persuaded E ist
Torrens to elect him in Eeb. ’75, and, excepting,
three years, during which, taking advantage of the'
second batch of contests, he was returned and sat
for Newcastle, he has stuck to that district ever
since. He has twice been Premier of the pro-
vince, and has served in Ministerial office for an
aggregate of 3654 days. Bray sat for 21 solid
years in one stretch as member for East Adelaide,
was Premier for three years. He became Agent-
General in January ’92. Bray’s father was a
shoemaker. The senior Playford fought as a
private soldier at Waterloo, and was afterwards a
Methodist parson in Adelaide.
Sydney can lay claim to the champion
polite letter-writer. A weekly paper prints a.
letter from a correspondent to the editor, whom
he addresses as “ Accursed Skunk.”
Owing to a mistake in the mechanical
department the advertisement of the Mutual Life
Insurance Company of New York, setting forth
its annual statement was omitted from our last
issue. It appears this week on page 21, and, as
will be seen, substantiates the reference made to
it in a par. last Bulletin. The assets of the co.
total <£38,338,332 13s. 7d., or about three and a
half million more than those of the next largest,
life-insurance office in the world.

POLITICAL POINTS
The (Lost) Paradise of the working man—
Victoria.
The 24th May approaches. O.M.G. :
Cheap Meretricious Glory ; also, Candidate Moral
Gallows.
“The Conservative believes that Provi-
dence is on his side. The Liberal—that he is on
the side of Providence.” — Zangwill.
The N.S.W. Ministry has “friends.” The
anti-ant business smells ; so apparently does the
Garvan estate improvement business.
Both Max Hirsch’s Beacon and Jos.
Symes’ Liberator appeal for aid, or—they die.
Absolute cocksureness always means martyrdom.
Chief-Secretary Gordon was S.A. Acting-
Premier during Kingston’s absence in Westralia.
Thus were settled the rival claims of Holder and
Playford.
The resolution not to sign the Labour
Electoral League pledge was the first absolutely
solid vote recorded by the N.S.W. Parliamentary
Labour-party.
The demand for Queensland-grown tobacco
is becoming so great throughout Australia that
the Northern Customs authorities propose a new
saale of excise-duties.
Two Sydney newspapers have offered
themselves from title-page to imprint to the
N.S.W. Freetrade Association for three months
at “ nominal figures.”
Be Mestre once said he was “crushed
under an enormous weight of nothing.” Which
accurately describes the present condition of all
the Australian Parliaments.
Clark is the only new Tasmanian Minister
who has established a definite claim to democratic
regard ; but almost anything is better than the
sway of the Dobson family.
Sydney has now four Labour papers : the
Workman, the Worker, Justice and the New
Order, and not one of them has a single good
word to say of any of the others.
The Yic. Government justifies its appoint-
ment of railway-station mistresses on “ the broad
ground of economy.” A direct contention that
female services are less valuable than male.
A significant, though small, circumstance is
that Frank Cotton, M.P., the N.S.W. Single-tax
leader, with seeming habitude, glibly writes of
sheep fanning as “the national industry.”
“ The three lions in the path of the Western
N.S.W. wool-grower are pine-scrub, rabbits, and
confiscation of invested capital in the shape of
improvement 3. Frank Cotton, M.L.A.
At Naraudera, N.S.W., the use of the
courthouse was refused to the aborigines flooded
out on the river flat. But it was granted to the
Chinese, when in a similar predicament two years
ago. Australia for the Australians !
Cabled that General Booth intends, unless
some unforeseen difficulty arises, to establish one
of his “oversea colonies” in Westralia. The
“ unforeseen difficulty ”is going to arise. It will
consist in the fact that very soon Westralia will
contain an army of diggers.
One of the letter-receivers at Sydney
G.P.O. has the following disloyal inscription :
Foreign letters other than those for the United
Kingdom.” And yet a N.S.W. Ministry once
struck a J.P. off the rolls for publicly alluding to
England as “ a foreign country.”
Rev. Hopper Joplin quits Australia for
ever. He says he tried by preaching in “ a Demo-
cratic fashion ” to induce men to aro to church, but
admits his efforts failed. Joplin was, therefore,
“a democrat” with an ulterior purpose. Alas,
how many are Joplins in that respect!
Premier Kingston (S.A.) has frequently
pledged himself to his constituents not to accept
knighthood. Apropos, “Honest Tom” Playford
was offered knighthood awhile ago and declined,
at the solicitation of the S.A. Premier. The two
have always been warm personal friends.
Sydney Labour-Councilman Flowers, sup-
posed to vent an official voice, says that the
Labour-party will be dissatisfied with the next
N.S.W. elections if afterwards it is not strong
enough to govern. The Labour-party will govern
the country when it has learnt to govern itself.
Albert J. Gould, M.P. (N.S.W.), the
colourless representative of Patrick’s Plains,
N.S.W., actually disbelieves in something—pay-
ment of members. Gould, as an empty-headed
squireen, was Parkes’s ideal of a Minister of
Justice. “J. Gould” sounds strange applied to
vacuity.
Melbourne paper
The Secretary of State for the Colonies has granted
Lord Hopetoun permission to spend the last six months
of his term, from April to November, 1895, on leave.
Premier Patterson ought to return the compliment
by granting Queen Victoria six months’ leave on
full pay.
Patterson’s right-about-face on the fiscal
system at Williamstown and the wrath of the Age
are said to be but beat-drops in comparison with
the thunder-storm to follow. Rumour in the
Vic. railway department says that Patterson
intends reinstating Speight, and that he gave a
promise to this effect to the departed Gillies !
Varney (N.S.W.), wants
every candidate for Parliament (or even for an
aldermanoy) to be compelled to pass an examina-
tion to test his knowledge or ignorance of public
affairs. Varney is illogical when be bases his
argument on the ground that Ms.P. are paid
salaries, since he also applies it to aldermen who
are not paid, and whom he does not propose to
pay.
Premier Patterson’s attack on the Protec-
tive duties, at Williamstown, the other day,
sounded the tocsin of renewed battle of the tariffs.
When Parliament meets, the Labour-party (who
are first of all Protectionists) must throw in their
lot with the Opposition; and a cry has been
raised for the Protectionists to close up their
ranks.
Ileid, Vie. Minister of Defence, and State-
aided drummer, tells Westminster Gazette that
“ a power is steadily growing in Australia which
will become an important factor in any future
war in which the Old Country may be engaged.”
Reid, no doubt, alludes to Victorian brandy. He
is trying to induce the authorities to use it in the
army and navy.
Josiah Symon, Q.C., once president of the
long since defunct S.A. Freetrade League, will
try to win election for Treasurer Playford’s vacant
seat at East Torrens, for the express purpose of
badgering Premier Kingston, whom he hates. The
Labour-party pin their faith to that pious share-
broker and aggressive single - taxer, Cornelius
Agrippa Proud.
The weightiest critic against N.S.W. banks
Was Premier Dibbs, who practically published
allegations of their insolvency by his panic legis-
lation. But Dibbs isn’t in gaol. Only the most
miserable of the runners who sold copies of a
foolishly candid paper, were housed in prison. If
Hard Cash libelled the banks, Dibbs “soft-
cashed ’’ them.
£IOJ,OOO is to be spent on the new bridge
across Brisbane River, replacing that partially
destroyed by the floods. This should make dis-
tressed Brisbane cheerful. Government money,
Of course, and two-thirds drawn from N. Queens-
land for the benefit of Queen-street property-
owners, who sniff contemptuously when you talk
of betterment taxation.
If the present crowd of Freetrade and
Protectionist members are sent back toN.S. W.
Assembly in a fairly substantial body at next
elections, leaders of both parties assume that pay-
ment of members will be either reduced by one-
half or abolished. A test-question at the elections
should therefore be—“ Are you in favour of pay-
ment of members ?” —meaning thereby £3OO a
year, which is not too much.
The Rue- Australians meet a generosity
from the Paraguayan Govt, which they wouldn’t
find in Australia. The Govt, gives the seceders
land at 3d. an acre, finds them food for six months,
gives them a cow and a calf, and cuts roads
through the scrub for them, besides supplying
tools at cost-price and seeds for the first crop.
And, as the soil is good, and it’s a case of “Root
hog or die,” the men are likely to give a good
account of themselves.
Holder, Commissioner of Works, will take
S.A. Treasurership, vice Playford, and John
Greeley Jenkins, Government whip, will succeed
Holder. “Jenks,” a ’cute Yankee in the book-
agency business, is a fairly consistent democrat,
WYALONG, WEST END, SHOWING GRENFELL STREET, RUNNING FROM LEFT TO RIGHT.
and is famous because he defeated the only unsuc-
cessful Labour candidate at last year’s general
election. “The member for Amurnka ” has
always been sore because Kingston omitted him
when forming his Ministry last June.
The unlucky Townsville (Q.) breakwater
has been badly damaged in the recent floods. This
outrageous mullock-heap was built at a cost of
£3OO 000 (Govt, money) in the vain attempt to
make a decent port of Cleveland Bay, with the
result that there was actually less water after the
breakwater was built than before. And 90 miles
down the coast is Bowen with a splendid natural
harbour, which a few thousands would make one
of the best in Australia. But there is no political
influence at Bowen.
Parkes, in one of his latest hours of
solemn reflection, observed: Never no, you
never will have a Press capable of independent
criticism until you renounce the advertisement
patronage from the Government of the day.
[The Press will never be independent until it
does without advts. altogether and lives on its
circulation.—Ed. B.]. lam as guilty as others,
but was first struck by the enormity of the
political profligacy when the Stuart-Dalley tribe
bushranged the seat of Government and have
held myself ready since that day to help any man
to put an end to the system of Press-bribery.”
Administrator Macgregor has had just
about enough of New Guinea. He must have
swallowed a good half-cwt. of quinine during the
last six years, for there’s no other way of fighting
the fever constantly prevalent. MTlwraith and
Co. won’t be averse to his departure, for Mac-
gregor has steadfastly set his face against the
deportation of natives to Queensland, and the
scarcity of Kanakas is becoming serious to the
black-labour planters. Already the underground
engineers are hard at work preparing for tne im-
portation of thousands of “cheap and reliable ’’
labourers from New Guinea. But the prospect
doesn’t look as bright as it was before Townsville
and Ipswich elections.
The amateur J.P. must go. It is a poor
country which can’t afford stipendiary justice.
Here is a sample of the kind of letter The Bul-
letin is constantly receiving
A certain Western J.P. sits on the Publicans’ Licensing
Bench, though he has an interest in a hotel; also, when a
butcher applies for a butchering licence, the J P. (a
butcher) refuses to sit on the bench for a few days,
thereby delaying the issue of the licence, as there are hut
two J.P.’s in town.
The foregoing may be true, or it may not. But
such tilings are eternally happening; and from
Melbourne, with its brazen roll-up of brother-
tradesmen and secret society men to pack the
benches, to the far Gulf country, there is naught
but rottenness and injustice in the J.P. system.
Since the recent Labour victories at
Queensland elections the following reasons for
Ministerial defeats have been given by the Q.
capitalistic press
(1) The constituency returning Labour-tnembef is only
an ii significant place which doesn’t count. This one elec-
torate is not all Queensland.
<2; The Labour platform is becoming so modified that it
differs very little from that of the Ministry.
(3) Only about half of the electors voted. Consequently
this election is no criterion of the real state of feeling in
the country.
(4) The candidate is not really a Labour man at all.
The Courier only (strange to say) honestly owns
up to defeat. Thus There is no denying the
significance of the Ministerial defeat. If Philp
(Mines) and Barlow (Lands) were to resign to-
morrow their seats would probably be captured
by the Labour-party. It would seem as if Queens-
land, in addition to its other misfortunes, were to
be the first colony to fall into the hands of the
Labour-party.”
Victoria, it seems, is to be the first of the
Australias to confess that its alleged democracy is
really a Fattoeracy which must needs have a Poor
Law, as an attempt to coax off the discontent of
its poverty-stricken masses. Treas. Carter is
drafting a Bill, which, amongst other provisions,
will appoint a Central Charities Council to receive
and distribute all charitable grants made cither
by the State or by municipalities, and a percentage
of the receipts from all public entertainments held
on “Crown” recreation reserves. Further, the
municipalities will be compelled to vote annually
a certain minimum percentage of rates for charity.
There is but a short step from the proposed Bill to
the establishment of parish poorhouses. Being
possessed of epidemic misery, Australian demo-
cracy does not object to poorhouses ; but how its
protesting gorge must rise against the nauseous
admission that its present condition breeds a vile
pauperism that has no present hope, beyond the
stolid refrigeration of old-world, workhouse
machinery !
Bagman Reid, M.P., of Victoria, now in
London, is drunk with gush. “In addressing a
meeting of volunteers in Westminster-hall, ha
expressed his thanks to the Duke of Cambridge,
the Commander-in-Ohief, for having sent such
splendid officers to commands in Australia. Mr.
Reid asserted that the colonial volunteers Were
ready to fight on behalf of Great Britain ill any part
of the world.” it is impossible, alas, for the Aus-
tralian nation to cable a muzzle to this fatuous
drummer. There are in Australia hundreds of
bravoes who, partly for money and partly for pure
blood lust, would be ready to fight anywhere for
Britain, or even for China. But— and'it is a big
but—they won’t ever get any more money out of
the State Treasury to go away and fight with.
“BILLY ” LANE, the “New Australia” leader.
Softgoods Reid, asked by a British inter-
viewer : “In case of war, you in Victoria are not
afraid of a hostile visit from the navy of any
European power?” defiantly inflated his chest ;
“ Not at all,” he replied. “We should give them
a particularly warm reception. The Cerberus (a
25-year-old tub capable of steaming a mile and
a-half per week) would blow almost any out
of water ;we in Australia are all Britons. ’ I hi*
comes of inflating a chunky shopman with mili-
tarism, and then letting him escape. It is now
necessary to include in each Australian Constitu-
tion a law prohibiting any Minister from visiting
England during his term of office—so many want
to rush off and exhibit themselves in the dear old
mother country immediately upon securing the
right to place “ Hon.” on their luggage. We can-
not be held responsible for the grimaces and
gyrations of a jumped-up politician whose chief
intention in going ’Ome is to amaze Ins lubberly
relations and those people in the village who
remember him only as a “ pore ” boy with holes n
his pants.
New Tasmanian Ministry : Premier (with-
out portfolio). Edward Braddon ; Treasurer, P O.
Fysh; Chief Secretary, William Moore; Attorney
General, A. I. Clark; Lands, A. T. Pillinger ;
Minister (without portfolio), Thos. Reibey. Brad-
don is an ex-civil servant (India), an ex-volunteer
soldier, an Indian pensioner, and an ex-Agent-
General of Tasmania. His chief recommendation
is that he is a brother of Miss Braddon, the
sensational novelist. Philip Oakley Fysh is a
Cockney, and used to trade in soft-goods till
politics seized him; he also has been a volunteer
rnajah, but strictly for show, while Braddon has
smelt powder. Attorney-General Clark is a
Hobartian, a good lawyer and advocate, and an
avowed Republican who can be relied on not to
take a little. Pillinger is simply a sticker-on in
Parliament. Tom Reibey used to be an Anglican
parson, and rose to be an archdeacon. His mother
was a sister of the late Wigram Allen (N.S.W.
Speaker). The annals of Tasmanian law-courts
mention Archdeacon Reibey in at least one case
that would make first-class “copy.” Reibey has
been Opposition Leader, Premier, Col. Secretary,
and Speaker.
Among the late Bathurst (N.S.W.) blasts
of Premier Dibbs, let these bo noted
When the province sent 36 Labour-mm into Parliament,
it tried an experiment by which it has not benefited. . •
The proclamation [proroguing Parliament! which had
shaken the nerves of the country had to be issued to sober
the Parliament. . . The fact that the Gov* imiinnt
obtained supplies for the current year on a test division
by a majority of 27 votes, justified it in every act it has
done. . . We are not going to have a suicidal fight
between Protection and Freetrade. . . Protection and
Freetrade have hitherto separated the best men in the
country. . . If certain questions are not dealt with
now, it will be years before the country recovers its wont d
prosperity. . . The whole thing might be summed up
in the wards—“ Stable Government.” . . The question
of the central leases is to be dealt with in Cabinet to-
morrow. . . Legislation will be prepared for the pur-
pose of dealing finally with this matter, so as to give the
country more confidence and establish confidence in the
pastoral industries. . . It we can restore the pastoral
industry to its position as the backbone of New South
Wales. . . When the Local Government Bill comes, it
w ill be a full and fair answer to the land-taxers and single-
taxers. . . There will be absolutely no room for the
land-taxer and single-tsi er.
It is difficult to accept the shake-up of a kaleido-
scope seriously, but the above fragments, dove-
tailing with many rjeent Dibbsian eccentricities,
seem to point suspicion to these five things as
planks to be avowed in Dibbs’ electioneering plat-
form :—(l) We confess that we side with the Fat
Man class, as against Labour. (2) We abandon
fiscal differences in order to consolidate the Fat
Party against the Lean. (3) We incline to a
Coalition, under the guise of “stable govern-
ment.” (4) We deify the squatter. (5) We will
dodge the land-tax by the Local Government Bill.
En passant: “ A suicidal fight between protection
and freetrade” could only have come from a
republican nobleman.
THE SUPPLIANT.
Whatever requisitions we make of the native races in
Africa, the latter have at least this guarantee —the proved
honesty of our dealings among them for tire past 20 years.
If this be not sufficient to ensure compliance with our just
demands, we must resort to stronger measures, &c.—
Times.
There came a churl with dagger bared and shining
And boldly oped a decent burgher’s door ;
Stepped in and asked, in accents soft and whining,
“ Have you no trifle for the homeless poor?
I know your pity’s ever prompt and speedy,
I know you sympathise with others’ woe;
I know your heart beats kindly for the needy—
For all whose lot is hopeless here below.
I beg in suppliant tones —I come not plying
The bandit’s trade beneath the beggar’s part—
I plead ” (and here he showed the steel) “relying
Entirely on the goodness of your heart.”
P. LUFTIG.

A letter from Lord Carington, lately
received in Sydney, shows that he was aware of
the neglect in store for him in the new arrange-
ments of the Liberal party. His claqueurs made
him successively Lord Lieut, of Ireland, Gov.-
General of India, and Gov.-General of Canada,
and were so dismally out in their reckoning that
when they now dangle the Gov.-Generalship of
Federated Australia before him he merely yawns.
But, though politically he sits far back, the letter
indicates that his animal spirits are as exuberant
as they were years ago, and it is quite possible
that if he were again offered his old post in Syd-
ney he would pack up and sail out. It is better to
reign in Hell than serve in Heaven.
# * »
.Sydney University Commemoration was
marked by the usual display of larrikinism by the
students —“ young gentlemen ’’ belonging to “our
best families,” and trained at considerable ex-
pense to the country to know that the hoodlum
who stands at the street corner and spits is a dis-
grace to civilisation, while the hoodlum who
mocks, shouts, jeers, and howls in the presence of
ladies and his seniors in authority, age, and
knowledge, is the flower of culture and refine-
ment.
# • •
Dear Bulletin, —I want to find the
meanest man (or men) in Australia. When I
knew a certain big squatter ten years ago he never
used to say his prayers in bad seasons. Said that
if the Almighty didn’t help him, he didn’t see his
way to shove along the Almighty. When there
was plenty of rain and a good lambing, the
squatter’s incense ascended regularly night and
morning. I used to think that was pretty mean.
Perhaps Melb. Trades’ Hall Council beat it when,
after damning the Chinkies all over the country,
they accepted an official Chinkie subscription of
£25 in aid of the ’9O seamen’s strike. That was
mean. And the other day, up at Bathurst, when a
poor devil of an unemployed stripped and went
into the water to look for the bodies of the
drowned girls whom M‘Credie tried to save,
somebody came along and robbed his clothes,
taking his miner’s right, his railway pass, and
something like Is. 9d. in cash. That was d d
mean, and I think even The Bulletin would
like to give that thief a touch of the whip. Do
you know anything meaner, now, or do your
readers ? I want Australian meanness, mind, not
foreign-we have plenty of our own. —Yours,
Thin Man.

declined to hear a waistcoatless solicitor who rose
before his Honor to “move probate” until the
“gent, one” had—buttoned up his coat !
Scoff not, oh ! cynic of the styles,
ie sjrious, and make the best of it.
Kofrain, ye torturers of smiles,
Nor dare to make a common jest of it.
For here’s no vulgar point to take
Concerning wig, and gown, and rest of it;
The Judge raislikes a “ gent.” to make
A lay-like, clean, and open breast of it!
According to his fastidious Honor, a lawyer who
wears no vest is indecently clad. The man-mil-
liner now gives place to the Manning-milliner.

A well-known Melb. electrician, slowly
recovering from a long and dangerous illness,
sends us the following tag, snipped by him from
an Age “In Memoriam ” advt. :
God takes the good—-too good to slay ;
God leaves the had—too bad to take away.
He says it makes him think.
* # #
One reason for the stupidity of the com-
mon policeman is said to be that ho stands so
much, so that the blood gets into his feet instead
of into his head. The natural consequence is that
he is a person of splendid hoof-development, but
yiar „
a Jjjof.
with no brains worth speaking of. What the
policeman wants is to be stood on lus head for ten
minutes every day to strengthen his intellect —
sergeants and inspectors to be up-ended for 20
minutes daily.
* * •
The other day a solicitor who, it was
alleged, had pocketed the fee, failed to turn up to
defend a man accused of a serious crime ; and the
court actually refused to grant accused even an
hour’s adjournment, although one of its own
officers had, apparently, broken a contract on the
performance of which depended, from one aspect,
the prevention of the court’s commission of the
crime of punishing an innocent man.

“WHY DON’T MEN MARRY?”
(Dedicated, without permission, to Miss Jessie
Ackermann —pronounce the name rapidly, please.)
Why don’t men marry when they can ?
Because they’re always called to rumin-
Ate upon the yacker-man.
But fail to find the yacker-woman.
# * *
A woman was imprisoned in Melbourne,
the other day, for pawning her employer’s
trousers. She was receiving 7d. a pair for making
them up. Under these conditions woman is justi-
fied in appropriating the breeches.
# * #
Another diver killed at Darnley Island.
Will some statist kindly find out how many pearls
go to a diver? It should comfort the society
beauty to know exactly what her necklace cost.
# # #
A Melbourne man has been fined a shilling
for tarring his wife with hot tar a few days back.
He asphalted her whilst she was in bed. She
escaped before he could find the feathers. That
bends evidently is not without sympathy for the
man who shows himself earnestly desirous of sub-
stituting a cheaper toilet for women.
» » •
A cushion was being raffled at the bazaar
in aid of St. Patrick’s Church, and the Mayor
threw 18 with the dice. Feeling pretty sure he
would win ho went off among the crowd. When
he returned he was told the cushion had been won
by Father Muldooney. “But,” said the Mayor,
“I threw 18.’’ “Of course ye did,” replied the
Milesian lady who conducted the raffle, “ but
didn’t his Rivcrince throw tvvinty !”
# * *
An Australian solicitor lately struck off
the roll for irregularities in the trust-moneys
department, had strongly developed tastes of a
Don Juanesque character. He maintained four
different suburban establishments with young lady
attachments at each—and he was a married man
into the bargain !
* * *
Cable tells us that the jealous London
husband who recently shot at and severely
wounded his wife ami her solicitor, when he found
them together in toe latter’s private office, has
been sentenced to 12 years’ penal servitude.
Five years for shooting the wife and the man he
supposed Jo be her paramour —seven years for
wounding a solicitor. Never hurt a limb of the
law if you hope for mercy in this world. On the
same principle, never start a crunk company with-
out having a lawyer on the board to ward off the
Crown’s vengeance.
# # #
Tennyson Smith, the temperance evan-
gelist, made a triumphal entry into Ballarat last
week. A mob of unwholesome fanatics and sly
drinkers met him at the railway-station, armed
with drums and banners. Subsequently a pro-
cession, mixed in the proportions of one lank-
haired adult to twenty-six totally abstaining small
children, marched through the streets with Tenny-
son Smith in tow. This demonstration incited at
least a couple of thousand citizens to liquor up on
the spot, but the people who never touched any-
thing before are still “off it,” so Smith’s mission
has given general satisfaction to the good cause.

“ Aged about 35, stylishly dressed, with
iD and a watch in his pockets -and yet found

* • •
The banjo-craze has fairly caught on in
Melbourne; the Block during afternoons presents
a ceaseless procession of girls carrying banjo cases.
To the uninitiated, the first impression would be—
a terrible infant mortality and a rush on baby-
coffins.
* # *
The self-assertive, positive female requires
a vote; the comparative Australian woman is
satisfied with existing conditions if she can get a
voter.
A Melb. genius has hit upon a good
anti-dun system. He rents two rooms.
On the door of one his name is painted ;
this door he always keeps locked. On a
card tacked to the door is a notice that all
messages are to be dropped into the letter-
box. In the door of the other room (on
the opposite side of the passage) is a small
peep-hole. Directly he hears anyone at
the locked door he “ takes a lunar” through
the peep-hole. Welcome visitors are re-
ceived ; duns are allowed to play on the
panels until they fall from exhaustion.
* * *
A Melb. firm the other day got 164
replies to an advt. for a junior clerk. Men
of all ages were among the applicants.
Many offered to work for from ss. to 10s. a
week, and a genius who claimed to be a
shorthand-writer and type-writer volun-
teered to work for six months without
salary. Which all goes to show to what
devilish depths of misery the Lean Man is
being driven nowadays.

bate for the Women’s Christian Temper-
ance Union Convention would bo “Bar-
maids and Female Nurses, comparatively
considered.” Which would the W.C.T.U.
vote as the more unsexed? Miss Jessie
Ackermann is invited to consider all the
points involved in this appetising problem,
and to deliver her sOul of its convictions
thereon.

A recent Yic. Civil Service bolter
made an official complaint, shortly ere he
went, that his local police-station, Caulfield,
was not sufficiently conspicuous. Caul-
field is the residence of other illustrious
personages.
A Sydney man last week broke a window
for the purpose of getting into gaol, as he was
starving and could not get work. The P.M. of
the Central gave him four months’ hard labour.
Another stole harness, purely as a thief, and pro-
tested loudly that he didn’t want to go to gaol—
and the P.M. of Redfern gave him 21 days’ hard
labour. Who will say that the moral aspect of
the deed is not considered by Sydney beak< ? All
the same, if a man is starving and wants to draw
atteirtion to his misery, he should try to refrain
from window-breaking It would be enough for
him to say to a policeman : “ Here is a stone. I
want food. Unless you arrest me I’ll break that
window.”

The latest plaint from New Australia rises
over the innate cussedness of the Paraguayan flea.
Neither steam-hammers nor pianos nor homilies
on “ true raateship” avail against him ; he cannot
be expelled by the Paraguayan police.
* * *
A select party of naval officers and gentle-
men lately made themselves most objectionable at
a suburban oyster-shop, but the “ Dago ” pro-
prietors just slung them out and handed them over
to the police. A subsequent discovery of the
identity of the prisoners resulted in their being
immediately released and no further questions
asked. The discriminative powers of the police
are wondrous keen—occasionally.

The N.S.W. Oddfellows have just made a
law fining a man 2s. Gd. for sleeping during lodge
business. 2s. Cd. for reading the newspaper, Is. for
eating, is. for interrupting a speaker, and half-a-
crown for singing an in lecent or a religious song.
In N.S.W. Parliament memlters read newspapers
with impunity, snore on benches —one lately
dropped on to the floor with a dull thud—and one
member is known to have eaten sardines from a
tin during debate.

Example is better (or worse) than pre-
cept. Since the broken Hill police shot Cousi-
dine, two alleged trespassers have been shot in
different places in N.S. Wales.
BEYOND THE BARRIER.
[For The Bulletin].
Are you tired of the South Land, comrade
The smoke and the city’s din,
And the roar of the chiding ocean
When the sobbing tide comes in ?
Would you ride to the northward, rather,
To the skirmish-posts of earth.
Where the darkest dust-storms gather
And the wildest floods have birth ?
Are you tired of the revel, comrade.
The life of folly and wine
With its one half lived in the shadow
And one half lived in shine ?
Are you tired of the poison glasses,
The lawless love and the kiss,
Out East where the brown range passes
Do you hope for dearer than this
Where the sweetest maid that ever knew
Love’s bliss and parting’s pain
Is waiting open-armed for you
Beyond the Barrier Chain ?
Let us steer to the northward, comrade,
To the Bush, with her -witching spells,
The sun-bright days and the camp-fire blaze
And the chime of the bullock-bells -
Down the long, long leagues behind us
The rain shall cover our track,
And the dust of the North shall blind us
Or ever we follow it back,
Away from the old friends, comrade,
The grasp of the strong, brown hand,
The love and the life and the laughter
That brighten the brave North Land
So long as the sunlight fills it,
So long as the red stars shine,
So long as the Master wills it
The North is your home and mine.
GLENROWAN.

Hear Bulletin,— Why is it that invariably
in the man and wife love-story, with its horrid
accessories of murder, or adultery, or suicide, or
all together, the luckless (and reckless) couple
never have any children? Instance Price Warung’s
“At the feet of his Fame,” last week, and—the
best story of the kind—Kipling’s “ At the Pit’s
Mouth.” Of-course you will answer me that Art
precludes the interpolation of children. But Art
(should be malleable. Anyway, here is the one
field for literary genius that is still untilled. Per-
sonally, I don’t think anyone could touch it with-
out making a mess of it. Undoubtedly the man
who shows (if he ever does) that he can artistically
handle a child in the tale of love, adultery,

The above is the simple addendum to a three-line
funeral notice in a Melb. daily. The unfairness
to the corpse is conspicuous. As the funeral
column of the paper is clearly for the convenience
of the corpse, the latter’s business should at least
have equal space with the undertaker’s.

Stipendiary-Magistrate Giles, who a little
while ago lost a valuable gold watch on Redfern
(Sydney) railway-station at the deft fingers of a
pickpocket, is now the “ Windeyer ” of the lower
courts when dealing with that particular class of
the community. An ounce of practice is worth a
ton of theory.

m # •
An elderly maiden lady, Miss Raymond,
has commenced a religious crusade within the
ranks of the Sydney police. Her congregations,
comprising some 24 married men, pray and sing
hymns with much zeal up to 9 p.m., when they
fall to and partake, with proportionate fervour, of
lams, scones, tea, coffee, milk, and so on. A
suburban sergeant and a policeman from the
Mint pray like practised parsons to their sinful
brothers, and it is hoped that their consoling
words will have the effect of bringing them all
fewer “cases.” Single policemen do not attend..
Tile rascals are probably banging upon the con-
soling words (and pie) of other (and younger)
maiden ladies.
“ London ” is the name of n small port in
Westralia, about 90 miles from the goldfield of
Marble Bar. There is a London in Canada, and
it is said that there is one somewhere in a place
called England.
A Melbourne paper reminds its readers
that the Mayors of Leicester (Eug.) used to be
chosen by a pig. The proceeding is often reversed
in Australia—the hog is chosen.
* * #
A Melbourne trooper, not long ago, found
a pin stuck up to the head in his horse’s haunch.
This had been done while backing into a crowd.

In a recent Sydney
fire scare, one youth tried
to leap from a window
30ft. from the ground,
and a frantic mother
threw her baby from the
gallery into the hall,
luckily withouthurt. You
never realise the utter
idiocy at the bottom of
human nature until it is
displayed in all its naked-
ness at a fire. What a
Bedlam hell must be !

silk umbrella and a grievance.”
“ To tell you the truth, I remember your
name perfectly,” said Oscar Wilde to an effusive
person who grasped his hand in the street, “ hut
for the life of me I can’t recollect your face.”
Sydney University Prof. Macallum’s book
on Arthurian idylls gets scant mercy from English
critics, who dub it a heterogeneous hash-up of
other men’s writings, darkened by the rushlight
of the professorial intelligence. Why will Aus-
tralian university men persist in plodding the
beaten path of letters, gleaning fields which hun-
dreds have reaped before them, while the new
problems of a new continent await the touch of
the master to thrill into literary life ? Perhaps
for the same reason that books published in
Australia fall flat, while anything sent to England
and returned with the stamp of a London publisher
is eagerly bought up. So far, the chief charac-
teristic of the Australian reader (yes, and writer)
is the absence of a mind of Ins own.
John Mat tin, a boy of 14, has been dis-
tinguishing himself in Adelaide. Fourteen times
lias he escaped from the local reformatory. The
last time he was hobbled to a bed in the third
storey, but he broke loose and, still hobbled,
climbed out of the window, went hand-over-hand
along a gutter 40ft. from the ground to the angle
of the building, and then slipped along the down-
pipe to the earth. He robbed a neighbouring
dwelling because, as he told the Judge, he was
cruelly treated at the reformatory arid wanted to
be sent somewhere else. Justice B'mdey, a kindly
yachtsman, declined to send Martin to gaol, but
handed him over to a gentleman who has more
than once before fashioned good citizens out of
alleged incorrigible lads. Some day Martin may
be either Premier of the province or leader of
the S. A. troops in their expedition to annex the
Barrier or Coolgardie.

There’s nothing that’s half as bright
As the things that we can’t remember
That we thought of in bed last night.

HUMANITY.
Sae far enough to guess infinity:
Then heavy night falls round.
Love true enough to wish eternity:
Then the life snaps—o’erwound.
Muse deep enough to doubt the Deity:
Then die, no answer found.
A GOOD STOVE.
A WORD OF CAUTION.
Everyone know's what a good stove is—at least,
the women do ; and every woman knows that, of
all the treasures of the household, a really first-
rate, reliable cooking-stove is the greatest desider-
atum in a house. A great many stoves are
advertised every year, many of which are like
jerry-built houses—thrown together to sell. A
word of caution is therefore necessary to intending
buyers, because tiiere are few things so annoying
and vexatious as to buy a stove and then find that
it d >es not possess all the qualities that are neces-
sary to give complete and lasting satisfaction.
Now, the points in choosing a good stove are: —
1. Strong cast-iron frame.
2. Good draught,
3. Facilities to burn either wood, coal, or
even turf.
4. Simplicity of parts, and thus, easy to clean.
5. Duplicate parts kept in stock, so that
bieakages can be replaced at small cost
and without delay.
And if, in addition to these five points, you can
get a stove that is also an ornament to the kitchen,
all the better.
Price, of course, is a point, but that is a matter
which need scarcely be mentioned, since, if the
above points are combined in any stove, it would
be cheap at any price within reason.
It only remains for us to say that, with con-
fidence, we can honestly recommend “The
Housewife,” sold by Holds worth, Macpherson
and Co., 250 George-street, Sydney, from £3 10s.
upwards, as one that fully and entirely “ fills the
bill.”
We should suggest our readers writing to this
firm for an illustrated catalogue, with further
iparticulars.
MOI ET TOI-TOI ET MOI.
All that 1 am is thine
My body, my heart, my soul ;
Thou fillest my life as wine
Filleth a white glass howl:
Colourless, cold and thin,
But for the wine therein.
Can I say more to thee ?
Nay, there is naught to be said
Thine am I, living or dead.
Thou art the All for me ;
Take but thy love away
1 am but dead, cold clay.
Fear not, my love hinds thee,
What is the earth to the sun ?
Is it his care that the sea
Lies dark when the day is done ?
Leave me, thou art most free ;
What is my love to thee ?
Upper Hunter.
A.B.
