The appeal in the somewhat celebrated
“Ananias” libel-case has been decided by' the
Privy Council in favour of the Australian Star
against Sydney E. News. The total costs will be
a couple of thousand at least.
When the Russian bridegroom is led into
the bridal chamber, he orders his wife out of bed
to pull off his boots, which she does as a token
of submission. In one of the boots is hidden a
whip ;in the other a trinket. If she draws off the
latter first it is considered a good omen, but if, by
bad luck, she chance to pull the former first, she
gets a stroke of the whip. Let us hope that on
her wedding night, at least, it is a light stroke. —
Pall Mall Gazette.
According to official records, there were in
the last nine years above 250 murders committed
in San Francisco, and only six white murderers
hanged. In the year ended June last, there were
30 murders, 11 cases of manslaughter, and 281
assaults with deadly weapons—and not a solitary
conviction for any of these offences ! In the whole
world there is no other city, having laws and
courts and calling itself civilised, that can show in
its history for 1893 such a page,
“ Let me give you my dying advice,” said
Rufus Choate, the acute American counsel, “never
cross-examine a woman. It is of no use. They
can not disintegrate the story they have once told
they cannot eliminate the part that is for you from
that which is against you. They can neither com-
bine, nor shade, nor qualify. They go for the-
whole thing, and the moment you begin to cross-
examine one of them, instead of being bitte nby a
single rattlesnake, you are bitten by a whole-
barrelful. I never, excepting in a case absolutely
desperate, dared to cross-examine a woman.”
Sand, village settlements and salvation
are the leading features in the landscape of the
lately-flooded districts of E. Gippsland. The
sand has settled on the recently submerged hop
and maize fields inches deep and the good soil has
been swept away to help silt up the lakes. Nine
acres of hop poles all bent at an angle of 45deg., as
they were left by the flood, is a depressing sight
on the Tam bo bank near Bruthen. An idea of
the damage done may be judged from this, that
Millionaire (will this soon be a misnomer?) Clarke
offers the whole of his Orbost (Snowy River) pro-
perty at £3 ss. an acre which before the flood was
worth £l5 or £2O.

The N.S.W. Labour-party “ outside ” are
throwing away what they regard as the dirty
water before they get the clean. In planning
Melville’s defeat in Northumberland they are
arranging for the return of a Property-man. In
trying to oust G. D. Clark, at Leichhardt, they
are preparing a welcome, not for their own candi-
date, but for some ass-nominated alderman who
will slip in between. And so on in a variety of
places.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The 360 Melb. cyclists who lately met and pro-
cessed, partly to show off their calves and partly as a
protest against the £5 duty on imported bicycles, did not
oonstitutea “remarkable Freetrade demonstration.” The
Victorian bicycle-tax isn’t a protective impost, for there is
in Victoria no local bicycle industry to protect; it is a
revenue duty pure and simple, of the good old Freetrade
pattern. The demonstrators simply wanted this Free-
trade impost shifted on to someone else. They were not
Freetraders, but merely 360 egotists with abnormally
developed legs, each demanding that the tax should be
removed from his particular form of nuisance for his own
particular convenience, and they had just as much
political significance as a procession of 860 dogs pro-
testing against the dog-tax, or 860 dingoes howling at
midnight against the State-purchase of dog-scalps.
* * *
The so-called “ Betting Houses Suppression
Act ”is administered most lopsidedly in Sydney. Nearly
every week little men who lay shilling doubles are fined,
and the shops of big fielders who do not bet to less than
half-sov. pasted by. Why ?

A Sydney stable-boy cheerfully admitted in
court last week that he had committed perjury. “Oh, it
wasn’t true what I said before; only the boss asked me
to swear it, you know.” For of such is the turf.
• * * *
Pedestrianism is reviving in Victoria, but it
must soon decline, for the peds, unwarned by the past,
are up to all the old games, and practise them in the most
barefaced fashion.
* * * *
G. Meadham has won the 1000 yards swimming
championship of N.S.W. in 14min. 43 2-ssecs., thus
beating W. J. Gormley’s record.
* * » »
Owing to the secession of Humphrey Oxenham
and other prominent men, that once powerful body, the
Australian Bookmakers’ Association, is likely to soon
collapse.
* * » ♦
As with pedestrianism, sculling, and boxing in
bygone days, pony-racing in Sydney is being done to
death. The N.S.W. Pony and Galloway Association will
shortly confer with the A.J.C. for the purpose of limiting
the number of racing days. Even those who live on the
game cannot tell where the money comes from.
* • * *
George Lohmann, so far from intending to
“settle” in Australia, hopes to get back to England for
the ’95 cricket season. Unless he is overestimating the
improvement in his health, we may find Lohmann playing
here with Stoddart’s Eleven at the end of the present
year. The story that he would settle in Australia for the
benefit of his health was decidedly ominous, and everyone
who knows this brilliant cricketer and bright, good fellow
will be glad to hear that his lungs are not such a bad case
after all.
♦ * * •
Journalist Scott, the party responsible for the
attack on Hayes, stated that he has never paused to con-
sider whether he is a smart writer, or not, inasmuch as
“ we don’t need to put smart things in the Australasian.”
The latter portion of the statement was unnecessary, but
it afforded another illustration of the pretentiousness of
heavy newspaper writing. “We” are dull on principle,
although “ we ” could reel off brilliant copy if the editor
desired it. As a matter of fact, every journalist has tried
to be smart, at some time or other, and the more utter
his failure the more virulent his avowed contempt lor
brighter men.
* * • •
The explanation of Titan’s vagaries was inside
of him, as with many another horse who gets a character
for “roguery.” That Titan didn’t die before he was
worked back into form speaks highly for Scobie’s careful
treatment of him.
* * * *
It is understood that the hushed-up cross-exam-
ination of a witness in the sporting libel-case last week
related to the old-time troubles of a certain literary per-
son. The hushing-up could scarcely have been done on
behalf of this notability, seeing that Christie Murray’s
small domestic scandals were being announced in large
type about the same time. No, The Bulletin holds the
press guiltless of any desire to spare the feelings of its
own people when their peccadilloes make material for
“copy.”
* * ♦ ♦
The publication, during the notorious turf libel-
case in Melbourne, of the fact that a boy-jockey, not yet
old enough to bring a legal suit in his own proper person
(such as it is), had, in three years, earned £5OOO by riding,
and had won another £5OOO by betting, will do more to
promote turf-madness than all the shocking suicides,
failures, gaolinga, and whatnot, with all the denunciatory
sermons added, can undo in a generation. For a hobble-
dehoy to “ make ” £lO,OOO in three years ia an Aladdin’s
lamp to lure to destruction the already over-sporting
Australian imagination.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
The evidence of Mr. Claude Scott, aged 23, at
the Hayes v. Australasian trial, reads as a solemn warn-
ing to embryo turfites. Beginning while an articled
clerk in ’9O, he arranged, he says, with Jockey Hayes to
pay him 10 per cent, of his winnings resulting from Hayes’
ups. Tiring of such uninteresting sport, he became, in
’92, the owner of Baltic, with which nag he and Hayes,
principally Hayes, won considerably. Once, he alleges,
Hayes said, “If we back Baltic (Hayes’mount) he may
win, but if we back Auster we shall, as only the two can
possibly win." This he agreed to, and Baltic conse-
quently lost. Another time Hayes was riding Stone-
broke, a later purchase, and they backed Bridegroom.
At the last jump Stonebroke was leading, but after
what Scott describes as “a superb piece of horsemanship
by Hayes" Bridegroom passed the judge first, and they
won their money. Mr. Scott severed his connection with
racingowingtoilayes“takinghimdown” with Baltic, since
which time he has ceased to be an owner. He still con-
tinues, however, he states, to evince a lively interest in
the turf. Whether the evidence is believed or not, it
jives a very beautiful idea of turf morality, and should
be read with care by all ambitious young sportsmen.

Dear Bulletin,—ln illustration of your corres-
pondent’s remarks re “knack" feats of strength, I nsod
to be able to raise at arm’s-length, by the lower fro it
“ rung,” a chair which men of twice my strength couldn’t
get clear of the floor to save their lives. I could no more
understand this than they could, until, after a hand-
hardening spell of existence, I found that, though actually
stronger, I couldn’t lift the chair ; the reason being that
the thickening of the palm-skin no longer allowed it to be
squeezed into such a ridge as had formerly prevented the
rung from slipping round in the grasp.—A.

Australian jockey Clack is back from India. He
tells of some out-of-the-way experiences, including the
taking of a couple of horses to Cabul as a present to the
Ameer from one of the Beresfords. “ India’s a different
sort o’ place to the Sydney side,'chaps,” he tells his
cronies. “ It’s a perlite sort o’ a place. There’s none o'
yer bloo-me and hli-me (here. It’s all ‘Yes, me Lord,’
and ‘Cert’n’-y, Sir Charles;’ and the toffs ’ud be just
’orrified if you put a bit on yer own fancy when you were
ridin’.’’
* ♦ * *
There’s a legend that the Shah of Persia’s race-
horses cannot be be den now, as the owners of those
horses who formerly beat his heads chopped off.
The Shah’s example is being followed in Australia. A
wealthy man practically owns a racehorse up North,
also a racing stable, and a judge and starter. One
recent day a horse of his was adjudged winner of one
race, although 99 men out of 100 would swear he was
beaten by a head. In another race the Shah’s horse got
so far away at the start that there could be no question as
to his winning, and in yet another case the Shah’s horse
was given a dead-heat for first place, when to all unin-
terested observers the third horse won by half a length.
Horse-racing is indeed a noble sport.

been powerless to bully that howling mob of infuriat d
pencillers.

project fell through.

Tom Sullivan, the erst M.L. sculler, is doing
well for himself in England, says The Bulletin’s corn s
pondent. He has got a nice pub. at Hammersmith, and
appears to have a good business. But, better than this,
he is about to secure an exceedingly nice girl for a w ife, a
Miss Rough, daughter of the well-known Oxford boat-
builder, who will give Tom a fit-out of boats for his slu d
connection with the pub. He has a staunch admirer in
in “ Billy ” Innes, who thinks highly of him as a sculler
and as a man. Any stake-money needed will not be
wanting.

Prynne’s ghost: As a moralist, I have been
asking myself whether it does the Vic. Government any
good to be racing-entrepreneurs. Here they are, making
a fuss over such a little go as a building society lottery,
which Purves declares to be a necessity, while they
swallow the camel, by being the actual managers of the
Melb. Cup lottery. What would it be sans the railways?
* * * *
At a recent Vic. race-meeting, bookmakers re-
fused to bet on the ground that there were not enough
“ triers,” and no backers for the stiff ’uns. One is apt
to forget that the “ books ” want what the public want-
large fields and active competition among owners. In
fact, the blasphemous Hebrew is, tor business reasons, in
favour of Turf morality—and steady profits.

Dear Bulletin,—Besides forgetting that Greek
boxing was for glory, not gate-money ! and that the
metal-bossed cestus was practically a deadly weapon,
“M‘G.” falls into the mistake of taking extreme cases.
The well-supported contention that the professional
pugilist is not necessarily remarkable for courage or
manliness, commits nobody to an admiration of the spat,
equipped dude ; the one animal, like the other, suffer s
simply from lack of intellect. The best man, theoreti.
cally, is the man who best combines intellectual and
physical efficiency, and there is no ground for assuming
that anybody who disapproves of prize-fighting as an
institution is not, like the present writer, a fairly good
Man of ms Hands.
» » * *
There is one race for which everybody enters—
the human race. It’s open to all—white, red, black or
yellow—born in the usual way ; the distance is four-score
and ten yards ; the prize is a promissory note payable in
another world; the judge is the Deity; the clerk of course,
who whips you up, is religion; a good start is the pro-
verbial * silver spoon;’ the applause which greets the
winner is green and happy old age ; the desire to make a
pot by losing is temptation ; Old Nick is the bookie who
tempts you ; the ped., who runs “stiff” for a longer start,
is the foolish virgin, and the handicapper is that despotic
character “class."

between Marvel, with Kelso up, and Gough, on Lady
Trenton, was something to be remembered, as they
battled it out from the Leger Stand to the winning-post,
Gough fairly lifting the daughter of Black Swan in a
winner by a foot or so.

The Dibbs Ministry should have mercy on
the two wretched aborigines under death-sentence
at Albury—if only because Australian black-
fellows are getting scarce, and it might be as well
to keep two or three to breed a dynasty from
when this country becomes a monarchy.

The Pastoralists’ Federal Council have
reduced the rates for machine shearing in N.S.W.
from £os. to 17s. per 100, except in the West, on
the ground that if 20s. be the standard wage for
hand-shorn sheep a man with a machine can earn
the standard with the same labour at the reduced
rate. The 20s. rate for hand-shears is maintained
except in Southern Riverina, where it is said an
18s. i ate'has been customary and will be main-
tained. The shearers’ representatives will pro-
bably meet to discuss the matter.
The new One-man-one-vote Bill before
the British Parliament will enfranchise 600,000
new electors. That should lift the democratic
cause along. And think of the indirect gam
through the abolition of the plural vote, so enor-
mously increasing the comparative weight of the
Radical factor! J.dumchere’s opposition to f)he
Rosebery leadership is tight in principle, built
will be a pity if such antagonism is pushed so lar
as to mar the noble opportunities for reform which
now open before the British democracy.
The New City of Manoa.
When the Spaniards finally achieved the conquest
of Peru they were led to believe that the greater
pint of the gold and jewels had been hidden by
the conquered. The survivors were subjected to
awful tortures as a persuasive to confession, but
the treasure was never found. From that time
daring adventurers roamed the Spanish main in
searcli of golden cities, and stores of fabulous
wealth were freely circulated. The most common
of these was told of the city of Manoa, a mythical
place paved with gold and supposed to be situated
in South Eastern Mexico. Since that time to the
present the daring men of all the nations of the
earth have been chasing a golden city in every
quarter of the globe. Ballarat, Bendigo, the
Thames, Charters Towers, Sud-Est, Croydon and
Bingera have been the recent Eldqrados, and now
from Western Australia come stories of gold held
together by quartz, and the fortune seeking ar-
gonaut of Australia is convinced from the news-
paper reports that the real new city of Manoa is
to bo approached from the dry claypans of Oool-
gardie. And who shall say he will not earn his
city even if he does not achieve it? We of the
settled states who eat three meals a day and
consider ourselves martyred if we are unable to
have ice in the torrid seasons cannot imagine the
sufferings of the gold seeker in the extremities of
hunger and thirst. The impure water, the
irregular life, the dyspeptic diet and other punish-
ments induce a list of troubles of which liver
disorganisation is the mildest and Bright’s disease
the strongest form. These diseases and their
various phases are absolutely curable by Warner’s
Safe Cure, and, as far as we know, by no other
medicine.
Richard J. Haynes, Esq., Barrister, Howick
St., Perth, W.A. writes thus; —“I have used
Warner’s Safe Cure and can strongly recommend
it to any person troubled with affections of the
liver. Indeed, in a hob climate, where one is
liable to suffer from the various forms of liver
complaint, no better remedy can be found. Apart
from my own experience, I know of several persons
in Perth who were constant sufferers, and who
have been relieved and restored to health by the
use of Warner’s Safe Cure.” _
This medicine is the specific for_ the dweller*
alike in the city and the bush in the case of
Bright’s disease, gravel, bladder, kidney and liver
troubles, female complaint*, &o.

Dodge discovered by the police at a Melb.
sale-yard. Cows for sale had their teats covered
with a thick coating of paste of kidney-fat and
glue to overstock the udders and give the cow the
appearance of being a great milker.

action,” are on the walls. Five years ago the idea

CITY MUTUAL LIFE OFFICES, HUNTER, BLIGH AND CASTLEREAGH STREETS, SYDNEY.
Some papers predict that Chapman, a
popular municipal magnate, will beat Toby Barton
at N.S.W. general election. It is hard to believe
this as Mr. Chapman, who was born a grocer,
was’probably intended by God to remain a grocer.
Commissioner Coombs, and half-a-dozen
other Salvationist officers, have shipped from
Melbourne for London, in the Oroya, all first-
class saloon. Formerly the rule was for the
Harmy to travel third-class, then they gob to
second. The starving officers left in Australia
must water at the mouth while the bosses wallow
on the cushions.
With deep regret The Bulletin hears of
the death, at Sydney, of Mrs. J. Medway Day,
the amiable and popular wife of the no less
respected publicist of that name. Mrs. Day died
after a long and hopeless struggle with disease,
leaving behind her countless friends in all the
provinces, who will sincerely sympathise with her
devoted husband in his affliction.
• “Thiscamel,” remarked aKurrachee driver
to the S.M. Herald special in Western N.S.W.,
the other day, “has travelled from here to
Wanaaring, li3 miles, in one day. He can start

Another thing, there are over 300 families (not people)
living on the ground that was given to us. You can see
a house every way you look almost—all peaceful and
civilised natives of the country—with nice little groves
of orange trees around each and an acre or two under
cultivation, and a few head of cattle, horses and sheep. I
believe they are to be shifted off to another place, there
to start afresh to build up homes again and orange groves
(with those they have twenty and thirty years old), to
make room for us ; but there are a lot of us against it.

Now whispered that Gladstone didn’t want
to resign, that he didn’t mean to resign, and that
he only did resign because he thought he wouldn’t
be taken at his word. He anticipated that a
weeping country and a shrieking party would
kneel and implore him to take it all back. Bub
the party was only glad to get rid of him, and the

the mortification of reading in the newspapers
that the Government is really stronger for his
withdrawal.
Last year’s financial crisis has perhaps
more than anything else brought the life offices
of these provinces prominently before the public.
The manner in which they have stood by their
policyholders merits all commendation. In the
van stood that sterling little Sydney institution,
The City Mutual Life, which, during the past five
years, has by the application of energy and
economy, assisted by more than the average busi-
ness ability, steadily emerged from obscurity
into the front rank. Mr. Crowley seized the
opportunity to notify his policyholders that
those of them affected by the then prevailing
suspensions could on application have the grace
on their policies extended without charge, and the
act, trifling as it was, bore fruit a thousand-fold,
The City Mutual did not hesitate either to pub
its hand pretty deeply into its pocket to assist its
needy policyholders in a practical way, and the
crowning act was the intimation, some six months
ago, through every paper in tne colony, that any
rneinber who had paid three years premiums
could obtain a fully-paid policy equal to the whole
amount of premiums paid with compound interest
added. Such treatment goes far to justify the
rather ambitious title which Mr. Crowley has
patented for his office—the most liberal and pro-
gressive Life office m Australia, ihe Society
removed into its handsome new building—of
which we give a pioture-ab the intersection
of Hunter, Bligh and Gastlereagh Streets m
J anuarv last.
Whether the act was due to temper or
strength, Mr. Gladstone praetically
thrown up the sponge m the great fight and left
victory with the Lords. He has acknowledged
defeat, and the scene which has followed reminds
one forcibly of the events which succeeded the
defeat of Jem Smith, once champion of England,
by the famous black pugilist, Peter Jackson,
That was a scene with a moral. Up to the hour
of combat Jem Smith had been the object of an
amount of hero-worship which a victorious general
might have envied. High and low, rich and poor,
gentle and simple, gathered around him and gazed
on him with admiration, and he strode about
ine Pelican Club attended by marquises and
millionaires. When he left the ring a
defeated man and- came presently to the
bar, he was allowed to stand there alone,
and he rather loudly expressed his opinion of
the change of attitude assumed by the members
towards him. Up to that moment the boots in
which he fought Kilrain had been exhibited in a
glass case in a place of honour on the club pre-
mises, and it was stated on apparently good
authority that many of the younger members of
the club would stand before those boots occasion-
ally in an attitude of silent prayer. That night
the boots of Jem Smith mysteriously disappeared.
It was rumoured that they had been flung out into
the street. Is the political champion of England
about to share the fate of the pugilistic champion ?
Will the National Liberal Club hurl Mr. Glad-
stone’s portrait out of the window ?—London
Referee.

The larrikin of Australian towns and cities beats the
hoodlum of California to the ropes. Nothing is too bad
too bold, too infamous for him. A dozen of them will
hold a town in subjection. But his common drink is a
cup of tea or coffee. Only when anything is to be done
which demands a cooler head and steadier hand than
usual—some murder or outrage too shameful for record-
then ha likes a glass of—lemonade.

that liquor-prohibition will breed a criminal and
degraded race, in ’Frisco News Letter of Jan. 27
last. The larrikin should feel flattered i

If Chamberlain had remained a Radical,
he and nob Rosebery would be Premier of England
to-day. As it is, there is talk of a coalition be-
tween the Liberal Unionists and the Conserva-
tives, with Joseph at the head, and an Earldom
of Birmingham in the distance; but it is doubtful
if the Conservatives who admire, while they dis-
trust him, will ever range themselves willingly
under his banner. There is no question that his
is the most brilliant head in the English political
arena. His speeches are the ecstasy of his friends,
the despair of his enemies. _ No man living is his
match for clear, cold, luminous intellect as a de-
hater. Compared with him Gladstone is ponder-
ous, Balfour fantastical, Roseoery dull. But—
his coat is turned.

There are three endings repulsive to a
man of to-day—suicide, the priesthood and mar-
riage. Suicide shows a lack of cuncsity which
shocks me; the one thing horrible in death is that
the curtain falls suddenly before the piece is
played out. The priesthood is a mere half-
measure: a suicide of the reason, with the flesh
left exposed to every natural vertigo. But mar-
riage is assuredly the most hypocritical of the
three solutions. As though fidelity to one woman
were not as difficult to observe as chastity to all
women I ”—Huguks Lb Roux.

The Victorian Bar.
The end, or pause, of the most important case
which has yet come before the Victorian, if not
the Australian Law Courts, may stimulate a few
reminiscences on the manner in which the legal
profession has grown. The Old Court, for many
years, was ornamented with the large painting of
Chief Justice A’Beckett, in his scarlet robes, pre-
sented by Mr. Dennistoun Wood, There are a
few yet active practitioners who remember the
first Chief Justice —A’Beckett, one being Mr.
Frank Stephen, the father-in-law of Chief Justice
Madden. Clark was the leading Melbourne
attorney of 40 to 50 years ago, and had the repute
of immense wealth. Other good old crusty
names are those of Muttlebury, Duerdin and
Bronckhorst, while the most able of all has lately
disappeared in Mr. Malleson. Messrs. Klingender
and Attenborough are reminders of the times
when squatting and mining, in an inchoate state
of the law, combined with banking, to make the
legal staple.
Chief Justice Stawell lives in memory. The
writer must be allowed to set down an individual
opinion that Stawell was not in the very first line
as a Judge, and even to say the same of Chief
Justice Higinbotham, a yet more eminent man.
Stawell had too much temper, combined with too
vivid a conscience, speaking in a legal way. His
somewhat mischievous principle appeared to be to
fix the rights and wrongs of the case in his mind
(as the police fix theories), and then endeavour to
torture the law to meet it, a system to which may
be traced some of his defeats before the Privy
Council.
Molesworth, a supremely-able lawyer, was the
best Judge of a long-associated quartette, the
others being Stawell, Barry, and Williams. His
dreadful fist in the written judgments—the spider
crawlings out of the inkbottle, on little squares of
blue foolscap tied with blue ribbon —is still a
recollection.
That magnificent courtier, Judge Redmond
Barry, is destined to a kind of immortality, not
from his legal acquirements, but through his con-
nection with the University and Public Library,
the germ of which was a lending library nobly and
freely established by him, in his own house at West
Melbourne. It is understood that Barry was
annoyed by Stawell’s jumping the claim of the
Chief Justiceship, from Attorney-General. How-
ever this may be, their relations became only too
harmonious. Barry, in short, allowed Stawell to
do as much work as he liked, which amounted to
nearly all, in the highest branches, on appeals to
the Full Court. ' The first Judge Williams was a
most easy-going man, too, though wrongly stigma
tised as lazy. He would sit, in Full Court, coiled
up in his chair for hours, saying nothing, and
then come out with just one luminous remark,
which shot the light into the very heart and core
of the case.
What golden memories cluster round the Old
Court ! Speight v. Syme may obliterate its record
on the civil side, but it boasts the immortal Eureka
tria’s, and a criminal case one may hope never to
see paralleled—Ned Kelly’s. The Dick Ireland
style of barrister has departed altogether, but how
he used to irradiate the scene with his malicious
humour, and what an impudent fashion he had of
leering at the jury, having his spectacles pushed
up on his forehead, over his black, beetling brows,
while his bronzed visage exuded, as it were, oil
all over. The one word “ blarney ” would sum
him up.
Court frequenters, professionals, usually held
Aspinall to be an over-rated man. “ The style is
the man ” may be a false general maxim, but it
accurately measured Aspinall. He had the
most fascinating ways conceivable in a barrister
wherever he had to plead some cause of humanity.
Then there were the rich tissues of his voice, arid
his scintillant, superficial, but eminently taking
wit.
Mr. Fellows is still the standard, as the fore-
most member of the Victorian Bar, past or present.
He had his limitations, and carried no particular
weight with an average jury, but the judges were
under his thumb in Banco, with an implicit trust
that he would never mislead the Court. Fellows
was a tall, burly, chubby, rather rosy-gilled,
almost clean-shaven man, with the look of a high
comedian, ever a smile curling up his somewhat
Tartuffian mouth, which seemed particularly cut
for the reception of his favourite oysters and
stout.
Mr. Dawson wrought to the fore, though he
had a voice nearly as squeaky as Gavan Duffy’s.
He was the brow-beater of his day, a plunger,
desperately tenacious. Suave Mr. Billing was
never a really strong man at the Bar. Indeed, the
reporters would usually tip either Dawson or
Billing for the losing side.
Sir Archibald Michie is Marius amid the ruins
of Carthage. We conjure him up in a social case,
which it would be painful to outline distinctly.
He was the advocate of a lady plaintiff, of unim-
peachable character, in an action for assault of the
most delicate nature. The other side won—by
the arts of ridicule, after Michie had exhausted
all the resources of chivalry. This case is probably
more deeply burnt into his memory than any other
throughout his career.
The conscientious, ecclesiastical, old-maidish
Wilberforce Stephen, and the strenuous Webb,
were the happiest of associates in Equity, where
Holroyd would usually plod on the other ?ide,
supported, perhaps, by the rather fluffy Bunny.
If the writer may again insinuate his private
opinion, Holroyd’s is about the most judicial mind
Melbourne has seen.
Putting back the wheel of time for just over
twenty years, we do not find Madden and Purves
giving undoubted promise of the distinction they
achieved, as dividing the Bar leadership. Both
forged ahead slowly at first. The Chief Justice’s
triumph is the fruit of prodigious assiduity, allied
with his very pleasing address and a mellifluous
oratorio flow, in which he happens to stand alone
among his contemporaries, somewhat recalling
Mr. Higinbothara, in a grace which is purely
Hibernian.
Mr. Purves, far superior to Dawson, has yet
built more on Dawson’s lines than on those of any
barrister one recalls —Dawson with an infusion of
the acrid Martley. Purves admits (like Brougham)
his deficiency in law, and perhaps his German
education had something to do with it. Many
years ago he said, in Court, “My friend, Mr.
(Hartley) Williams, will be a Judge, but I never
will.”
The stamp of the barrister has changed, as with
the cricketer —and someone also tries to show that
crack racehorses have entirely altered in appear-
ance, being now mule-like, with heavy, lumpy,
slabby necks and big fiddle-heads. It is never
safe to be absolute, in a comparison of past with
present. The barrister, like the actor, has become
more natural. People can less easily be hum-
bugged—or oven bullied —than of yore, and there
is some ground for Bismarck’s contention that
oratory will disappear with ignorance. Ways
alter, but the ability remains pretty much the
same ; wherefore, with confidence, one pits a
Madden against an Ireland, a Purves against an
Aspinall, a Hodges against a Michie—although
Fellows will for ever stand out as the lustrous
pleader. There are many others who could be
written of, including Bindon, Atkins, Sewell and
Chapman, but this article is merely a dip into the
past with an eye to such reputations as have kept
alive.
“IMITATION
is the
SINCEREST FLATTERY.”
The articles most imitated are
CLEMENTS TONIC and
FLETCHER’S PILLS.
WHY?
BECAUSE they have the best reputation and
are most in demand by the public, consequently,
every dishonest trader tries to trade on their
fame and renown.
It is really wonderful that the Australian people
are so slow to see the truth as regards the un-
scrupulous quacks and charlatans who prey on the
credulity of the Australian public. Scarcely a
week ever passes but what the press exposes the
barefaced and shameful practices of some of these
harpies, yet in face of all they seem to thrive,
and the paper who hounds them down most thrives
fattest on their advertisements and cracks cham-
pagne over each balance-sheet. Clements Tonic
and Fletcher’s Pills have never yet stooped to
deception and misrepresentation. They are
scientific therapeutical agents, and evidence as to
their value can be found in every district,
and such being the case it is unnecessary to
further extol their virtues.
The Wool Market.
SYDNEY V. LONDON.
Some years ago Sydney wool sales were frequented
by numerous speculators, who purchased here and
simply shipped to London, on the strength of the
ample margin then existing between the two
markets. Gradually that margin has narrowed
down, until at present not one man is brave
enough to risk a speculation purchase, unless with
an odd lot of greasy, or a parcel of well-got-up
scoured.
This fact proves beyond question the advantage
growers gain in placing their wool on the Sydney
market and selling for the best price obtainable.
Representatives from all the manufacturing
centres of the world regularly attend the Sydney
sales, and, from the quantity they buy and ship
direct to the various ports of England, Japan, and
the Continent, they are enabled to make highly
satisfactory freight arrangements, and conse-
quently can afford to bid at our sales to the
highest possible value.
Several lots of wool were offered in Sydney last
season and “passed in’’ and shipped to London,
where they were knocked down for the same price
offered here. Add shipping charges, &c., and it
is evident the motto for the wool producer is
“ sell in Sydney.” Invariably the sequence will
shew him in a position to pat himself on the back.
The young and successful firm of M'Burney,
Wallis and Co. have introduced into their busi-
ness all the new methods which commercial
experience has proved safe and valuable. From
none can the wool-grower get more consideration
and attention to his special interests coupled with
the knowledge of experts in all branches of the
trade.

Objects of the Company.
This Company is being formed to supply a want
which is very keenly felt at the present time,, and
which will become intensified during the next few
months.
It is proposed to erect a complete Ore crushing
and Assay Flint replete with all the latest im-
provements for the treatment of Auriferous Quartz
of every description, and thus to supply a most
pressing want for the Coolgardie and adjacent
Goldfields.
The boon will more particularly be felt by those
owners who are individually working claims, and
who for lack of Capital or other causes are unable
to run a Battery themselves.
In this connection is is proposed to erect Crush-
ing Plants on approved Mines in exchange for a
percentage of the yield of Gold. 'Phis will un-
doubtedly commend itself to Mine Owners, and
with a minimum of expense will very largely
augment the Revenue of this Company.
Site.
The site for the Central Plant and Assay Offic 6
will be the most eligible that can bo secured aite r
a thorough examination of the ground, and as fa r
as possible to suit the convenience of Mine Owners.
Water.
It has now been practically demonstrated that
an ample supply of water for Battery purposes
may be secured at a depth of about 200 feet, and
this is altogether apart from conservation by rain-
fall.
Engineer,
It happens most opportunely for the success i f
this Company that Mr. W. A. Mercer, of the well-
known firm of Messrs. Bewick, Moreing & Co.,
of London, is willing to act as Consulting
Engineer, and will superintend the erection of the
Company’s Machinery, and to his matured judg-
ment may safely be left the securing of a Battery-
site at Coolgardie and the selection of any proper-
ties which may be brought to the notice of the
Directors of the Company, with a view to the
erection of machinery.
Cost of Plant.
It is estimated that a Central Plant, inclusive
of an Assay Office at Coolgardie, as well as four
smaller Plants at outlying mines, could he erected,
notwithstanding the present heavy freights, for an
outlay not exceeding £BOOO, thus leaving £2OOO,
less expenses of flotation and usual Brokerage
charges, to the credit of the Company.
Batteries aggregating, say, 40 Heads would
crush 300 tons per week as a minimum, thus—
-300 tons @ 15s. per ton —£223 per week, or
£ Li,700 per annum. '
Estimated Revenue.
Expenditure.
(The Consulting Engineer’s fees to be settled
hereafter.)
Thus yielding a net revenue of over £6OOO per
annum, or more than 50 per cent, on the entire
('ajita. of the Company.
Figures under rather than over estimated.
I t will be observed that the charge for treating
t he Ores, 15s. per ton, has been assessed at a very
low figure, and it may be mentioned that on the
Murchison Field, where water is said to be plenti-
ful, no less than £3 per ton is being charged.
Further, in calculating the expenditure care has
been taken to place figures at a high ratio, and it
may therefore fairly be assumed that the Revenue
will be augmented, and the expenditure consider-
ably reduced.
Not a Speculation.
Looking at the fact that the permanency of the
Coolgardie Goldfields is now assured, and that
water for Battery purposes may bo secured by
sinking, quite apart from what may be conserved
by' rainfall, the project now brought before the
public may be considered removed from the region
of speculation, and may be viewed as a first-class
and highly remunerative investment.
Early Application for Shares Necessary.
A large number of shares will certainly be
secured by the Capitalists in the Eastern colonies,
who are interested in the Ooolgardie Mines, as
well as by those living on the fields, and as it is
desired, as far as possible, to make this a local
Company, it is requested that early application
may be made for shares.

They talk in Victoria now of opening up a
tremendous export trade in bacon, oblivious of the
fact that the local bacon is villainously cured and
abominably salt. Probably the Australian climate
will never permit of dead pig being treated to the
best advantage. In its present state it hasn’t a
ghost of a show against the pork and bacon of
colder countries, and Patterson, as an Irishman,
must know that.
Dear Bulletin, — Be t testimonials to public
servants. There is not much harm when the
servant retires. But when a rising man is pro-
moted he drags along with him those who have
scratched his back. An incident in my Civil
Service experience- the facts are within the know-
ledge of the present Master of Sydney Mint,
Johnny Robertson, late Collector of Inland-
Revenue at Manchester, was Excise officer at-
tached to Lochnagar Distillery, near Balmoral.
He courted a daughterof the distiller—who would
not hear of her marrying a beggarly gauger-
nothing less than a supervisor would suit Mr.
Begg. Johnny then had little prospect of ever
attaining that rank. But Queen Victoria and the
Prince Consort soon after visited Balmoral, and
distiller Begg invited them to look over the dis-
tillery. They were shown round, not by the pro-
prietor, but by the gauger—“ Yer ain servant,”
said the cute Begg, “an’ a word from Yer High-
ness to Somerset House would give him his due.”
In a few months, Johnny Robertson was pro-
moted. He married Miss Begg, and gradually
got to the top of the tree. —Yours, V.
A peculiarity of Grey is, that he is not a
newspaper-reader. “My friends,” he once told
the writer, “send me anything in the newspapers
that I ought to see, and, of course, I get all the
leading reviews. Do you know, promiscuous
newspaper-reading weakens the mental forces, and
I am sure it would weaken my power of express-
ing myself in English.” Not long ago a man cut
his throat, leaving behind him the most pathetic
hues on record : “ 1 can no longer work. I have
spoilt my intellect by reading the newspapers.”
The average man doesn’t speak English ; he only
gibbers journalese. In the papers certain sub-
stantives are always qualified by the same adjec-
tives. Every fire becomes a devastating confla-
gration ; business is ever stagnant; a site is always
picturesque; a fortune, princely; a citizen,
prominent; a cry, heartrending; a surgeon,
skilled; a theatrical performance, crowded from
floor to ceiling ; a detective, clever ; waves are
invariably rippling; darkness, Cimmerian; and
dawn, rosy. E. T. Gillon, of Wellington (M L.)
Post, has a list of “Terms not to be used ” pasted
up in the reporters’-room, and woe to him who
transgresses.
One Troubridge Critchell has been using
space in the London Standard to gird at Aus-
tralians for declining to make kangaroo the Aus-
tralian national meat. Mr. Critchell knows all
about the marsupial, and is positive that it could
be advantageously farmed, so as not only to supply
all Australia with flesh-meat, but also to fill up
a big export-trade. He ought to be fed for a
month on the regulation kangaroo-ham, as served
out to the regular Australian navvy, and surfeited
witli kangaroo-tail soup. He would soon call for
buzzard and black snake, both of which furnish a
more juicy repast than the long-tailed marsupial
which Charles Lamb styled the “spirit of Aus-
tralia.” Kangaroo sausage, moreover, already
rivals the far-famed Bologna, only it isn’t labelled
according to its genesis of manufacture, but is
known as Bungalow Scouse. Further, it may
interest Critchell and other curious English
scientists to know that the cross-breeding of
kangaroos and cassowaries, now such a profitable
squatting achievement on the slopes of the Wild
Waterfall Sierras in the Central Australian Desert,
has produced a variegated marsupial bird known
as the kangareemu, whose feathers yield six crops
a year, and, in beauty and value, surpass those of
the champion Takentottian ostriches. Australia
allows no English epicure to teach it its way
about either as to diet or dress. What Australian
dejeuner is complete without the scalloped wing
of the piebald kangaroo? What Xmas ball in
Australia has not its sacred kangaroo mizzletoe?
What but enough.

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHAT IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got tor sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. _ By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at

Alhambra Variety Show.
A. W. Pinero’s play, “The Second Mrs.
Tanqueray," was produced by the Brough-Bouci-
cault Company at Sydney Lyceum on April 14,
for the first time in Australia. The cast was :
Aubrey Tanqueray Mr. Titheradge.
Caley Drummle Mr. Boucicault.
Sir George Orreyed Mr. Ward.
Oapt. Hugh Ardale , .. Mr. Renouf.
Prank Misquith, Q.C Mr. Carey.
Gordon Jayne, M.D Mr. Mclntyre.
Morse Mr. Hards.
Paula Mrs. Brough.
Mrs. Cortelyon .. .. Miss Romer.
Ellean Miss Noble.
Lady Orreyed Miss Temple.
“ The Second Mrs. Tanqueray ” was the sensation
of last year’s London season. It shared with “The
Tempter ” distinction as a play more notable than
any seen for years. One defied the cant of social
respectability ; the other, the cant of religious re-
spectability. Both broke with traditions of the
English stage as old as the century.
» # *
The merit of “ The Second Mrs. Tan-
queray ” lies less in its cleverness and brilliancy
than in its strength, audacity, and truth. These
qualities are perhaps more easily appreciated by
a Londoner than by an Australian. In Australia
we have Mrs. Tanqueray’s genus, but not her
species. In London, from any one of a score of
Piccadilly supper-rooms her counterpart might
walk straight upon the stage, dressed and painted
and dizened exactly as we see her. The man who,
moved by love and chivalry, sacrifices friends and
position for such a person, is rare, but not un-
known. The other characters come within the
scope of every-day acquaintance. The present
writer, who saw the play as produced at London
St. James’s, holds that the Brough-Boucicault
company have given Sydney a presentment of the
author’s ideas still more even and harmonious, if
in parts less striking and powerful.
* * *
Nevertheless, the Lyceum audience re-
ceived the piece with a distinct sense of dis-
appointment. Its wickedness had been so well
advertised that they came to “sniff the sniffy,”
expecting to be shocked. They found, with sur-
prise, that they could stand a much greater shock
than the play provided. It was naughty, but not
naughty enough ; and the revulsion of feeling went
to its discredit. Eor what is daring in England is
commonplace in Australia. We are as broad as
our island ; our forbears are narrow as theirs. We
have far less social hypocrisy than they. Aus-
tralian men do not hesitate to call a spade a spado;
Australian women do not blush to hear them. Aus-
tralian boys are certainly bold; Australian girls
as certainly precocious. Those who know them
best believe as little as Daudet’s heroine in the
existence of ingenues—comme si du monde ou pas
du monde, toutes les filles ne savaient pas de nais-
sance, de quoi il retourne. It would be unjust to
accord them less virtue than their sisters of colder
climes ; but they have nob the virtue of ignorance.
» * •
Yet, though to some extent it failed to
impress, “The Second Mrs. Tanqueray” remains
an impressive play. Shallow critics object that
the heroine typifies but one mood, in one tense, of
the eternal feminine problem which is the joy and
despair of existence. A profounder judgment
admits that the mood is permanent, and the tense
always the present. Every woman is a potential
Mrs. Tanqueray, to be made or marred by cir-
cumstances. Aubrey asks Paula to look back to
the time when she was like Ellean; quite as fitly
he might have asked Ellean to look forward to the
time when she would be like Paula. All Paulas
have been Elleans; any Ellean may be a Paula.
The gulf seems wide, but it is easily and
naturally spanned. There is in any woman no
absolute, no limitation so far as the emotional
faculties are concerned. Each has within herself
the capacity for infinite development. Virtue is
an accident : between nun and none there is only
a difference of degree. As the human embryo
changes t hrough all gradations of the animal king-
dom in its progress to maturity, so the woman
passes through all the stages on which womanhood
is built. Celia is arrested at one stage, Delia at
another; in Rosalind a phase of feeling tints a
moment, in Juliet it colours a life. There has
been a time in the life of every woman when she
longed to kick over the traces. In the memory of
that time, for women, lies the strength of “ The
Second Mrs. Tanqueray.”
* # #
For men, the interest is more critical, less
subjective. And a critic must say at once that
Mrs. Brough is out of her depth as Paula. When
the task is merely to represent the riotous humours
of a vulgar woman she is admirable. In the high
comedy of the first scene with Mrs. Cortelyon she
is admirable again. But when comedy deepens
into tragedy she furnishes a perpetual anti-climax
to herself. Her face is not the tragic mask ; her
figure is that of Thalia, not of Melpomene. She
does not fail in intensity, nor in art, and in
spite of natural unfitness she often plays effec-
tively. But Mrs. Patrick Campbell is still more
effective, and chiefly because she has not Mrs.
Brough’s personal advantages. Space just permits
to add that Mr. Titheradge was excellent, if a
little heavy ; that Mr. Boucicault was excellent, if
a little light; that Mr. Renouf’s bearing was not
sufficiently dignified, nor his manner sufficiently
earnest; and that Mr, Ward, Miss Romer, Miss
Noble, and Miss Temple command between them
a good third of the success of the piece.
* * m *■
“ A Wife’s Peril,” which is more like a
London Journal story, in four squeals, than the
average drama is wont to be, went up at Sydney
Royal last Saturday, with Miss Blanche Lewis
giving a convincing rendering of the imperilled
spouse in question. When you stage a highly-
moral play which is going to be a salutary lesson
to every Mrs. Smith who finds that Mr. Smith is
getting horribly tiresome, and who meditates
going off with the lodger, it is necessary, in the
best interests of morality, that the lawful (stage)
husband should be beautiful to the eye, and not a
lanky man who wants his ’air cut, as the im-
perilled husband did in the play under notice. It
might be unnatural to present hubby with his hair
curled and a dreamy look in his eyes, but think
how flattered the married men would be!
* * *
Albert Norman, as the Italian count dis-
guised as a sculptor, occasionally forgot that he
was an Italian ; bub he was a fine-spirited villain*
and when he returned to the charge disguised in a
beard seven shades lighter than his hair, the audi-
ence trembled lest the husband should recognise
him. Ivy Scott, whose energetic spelling lesson
is as good as a week at a night-sehcol to the enrap-
tured galleryites, makes lots of her little part,,
and Miss Lou Amey and Cyril Keightly did much
to lighten the sympathetic gloom of the sixpenny
gods when the last act had left the heroine faint-
ing on her mother’s grave at midnight.
» • •
“Nan the Good-for-Nothing ” preceded
the main dish, and with Polly Emery playing Nan
with too much abandon, it looked as if the title
was neatly put. The Bulletin saw Nellie Farren
play Nan at a matinee, and since then it has
failed to see any funny points about succeeding
hoydens washing their faces monotonously with a
bar of soap while the audience wonders whether
the ablutions will stop short at a Hck-and-a-
promise.
*# * *
The ever-mildewed drama, “East Lynne, *
which smells damp and mouldy from the tears
that have fallen in and around it, went up for one
night last Saturday, at Sydney Bijou Gaiety)
Theatre, although a wise and far-seeing manage-
ment would have kept “The Strategist” in the bill,
seeing how theatrical Sydney was given over to
gloom and remorse and the hopeless wreckage of
the Ten Commandments on that particular even-
ing. As the public couldn’t support the sorrow
of Lady Isabel (played with the right amount of
wet-raggishness by the pretty Miss Gladys Leigh)
for more than one evening the management fore-
went (heir intention of sluicing out the theatre
with her for the current week and revived the
merry farce, hereinbefore alluded to, for one night
(Monday) to close the season.
** * *
The present hilarious revival of the im-
mortal “Lights o’ London,” now being played to
top-heavy houses at Sydney Her Majesty’s, is a
proof how much the setting stars in that sort of
play, because the present caste is the weakest—
here and there—that ever struggled with the
large, damp drama. George Rignold’s hero, and
Watts-Phillips’ heroine, are as forceful and con-
vincing as of yore, but Kate Bishop has at last
struck a part which doesn’t suit her in the travel-
ling show lady; and although everything Miss B.
does she makes acceptable, still Mrs. George
Rignold must remain the best Mrs. Jarvis on the
road. A hard-voiced lady takes Emily Fitzroy’s
old part as Hetty Preene, and kicks her train out
behind her heels like a vicious two-year-old, and
H. Plimmer, as the villain, seemed to consider
that vice cannot go half a block without lighting
a cigarette. Mr. Plimmer’s legs, by the way, are
plump and pleasing, so when he talks in the front
act of “fatted calves,” it sound as if he was mak-
ing personal allusions to himself. The “Lights
o’ London” is certainly raking in the cash. When
you have seen it, even for the hundredth time, you
always feel as if you had really been to the
theatre. Going to the “Lights o’ London ” feels
more like going to the theatre than does going to
any other play extant.
** * *
“ A Woman’s Revenge,” the last of Henry
Pettitt’s melodramas, was produced at Melb.
Princess’ on Saturday. Hopes of a great literary
work had been excited by advance paragraphs, in
which the. public were told to prepare themselves
for a powerful tale that would spell-bind them
from beginning to end. Considered as a play
with pretentions to greatness, “A Woman’s
Revenge ” is a distinct failure, but viewed as a
neatly-written Pettittism, in four acts, it yields a
fair amount of satisfaction. The author’s attempt
to rise superior to the scene-painter is sufficiently
vigorous to indicate that he might have succeeded
later on had he not died three months ago.
Miss Edith Blande, in her eternal capacity of an
adventuress, is the woman who revenges herself
upon Miss Watson, by separating her from her
faithful husband, Sass. The thinnest of evidence
suffices to “wreck the lives” of this sus-
picious pair, soon after the birth of their
cheeild. Sass is supposed to have eloped with
Edith, while Miss Watson gets discredit from her
rather maudlin husband for an improper attach-
ment to Cosgrove, that improving actor being
leagued with W. E. Baker, his companion in
financial crimes.
• * *
After long years, Sass sneaks into his
virtuous wife’s cottage, and interviews his little
daughter alone; before he can sneak out again
the mother comes from an adjoining room,
followed by Cosgrove, who has been looking at
papers. Sass “suspects the worst” from this
compromising situation, and the audience, having
seen the situation sticking out beforehand, are
not surprised. The men quarrel, Sass wrenches
a pistol from Cosgrove, puts the weapon on the
sideboard, and departs. Cosgrove had slunk
away previously, like the proverbial beaten hound.
Then Cosgrove returns with the intention of
embracing Miss for the first time. _ He
chases the lady round the furniture until the little
daughter pops up in her nightgown and sees her
mother defy the bad man with the pistol afore-
said. Miss Watson is the next to go away. Her
departure is the signal for the arrival of Baker.
He comes attended by an awful stage tempest,
and the noise of somebody knocking thunder out
of a big drum is heard in the land. Baker grabs
the omnipresent pistol and kills Cosgrove after a
bit. He turns the lamp down, rifles the corpse of
much ill-gotten plunder, and rushes forth into the
thunder and lightning with a serio-comic shriek.
* * *
MissWatsonis arrested for the murder. Sass,
an illogical person, believes in his wife all round
by this time, and when she comes up for trial at
the Old Bailey the heroic Sass, who happens to be a
qualified barrister, appears for the defence. The
set of the Criminal Court shows careful attention
to detail, and is soenically convincing. The trial
itself is merely a solemn farce, loaded with impos-
sibilities, and abounding in proofs that Petti tt
considered the average playgoer to be even a
bigger ass than the law. Much was expected of
this Old Bailey scene. It was to save the piece
and bring the curtain down upon an impressive
spectacle. In truth it couldn’t compare with the
trial episode in a play performed at the Royal
some four years ago—“ A Man and his Shadow.”
Much of the acting in “ A Woman’s Revenge ” is
too tame for the requirements of such a purely
stage fiction, but this matter may be attended to
if the show should, by accident, catch on.
** * *
“The Octoroon,” embellished with a few
variety items, is calculated to do very well at
Melb. Alexandra, where performances are criti-
cised from the popular-price standpoint. Perhaps
this is Dan Barry’s best achievement up to date,
and seeing that the intrepid Daniel has been
working up a connection for the last 19 weeks,
it follows that “ The Octoroon ” is a liberally-
mounted and decently-acted old friend. Uncle, Tom
Easton shines on the present occasion as a giddy old
nigger of musical habits. E. B. Russell, D. Barry,
and E. C. Appleton fill their places, and Miss
Eilla Wilde, with all her faults, can sob like one
genuinely afflicted. Here and there one comes
across a member of the Alexandra Company who
ought to be reprimanded with a heavy mallet,
and prevented from singing any more, but fiends
of the same description are found at more expen-
sive theatres.
** * *
The Royal continues to be filled five
nights per week and crammed on Saturdays by
“Struck Oil.”

Beaumont, the baldheaded denizen of the
tank, is back at the Alhambra, also his wife has
resumed her familiar piece of stitching under the
water. “ Charley’s Aunt ”is said to be bringing
an improved cast to the Bijou for her farewell
week, commencing Saturday next, and manager
Garner has definitely decided to open his reign at
the same house on May sth. Walter Bentley is
engaged by the new management as leading man,
a position which the gifted child of Scotia will
adorn if he makes up his mind to do so.
At Poverty Point.
One of the Melh. papers gave an elaborate account of
Vi. Varley’s marriage to Joseph Tapley—the flowers, the
bridal party , and other supposed etceteras. As a fact,
Vi. herself was the only flower visible, and “ the party ”
consisted of her mother and sister, with Mr. and Mrs.
Amos Norcott (Ida Osborne).

I don’t think was so much to blame.

There is a strong desire tor Miss Nellie Stewart to under-
take tuition. Her training has been highly scientific.
After long operatic experience, she studied under Ran-
degger. She would be at the top of the tree as a local
Marches!.
Myra Kemble left for England by the Oroya, partly on
business bent. She may now avail herself of some of the
opportunities neglected during her last trip. She returns
by the end of the year. Miss Kemble, by-the-way, says
she goes to London to consult Dr. Braxton Hicks. Hicks
is a coroner, by-the-way. The idea of consulting a coro-
ner for the good of your health recalls one evening when
the writer, who is near-sighted, hailed a hearse, thinking
it a ’bus.
Dear Bulletin,— Good old Kitts is credited with an
affection for the part of Devilshoof. Well, the part didn’t
much like Kitts, whose long body and short legs infused
an air of “ eminent respectability” into the moat blood-
curdling parts, and made his Devilshoof suggest a ‘‘right-
thinking person” shamming “ a bold, bad man.” By the
way, why is greasy respectability generally short in the
leg ?—A.M.
A certain tall, “show” member of a certain company,
that visited Australia some ages ago, earned a reputation
era voyage for general dirtiness of habits and repugnance
to cold water. It was such a great reputation that
passengers and crew feasted upon anecdotes concerning
the fairy, and took a strong personal interest in her
peculiarities. When the steamer got somewhat stuck in
the Suez Canal, causing mud to darken its slow water-
course, somebody anxiously enquired of the captain
whether they were aground. “ O, no; it’s only Miss
Blank having a bath,” said the captain. He had just
received news that the grimy actress was taking her first
wash since leaving England. It was considered, by those
most competent to judge, that she never committed a
similar indiscretion ashore.
Dear Bulletin,— Some of your correspondents are
rabidly patriotic, or rather chauvinistic. I was amused at
Steele Grey’s suggestion re the visit of an Australian
operatic troupe to England. It is true there are some
capable vocalists in our midst, but how many of these
possess the necessary qualification of a good actor as well
as of a singer? There is one true local artiste only whom
I could name, and her voice is already showing signs of
decay. And as to some of those enumerated by your
correspondent, I can but attribute the fact of their being
still on the boards to the good matured tolerance of their
audiences. What good would the invasion of England by
an Australian opera co. do except to show that we can
superficially imitate the veneer of an old-world civilisa-
tion ?— True Patriotism, Melb.
That magnificent travelling piety-show, organist Jude,
lately informed London Mornino Post that “in Adelaide
18,000 citizens petitioned him to stay another year, and
tendered him a free house, horses, &o.” News, indeed.
Jude was prolific in farewell seasons in Adelaide, and
after months of affliction the tired citizens presented the
pious organist with a travelling-bag. Anyone but Jude
would have taken the hint.

April 8. His old pal, Harwood, is now appearing with
Thornton, in “The Private Secretary,” in Adelaide.

The world’s romance is not all shut between the covers
of its novels. In New York, Pierre Brady, hopelessly in-
fatuated with Mme. Jane Hading, deliberately crossed the
great divide, leaving an empty laudanum-bottle and this
letter:—“Madame, my last thoughts are for you. At the
moment of quitting this world I bid you a passionate
farewell.” A curious creature, a man. Even his Maker
must be astonished at him sometimes.

“ No woman shall appear on the stage unless she be clad
in a dress the skirts of which do not open at the side, and
descend to within four inches of the ground, and the waist
of the dress shall be so constructed as not to expose any
part of the bust. And no woman shall appear in visible
tights, nor engage in public in any exhibition of high
kicking, or fan, serpentine, hip or skirt dancing.” This
is a clause from a Bill for the Suppression of Indecency In
Public Performances, now before Massachusetts (U.S.)
Legislature. Massachusetts is the old Puritan state which
has its early history filled with page after page of records
that “ John Smith, for carnally kissing Mary Jones, was
adjudged to be whipped and put in the stocks.”
The latest London music-hall success is a song with the
refrain “No Crackling on the Pork.” It appeals to the
inmost soul of a nation which eats late suppers and has
nightmares.

A N.Y. manager has added a nursery to his theatre.
Women carrying children can leave their babies in the
nursery while they themselves sit out the performance.
A business idea full of weird possibilities.
Editor Bulletin. Sir,—Will you allow me to correct a
statement made in last week’s Bulletin? Though no
doubt inadvertently made, it is calculated to do me some
injury in my profession. Your correspondent says that
“ I went to England last year for vocal studies, and had
returned weighted with disappointment, my voice, which
once promised so well, &c., &c., and for the present
meant to follow my family profession of dancing.” Now,
kindly allow me to say it is two years since I went
Home, during which time I finished my musical studies
under Mr. Sims Reeves and Madame Rose Hers6e, when I
unfortunately caught a very severe cold, and was ordered
by the doctors to rest for six months and reside in a warm
climate ; but I beg to assure you, Mr. Editor, that I have
no intention whatever of following the arduous profession
of a dancer.—Yours truly, Claire Duvalli. Melbourne,
13th April, 1891.

A Westralian correspondent to the S.A.
Register
One of the storekeepers at Coolgardie offered to sell one
of our party a lot of newspapers before we started, saying
that “the boys out there would be only too glad of them,”
and adding further that it was no use keeping them, as
Coolgardie was almost deserted. The newspapers were
bought, and they filled a couple of sacks. It was midday
on Sunday when we arrived at Kurnalpi, and a big crowd
was assembled playing a game of what is euphoniously
termed “ two-up.” The purchaser of thenewspapers rode
up holding a copy of the Sydney Bulletin in his hand.
Then there was a rush and a scramble, for the 800 diggers
gathered there had not seen a newspaper for weeks. For-
tunately the vendor was mounted on a tall camel, other-
wise he might have been torn to pieces. Half-a-crown was
freely offered for a newspaper, and at that rate Observers,
Australasians, Bulletins, and other weekly*publications
were all disposed of in almost a shorter space of time than
it has taken me to write this.

“ Injustice may be done two ways—by force or
Laud, baud being the property of a fox, force that of a
lion ; both are utterly repugnant to society, but fraud is
the more detestable. But in the whole system of villainy
no i« is more capital than that of the men who, when they
most deceive, so manage that they may seem to be vir-
tuous men.” —Cicero on the inhabitants of Melbourne.

the Queensland Government Gazette, that the assets of
its industrial branch are £152,951, and that the “ amount
estimated to be required to reinsure” all its existing
ri<ks is only £83,438, leaving a surplus of nearly £70,000.
X ,is looks good, but then who “ estimates ” the amount
required to reinsure, &0., and what is the difference be-
tween an estimate and a dead certainty ?

One Southern trade-assignee was, five years ago,
an in voice-clerk at 30s. a week. He has just built himself a
mansion. Another only assigned his own estate a couple
of jears ago, and was so impressed by the facilities
afforded his trustee to absorb all his assets that he started
on his own account. He now bets in fifties.

A certain “gent one,” after “ducking” his
fellow-solicitors for a subscription to pay back purloined
moneys, has “ done it all in " at racing.
* * ♦ *
A ’cute person who, not long since, privately
assigned his estate, is credited with having run
a racing-stable, a bone-mill (ostensibly to treat
at cost price the crooks the racing-stable consisted of),
a timber-yard, a tinsmith, a urinal-factory, a lolly-
factory, a lime merchant, a building society (with a thou-
sand houses on its books), a biscuit-factory, a coach-
builder, a livery stable (for such racing crocks as were too
thin to go to his bone-mill ?). Lastly, he ran a couple
of banks into such thundering overdrafts that for a whole
year the managers are rumoured to have secretly hired
assassins to mutilate their customer’s right hand, so as to
prevent him signing cheques which they were afraid to
dishonour.

One has a 10-roomed house, with two to three acres of

Tne other has also a 10-roomed house, with hot and cold

P.M.G. Wynne (Vic.) doesn’t like Maoriland
postal-notes. He says “ they are to all intents and pur-
poses bank-notes, with this difference, that there is no
limit to the amount issued, and no gold basis at the back
of them. What, then, becomes of the current coin paid
for them? It is simply introducing State bank-notes by a
side wind.” Of course it is, and it is a blow at private
banking enterprise, and several other things.

N.S.W. Government proposes to spend .£lOO,OOO
in extending Sydney tramways. With twopenny ’buses
competing with threepenny trams, and a proposed line of
penny ’buses fathered by the plucky Saywell, to run
from Redfern Station to Circular Quay, the money is
more likely to be needed to makeup the loss on the work-
ing of the trams.

♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
One large Sydney manufacturing firm, with
many thousands locked up in reconstructed banks, was
recently about to throw up the sponge, but the banks
kind y came to the rescue and lent exactly one-fifth of the
annexed money at —8 per cent! So it’s not true that
business people are being unduly harassed by the banks.
* » * ♦
The cold-blooded manager of a big reconstructed
financial institution has got the board in a tight place, as
it has just discovered. In addition to being manager of
the new concern he is also sole liquidator of the old one—
the one which burst. The directors want to get rid of
him on account of his aggressiveness and his general want
of manners, but they find that though they could sack
him as manager they couldn’t shift him from his other
billet as liquidator. And if he continues to be liquidator,
after they have irritated the animal by treading on its
tail in its managerial capacity, he may insist on keeping
hold of the funds as they come in to meet possible claims,
and the new concern may go broke before the cash of the
old one can be wrested from him by legal process. And
meanwhile the manager, who understands the situation,
blasts promiscuously and makes himself practically unen-
durable.
• ♦ • •
Seven thousand Victorians are booked to ship for
Westralia before the end of May. They must represent
£120,000 solid capital at least. The departures of the
boats for the West are the only events which now occur
to stir up Melbourne. The vessels are generally crowded,
and so are the wharves, and when the emigrants move off
hearty cheers go up from both sides.
* • * *
Hampton Plains syndicate is said to want 25
p.c. gross royalty from Lord Percy Douglas for prospect-
ing on their 216,000-acre estate ; how much he will want
from commoners, Heaven only knows. Should Lord P.D.
aforesaid, find much gold there will be 10,000 men at once
on Hampton Plains, and no power on earth can keep them
off.
* ♦ » *
As soon as the Westralian Premier returns from
the Murchison a proposal will be made him to build a
light railway from Southern Cross to Coolgardie. The
place cannot live without a railway to take water there
and bring ore away. Millar’s proposal to rail the ore to
permanent water is bound to be adopted should more
water be not found at the deeper levels.
♦ ♦ - » *
Condensing is the solution of the W.A. gold-
fields water problem so far as it affects domestic supplies,
and by its aid the country will now be prospected as
far as the salt lakes extend, which is hundreds of miles.
The mines must depend on deep sinking and surface dams
for supplies.
» * * ♦
A Bulletin correspondent:—Premier Forrest
keeps a party of prospectors on camels travelling through
this (Coolgardie) district in his interest. They hail from
Hillgrove, N.S.W., and last year secured the Brilliant on
the Premier line lately sold to Messrs. C. and E. Miller.
They are now opening up a property further south on the
same line, which is so promising that they have doubled
their holding during the week. As soon as summer breaks
up they will start out-back, probably' in the direction of
Lake Beyerlee.
* * * •
The Australian Institute of Mining Engineers
has ended another conference at which certain excellent
papers on threadbare subjects were discussed. The
time would have been better used in an attempt to burst
up the great Australian dynamite-monopoly. Under a
convention dynamite is sold at Is. 9d. per lb. Its manu-
facture costs less than one-fourth that amount. It is
made and retailed in America at 6Jd. to 7d. per lb. If it
were sold here at Is. it would make an enormous difference
in the cost of mining, particularly on Bendigo and
Charters Towers. Not 5 per cent, of the quantity used is
made in Australia. It is time it was all made here.
* * « •
Ballarat School of Mines about to test Hannay’s
electro-cyanide process which caused a little stir in Lon-
don last year. If we don’t mistake, Hannay is the
scientist who first made diamonds. But he can’t make
big ones-only the sort that come out of Capt. Rogesr’
Monte Christo.
» • • ♦
Bendigo miners are full of fads. The latest is
horses and buggies—the result of cheap oats and hay. To
see miners drive to their work in a gig is a cheerful sight.
Said gig is tooled home and sometimes capsized by the
miner’s boy, who is again capsized when the old man has
finished his shift. A driving and riding professor ought
to do well on Bendigo.
♦ * • •
The writer predicted it. It is the usual result
of newspaper booming. N.S.W. again has the new-gold-
saving-machine-epidemic. It has the John Jerger, the
Eclipse, the Weeks, the Eureka, the Miners’ Friend ;
others are threatened. Why is poor N.S.W. always made
the victim? Why not try Victoria? The Bulletin will
advocate a Gold-Machine-Limitation Act—with a death-
penalty.
• • * •
A Sydney paper ventures the opinion that the
internal expenses of Friendly Societies are made to appear
abnormally big by the inclusion in the balance-sheets of
‘‘the salaries of the medical officers.” What supremely
unconscious sarcasm !
Five years ago, we are told, bricks cost 50s. per
1000 at the kiln, at Hoffman Brick Co., Brunswick (Vic.),
and all orders had to be booked two years in advance.
Now bricks can be had for 12s. per thousand. Men and
boys then averaged IOJd. per hour. Now the few
mechanics and labourers (there are no boys) average only
6d. per hour.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
A small land-owner sought to boirow .£5O from
a N.S.W. money-lender, on mortgage of a piece of land
that had cost £l3O. The loan-spider gave him (nominally)
£3O, but actually £27 155., charging an expense fee of
£2 ss. ; promising £2O more in a fortnight, but refusing
this when the stated time had elapsed. The borrower
was under the delusion that he had to pay the loan back
at his convenience, with 12J per cent, interest added. He
was also under the delusion that he had signed only a
mortgage. Within 12 months he had repaid £8 10s.
Then he effected a sale of the land and repaired to the
money-lender to settle up. “ £4B, please. You signed a
transfer, and I have had the property conveyed to my-
self.” The dupe went to a lawyer. “ Consents to settle
the matter for £23 175.,” was the legal shark's report, in
due season. Settled accordingly. But the lawyer’s bill
came to £26, so that it would have been cheaper to have
been robbed by the money-lender only. N.B. Srme
lawyers are money-lenders themselves; most Sydney
lawyers are directly interested, by invested moneys, trust
or otherwise, in money-lending institutions.
* * * •
The editor of the Mundic Miner (Georgetown,
N.Q.) isn’t one of those who sneak into Heaven by the
back door. There are hard times on the Etheridge now,
but he won’t recommend a day of humiliation and prayer
in the hope that the Lord will send a fresh influx of Eng-
lish capital: ‘‘The Etheridge diggings has never at any
period paid any attention to the behests of the Almighty,
not even during the periods of wild and lavish prosperity
when gold was plentiful and gutters overflowed with rum,
and that invisible and inscrutable Being is not likely' to sit
up at night plotting and planning for the relief of this
diggings more particularly when He is being worried and
tormented with prayerful humiliation from all parts of
the world. Therefore, it is useless to adopt the pious
dodge and invoke heavenly assistance. Man must trust
to the strength of his joints and the durability of his
nerves.” Tub Bulletin loves that editor. If all Aus-
tralia would put its shoulder to the wheel, instead of sit-
ting on its bunk and singing out for Jupiter, the cart
would soon move out of the mud.
• • * *
The Southern Cross mining Warden warns the
crowd that the Coolgardie water trouble will be very
serious if no rain falls in April. Too little has been made
of this curse of most Westralian goldfields. A few weeks
back nearly an inch fell at Coolgardie, yet the water
caught in Bayley’s dam would not give a week’s crush-
ing. Had it been immediately followed by .another inch,
ten times the quantity would have been conserved. Com-
panies with big capitals and big dams will live to see them
full; those without will probably all die of waiting and
wasting.
* » * *
Some miners say Wyalong gold is not confined
to quartz. By no means a good sign. Gold without a
defined matrix is usually precarious and patchy—Pambula,
to wit.
* * * *
The South African De Beers Consolidated
Mines Ltd.—that monument to the organising genius of
Caesar Rhodes—which practically controls the world’s
diamond-market, and restricts output to maintain prices,
has paid £4,137,175 divs. since its formation in April,
1888. Sales of diamonds for last year, £3,239,389. Esti-
mated present income, £270,000 monthly ; annual divi-
dend, 12£ per cent, on capital of four millions. (Please
gasp here).

Current N.S.W. wages, as officially stated by
the superintendent of the Govt. Labour Bureau:—Farm
and orchard hands, 10s. to 20s. per week and found ;
station carpenters, 15s. to 325. 6d. per week and found ;
married couples, £5O to £BO per annum and found ; bush-
men, 10s. to 255. per week and found ; camp cooks, 12s. 6d.
to 30s. ; general station hands, 10s. 6d. to 275. 6d. ; handy
and generally useful men, Bs. to 20s. These prices repre-
sent reductions from the rates ruling last year of—farm
hands, 19 per cent. ; station carpenters, 21 per cent. •
married couples, 10 per cent.; bushmen, 22 per cent. ’•
camp cooks, 26 per cent. ; station hands, 16 per cent’
Last year’s wages for ‘‘generally usefuls ” are not stated".

A Hoeburne (N.W. Australia) corres-
pondent writes to The Bulletin You have no
idea what this country is like. Talk about slavery
in the olden days ! I reckon it never was a patch
on this. Here you see niggers, old and young,
chained together, working on the roads and train-
lines, in the broiling sun-some even chained to
the wheelbarrows they are working with. The
labour is of the worst description in all Australia—
Chinese, Japanese, Malay, Indian, Manila men,
half-castes and all the mixtures and colours under
the sun. And these brutes can get work when a
white man can’t. The wages run from £2 10s. to
£6 a month, I reckon over .£50,000 a year goes
out of this country —to China principally. . . .
Take W.A. as a whole it is a miserable squatting
country. All the squatters in this part are stone-
broke. They have been squatting for over 20
years, and some of them during these last two years
have had to walk out without a stiver, leaving
debts, in some cases as big as .£30,000, on about
half as many thousand of sheep.

Sportsmen Unanimous .
A CHORUS OF APPROVAL.
When sportsmen in all parts of the habitable
globe show unanimity in testifying to the marvel-
lous cures effected by a medicament, that medica-
ment must, indeed, possess most _ valuable pro-
perties. The virtues of St. Jacobs oil have elicited
from “sports” of every class in Australia,
America and Great Britain a chorus of approval.
The speed and efficacy with which St. Jacobs oil
cures and subdues pains and bruises of all kinds
are acknowledged by leading cricketers, foot-
ballers, pedestrians, cyclists, scullers, yachtsmen,
swimmers, shooters and “turfites” in both hemis-
pheres. The following representative sportsmen,
among many others, have, from personal experi-
ence, spoken to the merits of St. Jacobs oil; —
Charles Turner, Australia’s champion bowler.

wicket-keeper.

president of the Maryland Jockey Club.

John M ; Lean ; and
Edward Hanlan, the renowned scullers.

And so on, ad infinitum. While sportsmen so
strongly commend and recommend St. J acobs oil,

enthusiastic in its praise. It will instantly relieve
and cure neuralgia, sprains, rheumatism, bruises,
gout, lumbago, sciatica, soreness or stiffness of the
joints or muscles, backache, and toothache.
