In Praise of the Drum.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“The Salvation Army had as much right to
people in as the Church people had to ring them in.
“Colonel” DowdliE, at Melb. Town Hall.
Yes, there is no doubt about it, we can do just as
we please;
You can bet the Army’s got to make things hum.
Ring the church-bells from the steeple, if you like;
but with the people
The finest thing to fetch them is the drum,
Yes, the drum,
You can always fetch the people with the drum!
You may talk about the jew’s-harp, praise the
corpulent trombone,
Say the cornet has a tone that’s nyummy-nyum.
Admire the “ kist o’ whistles” from the land of
brose and thistles,
But we pin our faith upon the mighty drum,
Yes, the drum,
We will bet our bottom dollar on the drum !
See, your bishops sit in sackcloth, and your curates
ashes wear,
And your elders o’ the kirk look dour and glum ;
They would high as Hainan jerk us, ’cause we
emulate the circus,
And fascinate the people with the drum,
Yes, the drum,
The persuasive and ear-titillating drum.
Keep your anthems and your organs, keep your
surplices and bells,
Keep your candles and the rest that fill the sum:
For we’ve got, despite your railing, one great
instrument unfailing
That can always fill our sheepfold—that’s the
drum,
Yes, the drum,
0, we knock ’em with the him-hom of the drum !
STEELE GREY.
Heaven—The day after to-morrow.
Editor Bulletin, — Be Mr. M'Oooey’s
recent letter. The deaf- adder certainly has a tail-
sting, though whether he uses it for suicidal or
other purposes, or whether it is purely orna-
mental, I dunno. It’s a scarce reptile. I was
knocking about the bush for years I ever
saw one, and in three years, in one spot in New
England, within a half-mile radius, I killed seven,
and each one had a “ well-defineu and separate
sting. On one occasion I took some hurdles to a
lambing station, and, while speaking to a boy
who was there, a six-months’ pnp which
was with me kept up a barking, which I
thought was at the boy. There was little
grass about—in fact, the blades were M‘Cooeying
to each other, and I noticed that the pup had a
bead of blood on his nose. As I drove away, the
boy M‘Cooeyed to me to “ come and see if this is
a deaf adder.” I went, I saw, and I conquered a
brute over 4ft. long, with a sting on him like a
glorified horsehair sharpened to a point, and the
yellow which they all have at the tail about
l|in. long. The pup was chained up at sundown
as usual, and was dead and stiff next morning.
My theory is that he ran his nose against the
sting. If the adder had bitten him there would
have been two marks. It will take a more capable
man than Mr. M*Cooey to stamp out the belief
that the deaf adder has what can be seen with the
naked eye. The best way to set the question at
rest is for Mr. M‘Gooey to get a specimen and run
the sting into his finger or his thumb. If he dies,
it is poisonous; if he lives, it isn’t. I can, if
necessary, give the name and the then address of
the boy I have spoken of.—Yours faithfully,
Joynt M'Ginnis, North Sydney. P.S.—I am
inclined to think that, like a good many more so-
called scientists, your correspondent has never
been within M‘Gooey of a real deaf adder. —J.
M‘G.
’Frisco Examiner publishes an interview
with “The Man who was not Hanged.” It is
interesting in places. Here are a few extracts :
I saw the man who was not hanged on Friday. 1 saw
him at noon, the very hour he expected to die. The
man’s name is M'Nulty, John M’Nulty. He has been five
times condemned to death and five times reprieved#
Three times he has seen the gallows built for him. The
man who was not hanged is young. He looks not a day
over 25, and he is strong and full of vigor. His face is
round and not bad-looking. He has quiet, clear grey
eyes. His eyes did not tremble when he told the story of
his six years in the shadow of the gallows, and his voice is
clear and matter of fact.
“Yesterday, at this lime,” said the man who was not
hanged, “I thought my cell would be empty at noon
to-day. I have been in the condemned cell a good many
times, and 1 know it very well. Yesterday I shut my
eyes and saw it—empty. It didn t scare me, the empty
cell. It didn’t make me lose my nerve. 1 lose my nerve
a little when 1 wait and watch and hope for a reprieve. I
guess hoping is about as hard work as there is. Waiting
ain’t so bad, not when you know what you’re waiting for.
You can stand a thing when you know its coming,
eV “Do you believe it is right to tell a prisoner that he is
condemned—to let him know when he must die? 1 should
think hope” . , , , ,
“ I just said,” interrupted the man who was not hanged,
speaking quietly, but decidedly, “ that hoping is the
hardest work there is. I meant it. It would drive a man
erazv to keep listening for whispers every time steps came
near his cell. He’d think it was the sheriff come to get
him. If he knew it was for sure he wouldn’t care, but to
think may be —and may be not —that would be hard.
The man who was not hanged glances down the
corridor. ~ , ~ „
“There’s the place,” he said, “where the gallows
stood. I could hear them just as plain as if they d been
in my room,'* said the man. “ I could tell how far alonar
they were this time. I heard them letting the traps fall
to see if it worked all right. That was yesterday. I was
glad then that my mother was not here. The death
watch you hear so much about, I don t mind that; but
I’ll tell you what I don’t like. When the death watch is put
on a man the reporters come to see him, and they look at
him as if he was a wild beast. They say to the death
watch : ‘ How’s he eating ? ’ ‘ Does he sleep well ? Is
he breaking any ? ’ , , ... ~
“They never spoke to me, always to the death watch,
and it sounds as if they were talking of a dog or a horse.
I don’t like that. ... , , ~
“ It makes a man mad to have a lot of people watching
him to see how he acts when he knows he’s got to die.

Travellers’ Tales.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ The biggest oookroaoh I ever saw was on the
Gilbert; he woke me up fighting with a bush rat.”
“ Which licked ?”
“ The cockroach, easily.” _
“.I never saw a cockroach as big asi that, but
I’ve seen some terrors of white ants up in the Ter-
ritory. By gosh ! they can build faster than a
jerry-builder. We were camped with a small mob
of cattle in white-ant country, one night, and they
had built a wall four feet high round the cattle by
the morning. Devil of a job we had to get them
out of it.”
“ I’ve seen spinifex grow pretty fast out west.
The worst kind grows in rings, and the horses,
when their shins get sore, jump over instead of
walking through it. I’ve seen a horse get regu-
larly pounded ; spinifex grew so fast that it was
as high as his head before he had time to jump out
again.”
“ Tell you what I’ve seen a snake do.”
Chorus O dry up about snakes.”
“No, but this is a fact. We had a cook in
camp who used to bake light bread ; and, one
night, he had set ‘ a sponge “ all ready to pop into
the camp-oven at daylight. It was up North,
and one of these pythons came prowling around
the camp and swallowed the cook’s sponge, dish
and all. We found him close to the camp in the
morning, with part of his body fit to burst, for
the dough had risen inside him. When we killed
and opened him we took out the finest batch of
light bread you ever saw, cooked by the heat of
his body.”
And then the meeting adjourned.
D.
A Maoriland penlady of at least 50, and no
beauty at that, describes how a Brisbane procuress
tried to ruin her two years ago. _ Some procuresses
evidently don’t know their business.
The Prince of Wales signified his recon-
ciliation with the Honourable Artillery Company
by attending their grand smoke concert. A
while ago, he withdrew from the honorary
“command” of this non-combative cockney
organisation, over some absurd matter, but the
suggestion of a grovelling orgie in his honour
brought the Heir Apparent round. Touching this
instance of royal forgiveness, a London paper
s -iya : “ A man past middle-age, a grandfather
who has sown many wild oats, and ought to have
finished farming as far as this crop is concerned,
who is in delicate health, who but recently lost
his eldest son, whose wife has lately been seriously
ill, might have found a better way of passing an
evening than puffing at an enormous weed in
a drill-hall, listening to music-hall artists.” Very
likely. Nevertheless, The Bulletin must protest
against the theory that Albert Edward ought to
be repressing his instincts out of respect to the
memory of a feeble son who died two years ago.
At that rate, the father of a consumptive family
would never get a chance to enjoy himself in his
own way. Let us be fair to His Future Majesty.
God bless him !
THEY SPORT WITH HUMAN LIFE.
I’ll give you the plain facts in the case, and you
shall help me to judge whether there was a mistake
made or not. _ . ■
It seems that some time in 1889 —in the winter,
no doubt—Mr. Strong, of Leicester, was taken
down with influenza, often called the grip ; and
truly a strong grip it has when it once takes a
hold. Well, lam glad to say he was able to fight
out the battle and get the best of it. But the best
wasn’t anything to brag of. The influenza hadn t
exactly beaten Mr, Strong, but left him in a very
bad condition. And here is where you and I
properly pick up the thread of the story.
His meals didn’t tempt him; the most savoury
of dishes had no charms for our friend. He had
lost his appetite, and in the long run a man had
better lose his money. He forced down something,
of course, but it gave him such a pain in the chest
that he wished he hadn’t swallowed a mouthful.
Then, naturally, he got so weak and nervous he
couldn’t sleep o’ nights. This, as everybody
knows, is the straight road to the graveyard,
through the madhouse. Lots of people are making
that trip all the time. We must eat and we must
sleep. If we don’t we are done for. Neuralgic
oains, too, made matters worse for Mr. Strong.
He saw a doctor, and what did the doctor do?
This is what the patient says on that point:
“ This doctor gave me all kinds of strengthening
medicines, but none of them did me any and
I continued to suffer for month after month.
Just what we might have expected. Mr. Strong
further says: “In October, 1890, my friend, Mr,
James Webster, of 28, New Walk, Leicester,
advised me to try a medicine called Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup, and I followed his advice. The
first bottle gave me great relief, and I began to
digest my food and to have an appetite ; and after
I had used three bottles I was quite another man.
I was completely cured. All my aches and pains
left me, my strength returned, and I have been
all right ever since. What astonished me was
that the Syrup cured me so quickly, and I shall
never cease thanking Mr. Webster for making it
known to me. You are at liberty to publish my
oasefor thebenefit of others. Yours truly, (Signed)
W, Stkong, 41, East Street, Leicester, December
30th, 1891.” ,
Now, was there a mistake in this matter, and
if so, what was it? Yes, there was a common
mistake made. It is an old and seemingly hope-
less blundor#
“The doctor,’’ says Mr. Strong, “gave me all
kinds of strengthening medicines."
Open wide your ears and remember what I m
going to tell you now ; remember it for the hour
of your own helplessness and pain: There is no
such thing as strengthening medicine, neither in the
earth nor in the waters which are under the earth.
That is the truth ; all the high-class doctors know
it. As for the others, well there ! the less said
about them the better,
Mr, Strong took “strengthening medicines,
and what happened to him? Why, he “continued
to suffer month after month.’’ Did you ever see
a horse made stronger and fatter by spurs and
lash-whips, leaving off the oats and hay ? I think
you have not. And that is what “strengthening
medicines ” do, and all they do. So-called tonics
are like making a sick man walk far and fast by
kicking him at every other step.
Nothing under the sun but digested food imparts
strength ; no drug ever does.
And herein is the perpetual success and victory of
Mother Seigel’s Syrup; it cleanses the system of
disease-poison and leaves Nature sweet and free.
The stomach then cries, “ Feed me,” and so power
and health come back like the green grass after a
shower. Do you see? Mother Seigel proclaims
“I destroy disease!” Nature responds, ‘ Only
do that, and I can take care of myself.”
The disease is indigestion and dyspepsia. Mr,
Strong had it and was badly treated. Should you
have it, trv the Syrup first—not last, for I have
told you why.
London, March, 1892.
EXPERIENCE
Dibbs in 1855.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The early days of great men, generals, statesmen
and philosophers have ever possessed a thrilling
interest for the students of human nature.
In order to give Alexander, and Caesar, and
Peter the Great and other distinguished persons a
brief period of repose, it may be well to attract the
world’s attention to the youthful career of a still
greater man, the present G. Republican Dibbs.
In the year 1855 a tall, loosely-constructed youth,
about 24 years of age, was supercargo on board a
barque called the Monarch. His name was George
Dibbs.
The Monarch was then engaged by A. 0. Gregory
to take the “North Australian Exploring Expedi-
tion ” from Brisbane round the north coast of the
Victorian River.
The Monarch and schooner Tom Tough
sailed out of Moreton Bay on the 12th Aug., and
on the 28th the Monarch ran on a reef off Port
Patterson, and was not floated off until 10th Sept.,
after cutting away the false keel.
On the 18th the exploring party started to land
the horses at “Treachery Bay,” and on the 24th
Sept, the Monarch —Capt. Robinson —sailed away
for Singapore, carrying, among other valuable
cargo, the tall, loosely-constructed youth, herein-
before mentioned.
George was tall and apparently robust, but his
muscles had not developed in proportion to the
length of his legs, a fact well known to all on
board the Monarch, and when the boats returned
from the shore, the only way for the crews to
board the barque was by climbing up a rope hung
over the side.
This rope was intended solely as a trap for the
future Republican. The wiry, small men went up
that rope like opossums, and then Dibbs started
to climb ! As well might a giraffe have tried to
shin up a palm tree. Two long arms clutched
madly at the suspended rope; two long legs,
terminating in two vast feet, kicked wildly at the
empty air, one convulsive struggle to reach the
guard-rail, and the arms, legs, feet and the balance
of the supercargo slid back baffled and defeated
into the boat, amid hilarious demonstrations from
the irreverent persons on board the Monarch.
And no man laughed louder than the present Hon.
A. 0. Gregory. But even in the bitterness of that
trying hour, the sublime self-possession of Dibbs
never forsook him. The stern voice which, in
memorable after years, consigned Chicago to the
Infernal gods, was heard only to murmur —not
loud, but deep as an artesian bore —“ Oh, damn
the rope!”
And so the Monarch went on to Singapore,
shipped about 200 Chinese passengers for Sydney,
and caught fire on the return journey !
In the first stage of the conflagration, Capt.
Robinson called for George Dibbs and told him
to “ keep them blasted Chinamen under cover ! ”
And forthwith the valiant supercargo armed
himself with a capstan bar, stood over the hatch-
way and played the devil’s tattoo on Mongolian
skulls, until the fire was extinguished and the
Monarch was saved ! And the captain believed
that Dibbs would one day be a great general, or a
hero of some kind or other, for he was “ a young
fellow, Vi rave and reckless as a lion, and honest as
the i un.”
And now the rope-climbing, skull-cracking
supercargo is—Premier of New South Wales 1
Brisbane.
A. MESTON.
Artificiality—lack of vanity.
Naturalness —supreme egotism.
It would be interesting to know how much
an M.L.A.’s free railway pass is worth to the
barrister who goes on circuit.
The English Tories are hunting for a man
to beat Labouchere at next election. He is the
worst thorn in the Tory side, and if Northampton
would but throw him over, royalty and aristocracy,
from the Queen to the last baronet’s scullery-boy,
would go mad with ecstasy. But there’s no such
luck in store.
Probably Boulia (Q.) has the most cheer-
fully eccentric climate in Australasia. Summer
in that festive locality is pure, unmitigated Sheol;
winter (in the night) has an invigorating sniff of
Lapland about it. Recently (March) the rainfall
for three days was 12Jin. —exactly the amount
that had fallen during the preceding six years.
State socialism has worked out in an un-
expected way in U.S.A, The Union Pacific rail-
way is insolvent, and in the hands of Government
receivers. They couldn’t make it pay with cur-
rent expenses; so Judge Dundy, of Nebraska,
has granted an order reducing wages by 10 per
cent., coupled with an injunction forbidding the
men to strike.
A few days ago a well-dressed person was
convicted before a Wellington (M.L.) magistrate
of brutally and wantonly kicking and mangling a
small dog, and the P.M., fining him 405., re-
marked that the case was a bad one, and if
defendant hadn’t been a respectable man he would
have been gaoled “without the option.” Evi-
dently, in Wellington, a respectable men is a
person in new clothes who jumps on a small dog.
Formula for Georgian (U.S.A.) marriage
ceremony
By the authority vested in me as an officer of the State
of Georgia, which is sometimes called the Empire Stale of
the South ; by the fields of cotton that lay spread out in
snowy whiteness around us ; by the howl of the coon dog,
and the gourd vine whose clinging tendrils will shade the
entrance to your humble dwelling-place ; by the red and
luscious heart of the watermelon, whose sweetness fills
the heart with joy; by the heavens and earth, in the
presence of these witnesses, I pionounce you man and
wife.
Cairns and Geraldton are rival ports for
the traffic of Towalla (N.Q.) goldfield, midway
between them. Oairna had the better road, and
was leading slightly, when Geraldton made a new
road which cut off ten miles, and began to get the
trade. But this new road crosses the Johnstone
River, and the Cairns people, finding they
couldn’t win by fair means, tried foul : they
bribed a 15-ton alligator to lie in wait at the
Johnstone crossing, and attack all the Geraldton
trade. One Chinaman and three horses have gone
already.
Editor Bulletin,— -Re a par. as to extra
fees received by Bankruptcy officials, let me draw
your attention to the N.S.W. Sheriff’s bailiffs.
They get a fair salary with considerable pickings,
yet they never miss an opportunity of clapping on
the costs, and I know of cases in my district
where the bailiff has had two or more writs to
serve on people residing, say, 15 or 20 miles away,
and the full mileage was charged in each and every
case ! I have also heard the bailiff boasting how
much he earns-an income much more than is en-
ioyed bv hundreds of highly-educated professional
men When we consider that most of this money
comes out of the pockets of poor and distressed
people it seems to me that reconstruction is
needed in the Sheriff’s office. -Growler [A
shocking example of the callousness of legal ad-
ministration. —Ed. B.]
TO THE EDITOR OF THE COLONIST.
Sir,—The name of Professor Richard is per-
fectly familiar to me, he having lived in 1 ar k‘
lane, Mayfair, W., within a stones throw of
where I resided in London. The leading phy-
sicians there acknowledging the curative power of
electricity, sent to the Professor those of their
patients requiring electrical treatment; in fact,
Professor Richard is reputed to have made a for-
tune out of the London medical profession. He
is a man looked up to and respected, and worked
harmoniously with the scientific and leading phy-
sicians of the days. —Yours, &c.,
F. Buxton, C.E.
Nelson (New Zealand), February 27, 1894.
PROFESSOR RICHARD, M.B.
Professor Richard appeared at the Masonic
Hall again last evening, and his performance was
again most successful. The building was uncom-
fortably crowded, but the interest was so very
keen that the spectators bore all discomforts un-
complainingly, and displayed unparalleled en-
thusiasm. As the various patients were called to
the platform the audience appeared to look upon
it as a matter of certainty that immediate relief
would result, and their anticipations were borne
out in a most gratifying manner. During the
course of his opening remarks, Professor Richard
explained his system of applying the electric cur-
rent, and clearly demonstrated the great power of
the force at his command. He took the manage-
ment of the whole proceedings in hand, and
checked in the most exemplary manner a tendency
of certain persons in the rear of the building to
become noisy. He stated that the ladies and
gentlemen in the building were present as his
guests, and that he would not have the
harmony of the evening disturbed. If any
persons were restless, or desirous of creating
ferment they would be respectfully requested to
leave. If that request were not sufficient, the
services of the police would be brought into requi-
sition and ejectments would follow. The pro-
fessor also announced that he to appear
in the Masonic Hall again on the evening of the
19th inst., and would give an entertainment in aid
of the charitable institutions of the city. In the
early part he would give a lecture and treat a
number of patients free of charge, and the remain-
der of the evening would be devoted to an elec-
trical performance that could not fail to arouse
the mirth of every person in the building. The
charges of admission would be 35., 25., and Is.,
and as the gross proceeds would be devoted to a
charitable object, he trusted that the building
would be filled. He would remain in the city
four weeks longer, and would treat patients at his
consulting-rooms, Union Bank Chambers, Bolton-
street. If any poor persons came for relief he
would use his discretion about reducing his fee.
He then called for the first case.
The first case was that of a young man totally
blind. When he was seated on the platform the
Professor said many people .expected him to prac-
tically take people from their graves. This, of
course, he could nob do. He was there to do all
he could for the benefit of his suffering fellows,
but he was not endowed with supernatural powers.
In the case before him he would do his best, but
at that stage he could nob guarantee a cure. If
he succeeded he should be very pleased. The
patient’s mother was very poor, and if the son
could be sufficiently relieved to be able to earn his
living it would be a glorious thing. The patient
was recommended by the Mayor of Newcastle,
who said that the young man had been afflicted,
since he was four years of age. After making an
examination the electrician stated that the eyes
were completely dead, and all he could do was to
bring the whole of his skill to bear, and hope to
win his object. The treatment lasted a couple of
minutes, and the patient was then enabled to
distinguish a light held before him. The fact was
received with storms of applause. The Professor
stated that the case was curable. He would treat
the patient, and he hoped so successfully that he
would be able to see, perhaps not enough to read
or write, bub sufficiently to passthrough life with-
out assistance.
The next patient was stone deaf. He was a
young man, and had been recommended by the
Mayor of Greta. The cure in this case must be
regarded as phenomenal, as the patient, within
less time than it takes to record the fact, received
his hearing. The knowledge appeared to
thoroughly bewilder the poor fellow, and he stared
round in genuine amazement, and seemed to be
astounded at hearing the applause of the audience,
and the notes of the piano in the hall.
The next was also a deaf patient, and was
recommended by Alderman Thwaites (Mayor of
Adamstown), who stated that he had a large
family, and was so poor that he was compelled to
seek advice gratis. After the treatment the man
stated that he had come from Adamstown, and
had been deaf for 14 years. It is needless to say
that the success of the treatment created a furore.
The professor instructed the patient to attend his
consulting rooms on Sunday next, and he would
complete the cure.
An elderly man next walked on to the stage,
and the Professor tried to inform him that he must
wait till Sunday to be treated. The words were
uttered in stentorian tones, but the stranger still
held his position on the platform and seemed in-
capable of comprehending. As a way out of the
difficulty, and probably thinking it would be
quicker to relieve the patient than explain, the
Professor sab him in the chair and treated him.
Two minutes sufficed to bring about the desired
effect. The patient, who had been recommended
by the Rev. C. Whyte, of Newcastle, and the
Rev. J. Jones, of Lambton, was informed that if
he proceeded to the consulting rooms on Sunday
next the defect would be entirely removed.
The following case was an elderly rheumatic
patient, who had been crippled for three years.
He was recommended by the Mayor of Newcastle,
who stated that all efforts to relieve him had
failed. The man stated that he had been afflicted
for 10 years. The fingers of both hands were
badly contracted, and his legs were almost useless.
The treatment was wonderfully efficacious, the
healing hands scarcely having been passed over
the nerves of the hands and legs when the power
was restored. The subject manifested his delight
by skipping along the platform and up and down
the steps two or three times. He then seized his
bat, and walked out of the hall with youthful
agility.
The next and last case was also a rheumatic
patient, who had been recommended by Alderman
Kidd. He stated that he had been an acute suf-
ferer for eight years, and it was apparent, from
the assistance that was required to get him to the
stage, that his legs were in a terribly bad condi-
tion. The application was successful, and the
patient was given the use of his legs again.
The remainder of the evening was devoted to a
most amusing electrical entertainment, which
evoked shrieks of laughter and genuine admira-
tion.
—Newcastle Herald, April 12,1894.

The Knave of Diamonds.
( FOR THE BULLETIN .)
“ Documents of all kinds altered so as to
defy detection. Letters skilfully opened and re-
closed. Handwriting imitated to perfection ;
signatures a speciality. Moderate terms and pro-
found secresy! ”
To some such tune might have run Yee Wung’s
advertisements, had not that great artist been
debarred by an unfortunate prejudice of society
from letting his light shine before men. Upon
Yee Wung in the flesh no user of his skill had
ever yet set eyes, which was why—though most of
his clients had come, sooner or later, into disas-
trous collision with the prejudice aforesaid —the
accomplished Yee pursued his peaceful way, as
untroubled of the law as of his conscience.
Scarce ten feet square was Yee’s skylighted
den, be-shelved and pigeon holed from floor to
ceiling, and stuffy with tobacco, tea and opium.
The opium-lamp, in fact, stood ever on the table,
nor bad 30 years of smoking dulled one whit the
subtle intellect of this candle-coloured little man
with European-cut grizzling hair and a long
peninsula of thin grey beard.
A scuffle in the ante-room—an unwonted sound
of voices—and lo ! Yee Wung’s professional in-
cognito was broken at last! A woman walked in
upon him—tall, closely - veiled, and palpably
excited, but none the less determined. Behind
her grinned and jabbered Yee’s faithful sentinel,
taken thus for the first time off his guard. But
the ohl man’s wits were ready. In an instant he
had slipped round the table—thrust the intruder
in—the janitor out—locked the door inside and
put his back against it. The woman eyed him
through her veil, and quailed a little. The little,
bird-like man—long-clawed and bright-eyed—was
dangerous!
“No harm, John!” she said. “Only me
wantee you writee me something ! That all 1”
Yee Wung cackled. “Sit down, madam ! If
you wish to speak English—speak it! or Dutch,
or Spanish, or Malay !—I am at your service in
whichever you please ! Do I understand you to
say you want something written for you ?”
The astonished woman sat down. “Yes!” she
said. “ I knew that old idiot outside couldn’t be
the man I wanted, so I pushed my way in.”
Yee Wung came to the table. “Something in
Malay you want, no doubt; or is it in Chinese?”
The woman recovered her arrogance. “ Non-
sense !” she said. “It’s English I want written,
in a particular handwriting ! —in this !” and she
held out a letter.
The Chinaman took it “The signature?” he
a ked. “Do you want that imitated, too ?”
“Of course !”
“Forgery, madam!— forgery!”
“I know it! But I was told that you—if you
were well paid, of course ”
“Would risk 10 or 15 years of the Andamans ?
You have been grossly imposed upon, madam !
Let me wish you good-day—and better advisers !”
And Yee Wung unlocked the door.
Mrs. Harrison—the letter had told the China-
man her name—stared at him for a moment,
sprang up, and went without another word. Yee
Wung cackled softly again, took from within the
forgotten letter the pencilled draft of another,
absorbed its tenor at a glance, replaced it, dropped
both on the floor, and told his seneschal to admit
the lady when she came back, which would be
soon. In three minutes back she came, saw the
letter on the floor, snatched it up, and was gone
again ; whereupon Yee Wang took unto himself a
sturdy cane, called in the defaulting Ah Meng,
and, having batoned that unlucky servitor with
much spirit and satisfaction, kicked him out again,
re-looked the door, and mounted upon a step-
ladder to search an upper shelf.
Systematic to the ends of his claws, Yee Wung
kept stored upon his shelves not only cheque-
books of every bank in India, China, and the
Peninsula, and special stationery of every leading
firm in Singapore and Malacca, but also an indexed
multitude of letters, procured per medium of the
expertest sneak-thieves in the world—his Singa-
pore compatriots. Ere long Yee found what he
wanted—some headed paper of Harrison Bros,
and an autograph letter of the senior partner in
that great American concern. With these at hand,
Yee Wung sat down to commit to paper the words
of the pencilled draft he had glanced at.
Gentlemen,—Please deliver to the bearer—my wife—
the diamond bracelet completed last week to my order.—
Wilberforce F. Harrison. Messrs. Van Houten &
Groot, Jewellers.
Yee Wung lit his pipe and ruminated. Gay
dog, Wilberforce Harrison ! all Singapore knew
that! Bracelet not for his wife! Wife wanted
to get hold of it, and thus have a pull upon the
faithless one. “Now, wherein could all this be
manipulated unto the personal use and benefit of
Yee Wung? ” asked that ingenious Pagan of him-
self. Two minutes brought the answer, and then,
with several pens and various inks, the expert
addressed himself to the production of two docu-
ments which were to have important consequences.
In half-an hour the job was finished, and, solacing
himself once more with the “ drowsy syrup of the
East,” Yee Wung descended to his subjacent
cubicnlum, and there upon his ancient person
worked such wondrous changes that his mother—
had that excellent heathen lady been alive—would
have repudiated him as of an alien race. From
dirty quinceasolour he changed his face to brightest
lemon, shaved his beard, blacked his thin
moustache, and conferred upon himself an arching
pair of jetty eyebrows. He clipped close his
portentous finger nails, arrayed himself in snowy
drill and spotless topee, and, thus equipped, went
forth upon the warpath.
* * •
“ Messrs. Van Houten and Groot. —I had intended to
call to-day for the bracelet, but, as I have some unex-
pected business to attend to, I hereby authorise you to
deliver over the article to the bearer, Mr. Lathi Tal, my.
confidential clerk, who will countersign enclosed receipt.
—Wilberforce F. Harrison.
Letter and receipt were on the firm’s business-
paper ; the signatures were beyond dispute; and
thus, when to the acknowledgment the irreproach-
able-looking Bengali gentleman bad appended bis
sign-manual, the jewellers handed him the bracelet
without a qualm—the more readily that it was
already paid for. But, ten minutes after the
smiling Lathi Tal had vanished, like a thunder-
bolt upon the beads of Van Houten and Groot
descended the purport of a document which that
confidential person must have accidentally
dropped in their shop.
Received from Mrs. W. F. Harrison, upon handing over
to her a diamond bracelet obtained by me from Van
Houten and Groot, the sum of Rs. 1000 —Lathi Tal.
“ Der skoontrel!” spluttered old Yon Houten,
smiting hard upon his bald Dutch pate. “He
moost haf hat der rezeed all retty to gif dat
voman, und he never know he tropt it here!”
But Lathi Tal—which is to say, Yee Wung—
knew very well where he had dropped it, and had
calculated the result of the finding thereof.
Which was, that after Lathi Tal had proved un-
discoverable—which, considering he didn’t exist,
was not surprising—the disgusted Harrison, dis-
believing utterly his wife’s protestations, made no
further fuss about the bracelet, although, to soothe
his wounded purse and feelings, he sued the
jewellers for the price of the undelivered article.
“Delivered to his wife through her agent,” con-
tended those level-headed Dutchmen, and thus
arose the famous “Bracelet Case,” which, ridng
by degrees in courts and costs,’fizzled out at last
in the Privy Council decision that there could be
no decision, and that each side should pay its by-
that-time-prodigious whack of the expenses.
The bracelet long ere then was a bracelet no
longer. By way of Batavia it had found its way
to Amsterdam, from which emporium of diamonds
“its stones were scattered far and wide,” to the
ultimate satisfactory fattening of Yee Wung’s
balance with the Bank of Cathay.
A LEX. MONTGOMERY.
Richard Coles, undergoing a sentence for
burglary at Pentridge prison, stabbed himself
about the head in ten places with a pair of scissors
for fun. Sentenced to an additional term of
solitary. That man wanted the cat.
Springvale, Vic., agitates to have Melb.
general cemetery removed into its midst, and has
appointed a committee to bring the advantages o
Springvale, as a burying locality, before the autho
rities. Springvale is evidently a good place to be
found dead in.
Premier Patterson has got a whiff of real
glory. Having feasted considerably with the
Mayor of Williamstown, the Vic. dictator sug-
gested a State visit to the Austrian and Russian
war-ships in the Bay, where further refreshments
were served. A proper sense of Patterson’s
importance was instilled into the commanders of
both vessels, and when he put off for shore
“salutes were fired in his honour.” For some
few seconds Williamstown thought the capture of
Australia was at hand. Then came the reassuring
news that Patterson was being saluted, and joy
supervened.—
AT WILLIAMSTOWN. April 5th, 1894.
Cried the father to his son,
Also wife and daughter
“ Did ye hear the booming gun
Out upon the water?
Did ye hear the cannon roar,
Making windows shake ashore?
Such a din in time of war
Tokens human slaughter.
“Conies the foe to lay us low 1
Is the land invaded
That the windows rattle so—
Must we fight unaided ?
Daughter, son, and loving wife,
Partner in domestic life—
Run and get my pocket-knife,
New and douhle-hladed!”
“Stay your hand !” the neighbour cried,
Rushing in excited
“ Put the fear of deatli aside,
Look not thus affrighted.
None have come to slay and shoot,
Safe are we from foreign loot:
Patterson those guns salute !
When will he be knighted?”
All the town is in the street,
Never town was prouder ;
Hither comes the Man of Meat I
Boom his praises louder.
Patterson, from Castlemaine!
Bid the cannons roar again ;
Let his speechifying reign,
Smell of smoke and powder.
Chief Justice Madden re a Melb. Lunatic
Asylum difficulty : “No regulations will prevent
young men from kissing young women.” Try an
onion diet.
At a dinner lately given in Loudon some
turtle-soup was served which had been sent to
Robert Herbert over 20yearsago by Captain Towns,
at whose meat-preserving works on Brisbane
River it had been made. It was quite equal to
what is tasted at city banquets.
When the Matabele campaign was over
the whole list of wounded prisoners only
amounted to eight or ten. In the ordinary course
of events there should have been many hundreds,
but the flying column of the Chartered Co.’s
brigands couldn’t be burdened with captives, so
they were shot out of hand. The high-toned and
culchawed Briton wouldn’t do the butchery in
person, but the prisoners were handed over to the
Native Contingent, with a general understanding
as to the course to be taken, and when the crippled
heathens were killed and planted no one ever
asked the Native Contingent what had become of
them.
Curious that not one of the Australian
chroniclers who have commented on the minis-
terial retirement of Gladstone has remembered
against that statesman his anti - Australian
attitude in ’45-6. During that period, Gladstone,
as secretary for the Colonies, mooted the renewal
of the transportation of convicts to Australia.
He went so far as to draft a constitution
proclaiming the district of Port Curtis (now
named “Gladstone,”) and all north of it, anew
Crown colony to which British convicts were to
be deported. Political accident alone prevented
Central and North Queensland from being,
for a time at least, subject to all the
horrors of laggery. Gladstone’s Port Curtis
proposal may, however, be debited with having
borne some fruit, in conjunction with an insane
resolution of N.S.W. Legislative Council in ’4B.
That body actually agreed to a motion for the
renewal of convictism, with the hollow modifica-
tion that each batch of convicts despatched should
be counterbalanced by a number of free immi-
grants sent out at British expense. Earl Grey,
the then Secretary for the Colonies, took advan-
tage of that opportunity to revoke the Order
in Council that liberated N.S.W. from its British
chain-gangs, and renewed the old, unrestricted
system of transportation! And this was not
formally terminated till ’53.

The Wyalong gold discoveries, following on those at Coolgardie, the Mur-
chison, and elsewhere, have given Australia a lift which was never more badly
wanted. Some of these finds may prove only a repetition of the
delusion of Kimberley, but some, at all events, are genuine, and will do much
good to this distressful country.

The Last of Six
Tales of the Austral Tropics.
By ERNEST FAVENG.
With Prefatory Note by Rolf Boldretvood.

Opinions of the Press :
“ The volume takes its name from one of the sketches, ‘ The Last of Six,’ though in point of merit they all stand pretty well on a par The-
author, who is familiar with life in Northern Queensland, writes with vigour about the doings of the whites and the natives in these farawav
parts. A strong vein of mysticism runs through his narratives, and there are ghosts in almost every chapter, and for those who aw fnna Z
realistic work the volume will prove attractive reading. ”-S.M. Herald, July 8, 1893. 6 £ond of
“ No writer has given the world a more vivid description of Australia and its wilderness than Ernest Favenc, and the secret of thp is tlni
he has, by many years’ experience of travel and bush life, acquired a knowledge of this continent which is possessed’by scarcely any other writer
He, too, possesses great descriptive powers. Whatever he puts pen to is certain to be good. In the stories under notice he has given ns «nme r.f
his best work. That they will be widely read is certain,”—Sydney Mail, July 8, 1893. g nus some o£
“ They present with force and vividness, as well as with the accuracy arising from long contact and keen observation striking pictures of
parts of Australia unfamiliar to most of its inhabitants ; they tell, sometimes with remarkable power, of incidents such as happen in wild nlaces
when human hearts are stirred by strong passions, and of weird imaginings that move the minds of lonely men. ‘ The Last of Six ’is a terrible
tale of cannibalism, treachery, and revenge upon the dead, which in other hands might be gross and hideous, but which Mr Favenc makes of
thrilling interest. ‘ The Haunt of the Jinkarras’ suggests Rider Haggard and his imitators to some extent, but is itself relieved from the
suspicion of imitation by faithful local colour and a grotesquerie of conception, in which Mr. Favenc here and there approaches Poe This
characteristic is especially noticeable in ‘ Spirit Led,’ which the great American might have written. ‘ A Cup of Cold Water ’is perhaps the
best tale in a collection that should be widely read, and which forms a worthy addition to'purely Australian literature.”—Daily Telegraph
July 15,1893. i '
“ ‘The Last of Six ;’ and other stories. By Ernest Favenc. (Sydney : The Bulletin Newspaper Co.) This is a collection of short but
graphic sketches by Mr. Favenc, the well-known explorer, who has a rare collection of Australian legendary lore. All the stories here are onod
and we can heartily endorse the recommendatory notice by Rolf Boldrewood prefixed to this collection. The present issue forms the third otTua
Bulletin scries of Australian reminiscences.”—Australian Star, July 15, 1893.
